Defining Intuition: Beyond Guesswork
Author defines intuition and lists its three key characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three characteristics of intuition (hads) as described in the Quranic epistemological framework and distinguish it from mere guesswork.

Core Idea
Intuition in Islam is direct, immediate, and individual — not wild guessing.

The author begins by clearing a common misconception: intuition is not mere guesswork. Then, citing researchers, he lists three defining traits that set intuition apart from other forms of knowledge. Understanding these traits is essential for correctly situating intuition within the Islamic epistemological hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish intuition from guesswork
Note that the author explicitly denies that intuition is mere guesswork. This sets the stage for a positive description.
2
Extract the three characteristics
Read the three enumerated points. For each, identify what aspect of knowledge it describes: directness, focus on essence, and individuality.
These characteristics are not exhaustive but are key differentiators.
3
Contrast Islamic intuition with other concepts
Consider how these characteristics differ from everyday guessing or even rational inference. Why might intuition be considered a valid secondary source?
Think about how this definition aligns with the Quranic emphasis on certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يرتبط الحدس بالتخمين المحض أي إن المفهوم السائد عن الحدس بمعنى التخمين ليس له أساس علمي. وأراد بعض الباحثين تلخيص تلك السمات فأوجزها في نقاط ثلاث هي: ١- معرفة مباشرة لا تحتاج إلى وسائط، ولا تدرّج فيها. ٢- الانتقال المباشر إلى لب الموضوع المعروف بدلا تقديمه أوصافاً خارجية أو سطحية للموضوع، أو يقتصر على معرفته من خلال مقارنته بغيره. ٣- معرفة فردية لا تنتقل إلى الآخرين ولا يمكن تلقينها أو تعميمها. موقف القرآن من الحدس: قبل المبادرة إلى تسجيل ما يبدو أنه موقف للقرآن من التفكير الحدسي بوصفه مصدراً في الحصول على المعرفة أخال أنه من غير الموضوعية إغفال مدى التأثير الكبير لـ (هنري برجسون) الذي يعدّ مؤسساً لهذا الاتجاه في العصر الحاضر. مما جرَّ كثيراً من الباحثين المعاصرين لتحديد موقفهم من الحدس في ضوء رؤية برجسون. والحق أنه أياً ما كان من اهتمام متميّز لهنري برجسون بالحدس حتى أضحى صاحب نظرية قمينة بالدراسة؛ فإن ذلك لا يجعل من دراسته معياراً عاماً مطّرداً، إذ إن أكبر عامل نقد موضوعي يوجه إلى نظريته تلك – من زاوية التصور الإسلامي - قيامها بعيداً عن توجيهات الوحي واستهداءاته. ولعل هذا هو الذي دفع به إلى المغالاة في أهميتها إلى الحد الذي قلّل فيه من أهمية العقل، بل وجعله غير كاف وحده في الحصول على المعرفة ولا بد من أن يؤيد بالحدس الذي اعتقد أنه أعمق من المعرفة العقلية بكثير. إن جعل نظرية برجسون معياراً في القبول أو الرفض هو الذي حمل الباحث المشار إليه على أن يتلمس لبرجسون ومن يشايع رأيه العذر في موقفهم الحدسي المغالي وزهدهم في العقل إلى حدّ يشتم منه رائحة الازدراء. فمشكلة برجسون من هذه الوجهة - الخلط بين مقتضيات الحياة الشخصية والتجارب الفنية والشعرية من جانب ومقتضيات المعرفة العلمية من جانب آخر. ولذلك فلا تستطيع تكذيب برجسون، بيد أن ذلك محصور في مجال بعينه لا يتعداه. أما موقف القرآن الكريم من كل النظريات والفلسفات القديم منها والحديث فهو موقف الهيمنة والتصديق، أي إقرار الصحيح منها ونبذ السقيم وهذه قاعدة عامة مطّردة موجّهة لقاعدة التفكير في الإسلام، كما وأنها منهجية القرآن المعرفية في التعامل مع سائر المعارف الأخرى. وذلك يرجع إلى سبب جوهري هو مصدريته الربانية القطعية. من هنا فإن نظرية برجسون في الحدس مأخوذة بعين الاعتبار، بيد أنها يجب أن تخضع لاختبار الوحي قبولاً أو رفضاً، وسواء كان هذا القبول أو الرفض جزئياً أم كليّاً، وفقاً لقاعدة التفكير في الإسلام واتساقاً مع منهجية القرآن المعرفية. والقرآن يدرج الحدس ضمن مصادر المعرفة الثانوية المعترف بها.
Intuition is not connected to pure guesswork, meaning the prevailing concept of intuition as guesswork has no scientific basis. Some researchers summarized its characteristics in three points: 1- Direct knowledge that does not need mediators, and has no gradation. 2- Direct transition to the core of the known subject instead of presenting external or superficial descriptions of the subject, or limiting knowledge of it to comparison with others. 3- Individual knowledge that does not transfer to others and cannot be taught or generalized. The Quran's position on intuition: Before initiating to record what appears to be the Quran's position on intuitive thinking as a source of obtaining knowledge, I think it is not objective to ignore the great influence of Henri Bergson, who is considered the founder of this trend in the present age. This has caused many contemporary researchers to define their position on intuition in light of Bergson's vision. The truth is that whatever the distinguished interest of Henri Bergson in intuition until he became the owner of a theory worthy of study; that does not make his study a general, consistent criterion, since the greatest factor of objective criticism directed at his theory – from the perspective of the Islamic conception – is its being based far from the directives and guidance of revelation. Perhaps this is what drove him to exaggerate its importance to the extent that he diminished the importance of reason, even making it insufficient alone in obtaining knowledge and that it must be supported by intuition, which he believed to be much deeper than rational knowledge. Making Bergson's theory a criterion for acceptance or rejection is what led the aforementioned researcher to seek an excuse for Bergson and those who share his opinion in their extreme intuitive stance and their asceticism towards reason to the point of smelling contempt. Bergson's problem from this perspective is the confusion between the requirements of personal life and artistic/poetic experiences on one side and the requirements of scientific knowledge on the other. Therefore, we cannot deny Bergson, but that is limited to a specific field that does not go beyond it. As for the position of the Noble Quran regarding all theories and philosophies, ancient and modern, it is a position of dominance and verification, i.e., affirming what is correct from them and rejecting the unsound. This is a general, consistent rule guiding the rule of thinking in Islam, and it is the Quran's epistemological methodology in dealing with all other forms of knowledge. This is due to an essential reason: its absolute divine source. Hence, Bergson's theory on intuition is taken into consideration, but it must be subjected to the test of revelation for acceptance or rejection, whether this acceptance or rejection is partial or total, according to the rule of thinking in Islam and in consistency with the Quran's epistemological methodology. And the Quran includes intuition among the recognized secondary sources of knowledge.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about intuition (hads) as a source of knowledge, specifically addressing the Quran's stance. The author begins by clarifying what intuition is not: it is not mere guesswork. Then, citing some researchers, the author lists three characteristics of intuition: it is direct, immediate knowledge of the core of a subject, and it is individual and not transferable. The author then argues that any evaluation of intuition from an Islamic perspective must consider Henri Bergson's influential theory, but warns against making Bergson's view the absolute criterion. The criticism of Bergson from an Islamic viewpoint is that his theory is divorced from revelation, leading him to overvalue intuition at the expense of reason. The author points out that Bergson's error is conflating personal/artistic experiences with scientific knowledge. Then, the author states the Quran's general principle: it dominates and verifies all theories, accepting what is correct and rejecting what is unsound. This is because the Quran is from a divine, certain source. Consequently, Bergson's theory is considered but must be tested against revelation. Finally, the author asserts that the Quran recognizes intuition as a secondary source of knowledge, subordinated to revelation. The argument follows a structure: definition of intuition, critique of Bergson, and establishment of Quranic epistemology.

Key Terms
الحدس (al-hads): Intuition as a direct, non-mediated form of knowledge that is individual and immediate, distinct from wild guesswork. Used as a secondary source of knowledge in the Quranic framework. [Note: In classical Islamic logic (Mantiq), 'hads' sometimes refers to a certain kind of inference or acumen; here it is used in a modern epistemological sense influenced by Bergson.]
المعرفة (al-ma'rifa): Knowledge, particularly in the context of sources: rational, intuitive, and revealed. The passage discusses intuition as one source of knowledge. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable across disciplines.]
الوحي (al-wahy): Divine revelation as the primary criterion for truth in Islam. It is the touchstone against which all knowledge sources, including intuition, are measured. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a characteristic of intuition?
A) Direct knowledge without mediators
B) Knowledge that can be taught and generalized
C) Immediate transition to the core of the subject
D) Individual and non-transferable

Key Takeaway
Intuition is a valid secondary knowledge source only when it is direct, immediate, and individual — not guesswork.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the three characteristics of intuition listed in the passage, provide a counterexample: a type of knowledge that is not intuitive. Explain why it fails the characteristic.

Critiquing Bergson: Intuition Without Revelation
Author critiques Bergson's theory of intuition as lacking revelatory guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's critique of Bergson's theory of intuition from an Islamic perspective.

Core Idea
Bergson's intuitionism fails because it is cut off from divine revelation.

The author acknowledges Bergson's immense influence on modern intuition theory but argues that his framework cannot be adopted wholesale. The Islamic critique centers on Bergson's neglect of revelation, which leads to an overvaluation of intuition and a denigration of reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify Bergson's central claim
According to the passage, Bergson believed intuition is deeper than rational knowledge and that reason alone is insufficient.
2
Pinpoint the Islamic critique
The author criticizes Bergson for building his theory 'far from the directives and guidance of revelation.' This is the core Islamic objection.
What does this imply about the source of knowledge?
3
Analyze the confusion Bergson is accused of
The author says Bergson confused personal/artistic experiences with scientific knowledge. Why is this a problem epistemologically?
Think about the difference between subjective experience and objective truth.

Quranic Epistemology: Testing All Theories by Revelation
Author articulates the Quran's overarching epistemological principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quran's epistemological methodology of 'dominance and verification' (haymana wa tasdiq) and apply it to evaluate a philosophical theory.

Core Idea
The Quran dominates and verifies all knowledge, accepting truth and rejecting falsehood.

In the final section, the author articulates the Quran's overarching epistemological principle: it exercises dominance and verification over all theories, ancient and modern. This principle is rooted in the Quran's divine origin and serves as the ultimate criterion for accepting or rejecting any knowledge claim, including Bergson's intuitionism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quran's epistemological stance
The author states the Quran's position is 'dominance and verification' (haymana wa tasdiq). This means it rules over all theories.
2
Understand the principle of testing by revelation
The passage says that even Bergson's theory must be subjected to the test of revelation for acceptance or rejection.
This is not blind rejection but a critical evaluation.
3
Apply the principle to the status of intuition
The Quran ultimately includes intuition as a secondary source, but only after being verified by revelation. This shows a balanced approach.
How does this contrast with Bergson's view?

Perhaps this is what drove him to exaggerate its importance to the extent that he diminished the importance of reason, even making it insufficient alone in obtaining knowledge and that it must be supported by intuition, which he believed to be much deeper than rational knowledge. Making Bergson's theory a criterion for acceptance or rejection is what led the aforementioned researcher to seek an excuse for Bergson and those who share his opinion in their extreme intuitive stance and their asceticism towards reason to the point of smelling contempt. Bergson's problem from this perspective is the confusion between the requirements of personal life and artistic/poetic experiences on one side and the requirements of scientific knowledge on the other. Therefore, we cannot deny Bergson, but that is limited to a specific field that does not go beyond it. As for the position of the Noble Quran regarding all theories and philosophies, ancient and modern, it is a position of dominance and verification, i.e., affirming what is correct from them and rejecting the unsound. This is a general, consistent rule guiding the rule of thinking in Islam, and it is the Quran's epistemological methodology in dealing with all other forms of knowledge. This is due to an essential reason: its absolute divine source. Hence, Bergson's theory on intuition is taken into consideration, but it must be subjected to the test of revelation for acceptance or rejection, whether this acceptance or rejection is partial or total, according to the rule of thinking in Islam and in consistency with the Quran's epistemological methodology. And the Quran includes intuition among the recognized secondary sources of knowledge.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about intuition (hads) as a source of knowledge, specifically addressing the Quran's stance. The author begins by clarifying what intuition is not: it is not mere guesswork. Then, citing some researchers, the author lists three characteristics of intuition: it is direct, immediate knowledge of the core of a subject, and it is individual and not transferable. The author then argues that any evaluation of intuition from an Islamic perspective must consider Henri Bergson's influential theory, but warns against making Bergson's view the absolute criterion. The criticism of Bergson from an Islamic viewpoint is that his theory is divorced from revelation, leading him to overvalue intuition at the expense of reason. The author points out that Bergson's error is conflating personal/artistic experiences with scientific knowledge. Then, the author states the Quran's general principle: it dominates and verifies all theories, accepting what is correct and rejecting what is unsound. This is because the Quran is from a divine, certain source. Consequently, Bergson's theory is considered but must be tested against revelation. Finally, the author asserts that the Quran recognizes intuition as a secondary source of knowledge, subordinated to revelation. The argument follows a structure: definition of intuition, critique of Bergson, and establishment of Quranic epistemology.

Key Terms
الحدس (al-hads): Intuition as a direct, non-mediated form of knowledge that is individual and immediate, distinct from wild guesswork. Used as a secondary source of knowledge in the Quranic framework. [Note: In classical Islamic logic (Mantiq), 'hads' sometimes refers to a certain kind of inference or acumen; here it is used in a modern epistemological sense influenced by Bergson.]
المعرفة (al-ma'rifa): Knowledge, particularly in the context of sources: rational, intuitive, and revealed. The passage discusses intuition as one source of knowledge. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable across disciplines.]
الوحي (al-wahy): Divine revelation as the primary criterion for truth in Islam. It is the touchstone against which all knowledge sources, including intuition, are measured. [Note: No significant shift; term is stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Absolute rejection of all non-Islamic theories
B) Complete acceptance as long as they are rational
C) Dominance and verification, accepting truth and rejecting falsehood
D) Indifference, as the Quran does not address human theories

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the ultimate criterion; all knowledge sources, including intuition, are secondary and must align with revelation.

Educational Implications of Intuition as a Source of Knowledge
Author lists four educational implications of intuition as a source of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the four educational implications of intuitive knowledge as derived from the text, and apply the distinction between genuine intuition and clever trickery to an educational setting.

Core Idea
Intuition is a divine gift that cannot be taught but must be recognized and refined.

The author of this passage explores intuition (al-hads) as a source of knowledge and its relevance to education. He identifies four key implications: intuitive knowledge is a priori, cannot be systematically taught, impacts teaching methodology, and affects the learner. He also warns teachers to distinguish between true intuition and students' clever tricks to hide ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four implications
Read the Arabic passage or its translation and identify the four numbered points. Write them down in order.
Each point is introduced by a number (١-٤).
2
Understand each implication
For each point, paraphrase what it means. For example: 'Intuitive knowledge is a priori' means it precedes experience. 'It is a divine gift' means it cannot be acquired through formal schooling.
Focus on the logical relationship between the claim and the reason given.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
The author mentions Prophet Jacob's statement 'I find the scent of Joseph.' Some commentators call it inspiration, but the author argues it is intuition. Reflect on why the author insists on this terminological precision.
Consider how the definition of intuition (direct, without external mediator) applies here.
4
Apply the distinction
The author says teachers must distinguish between 'brilliant inner intuition' and 'clever tricks' that hide ignorance. Think of a classroom scenario: when does a student's insight seem intuitive, and how might you test its genuineness?
The distinction hinges on whether the knowledge is truly independent of external sources and predictable reasoning.

As for the educational implications of the intuitive source, we conclude the following: 1 - Intuitive knowledge is considered a priori knowledge, because events are known before they occur in reality. 2 - Education cannot seek to acquire intuitive knowledge through systematic education because it is a divine gift. 3 - Intuition has its impact on teaching method with respect to the teacher, because intuition is classified as a form of self-learning, as it occurs without a mediator, it is direct from within. 4 - Intuition also has its impact on the learner, despite the difficulty in developing the ability to use it as a source for the learner due to the impossibility of predicting the event to a large extent; but its productive importance for producing knowledge necessitates working on refining the intuitive ability in the learner as an important source of creative ability. It is also important for the teacher to strive to be able to distinguish between the intuition that the learner already possesses, called (the brilliant inner intuition), and the clever trick that the student might use to hide his ignorance. Commentary
This passage discusses the educational implications of intuition (hads) as a subsidiary source of knowledge. The author lists four points. First, intuitive knowledge is a priori, meaning it knows events before they happen. Second, it cannot be acquired through formal education because it is a divine gift. Third, intuition affects teaching methods as it is a form of self-learning without external mediation. Fourth, it affects the learner; despite difficulty in developing it due to unpredictability, its importance for creativity necessitates honing it. The author adds that a teacher must distinguish between genuine intuition and clever tricks hiding ignorance. A footnote clarifies a Quranic example: Prophet Jacob's statement about Joseph's scent is considered intuition, not inspiration, by some commentators.

The Definition of Inspiration: Criteria and Distinctions
Author defines inspiration linguistically and philosophically, distinguishing it from satanic whispering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the criteria for authentic inspiration and distinguish it from satanic whispering using the definitions provided.

Core Idea
Inspiration is divine knowledge impelling to good, distinct from Satanic whispers by its source and content.

How do we know if a thought is truly from God or a whisper from Satan? Classical scholars defined inspiration (ilham) carefully, setting conditions to distinguish divine guidance from deception. This lesson unpacks those definitions from linguistic, philosophical, and psychological perspectives, highlighting the key criterion: inspiration always impels to good.

Guided Path
1
Extract the linguistic definition
Look at the first definition: 'what is cast into the soul' from God. Notice the passive construction – the recipient plays no active role.
2
Identify the philosophical nuance
The philosophical definition adds 'by way of emanation (fayd)'. Understand that emanation implies a direct, unearned bestowal from God.
Emanation is a key concept in Islamic philosophy, but here it simply means direct divine infusion.
3
Pinpoint the psychological definition
The psychological definition describes it as knowledge that reveals secrets and truths. Note that it is still 'without acquisition, thought, or seeking'.
4
Identify the distinguishing condition
The Philosophical Dictionary states inspiration must impel to good or leaving evil. This is the crucial differentia that excludes satanic whispering (waswasa).
Whispering is the opposite: it incites to evil.

تعريف الإلهام
الإلهام لغةً ما يلقى في الرُّوع يقال ألهمه الله، واستلهم الله العبد (٢). أو إلقاء شيء في القلب يطمئن إليه الصدر يخص الله به بعض أصفيائه (۳). وفي الاصطلاح الفلسفي ما يلقي في الروع بطريق الفيض(٤). ومن هذا المعنى صاغ بعض الباحثين في ميدان علم النفس تعريفا للإلهام يخلص إلى أنه نوع من العلم الذي يفيض الله - سبحانه وتعالى - به على الإنسان، ويلقيه في قلبه، فتكشف له بعض الأسرار، وتتضح له بعض الحقائق))(٥). وفي المعجم الفلسفي الإلهام مصدر ألهم، وهو أن يلقي الله في نفس الإنسان أمراً يبعثه على فعل الشيء، أو تركه، وذلك بلا اكتساب أو فكر ولا استفاضة، وهو وارد غيبي ويشترط فيه أن يكون باعنا على فعل الخير، أو ترك النشر، ولذلك فسره بعضهم بإلقاء الخير في قلب الغير، بلا استفاضة فكرية فيه، وهذا يخرج الوسوسة، لأن الإلقاء من الله، أما الوسوسة فمن الشيطان. وقيل الإلهام ما وقع في القلب من العلم، وهو يدفع إلى العمل من غير (١) محمد منير مرسي، مرجع سابق، ٥٨. Secondly - Inspiration: (Inspiration)
Definition of Inspiration
Inspiration linguistically: what is cast into the soul; one says 'God inspired him' and 'God inspired the servant'. Or: the casting of something into the heart that the chest feels at ease with, which God bestows upon some of His chosen ones. In philosophical terminology: what is cast into the soul by way of emanation. From this meaning, some researchers in the field of psychology formulated a definition of inspiration concluding that it is a type of knowledge that God - the Exalted - pours upon a person, and casts into his heart, so that some secrets are revealed to him and some truths become clear to him. In the Philosophical Dictionary, inspiration is the source of 'alhama', meaning that God casts into the soul of a person an order that impels him to do something or leave it, without acquisition, thought, or seeking, and it is a hidden influx. It is required that it be an impetus to do good or leave evil, and therefore some interpreted it as casting good into the heart of another without intellectual seeking, and this excludes whispering, because the casting is from God, while whispering is from Satan. It is said that inspiration is what occurs in the heart of knowledge, and it drives to action without...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage defines inspiration (ilham) as a subsidiary source of knowledge. The author presents definitions from linguistic, philosophical, and psychological perspectives. Linguistically, it is 'what is cast into the soul' from God. Philosophically, it is cast by way of emanation (fayd). The author then quotes a psychological definition: a type of knowledge that God pours into the heart, revealing secrets and truths. The Philosophical Dictionary adds that inspiration impels one to action or abstention without thought, and it specifically impels to good, thus excluding satanic whisperings (waswasa). This distinction is crucial: inspiration comes from God, whisperings from Satan.

The Defining Characteristics of Ilham and Hads
Author defines and contrasts ilham and hads.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features that differentiate ilham (divine inspiration) from hads (intuition) as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Ilham is immediate and faith-rooted; hads is predictive and universal.

Many students confuse ilham with hads, but the author of this passage draws a sharp line between them. By understanding the defining traits of each, you'll gain a tool for analyzing how knowledge claims are justified in Islamic thought. Let's extract those traits from the text.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions
In the Arabic passage, find where the author describes ilham and hads. Ilham is characterized by immediacy (الآنية) and certainty (اليقين), while hads is based on prediction (التنبؤ) and foresight (الاستشراف).
2
Identify the contrasting elements
Note that ilham is described as overwhelming the person with an idea automatically, whereas hads requires verification over time and is not immediate.
The author uses 'بخلاف' (contrary to) to mark the opposition.
3
Recognize the religious element
The author states that ilham gains a religious dimension (عنصر التدين) through devotional acts like dhikr and love of God. Hads is a common denominator among all people, religious or not.
This makes ilham specifically Islamic, while hads is universal.
4
Summarize the distinction
Create a two-column table with ilham and hads, listing their characteristics: immediacy, certainty, religiosity vs. prediction, delay, universality.
This table will help you see the contrast clearly.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعلّ بلوغ هذا الموضع من البحث يفرض سؤالاً منطقيا أملاه ما أشرنا إليه من تداخل لدى بعض الباحثين بين مصطلحي الإلهام والحدس فهل حقا ثمة تداخل بينهما، وإلى أي مدى يلتقي كل منهما بالآخر؟ والحق أنه لا مناص أمامنا من التذكير بالفارق الجوهري بين كل من الحدس والإلهام، حيث سبق الإلماح إلى أن السمة الجوهرية للحدس هي قيامه على أساس من التنبؤ والاستشراف والترقب من غير إجهاد للحس أو العقل في القيام بعمليات إدراكية حسية أو عقلية مقصودة والتأكد من صحته ليس لحظياً آنياً، بمجرد انقداحه في ذهن المرء، وذلك بعكس الإلهام، إذ إن انتسام هذا الأخير بالآنية التي تغمر المرء بالفكرة، فيجد نفسه متحدثًا بها على سبيل اليقين، أو مطبقاً لها، متأكداً من سلامتها تلقائيا يضفي على الإلهام عنصر التدين، ويمكن أن يذكى بموجبات تعبدية محضة كالذكر والحب الصادق لله - تعالى. وعلى هذا فإن عنصر التدين هذا هو الذي يجعل من الإلهام سمة إسلامية إيمانية، في حين أن الحدس قاسم مشترك بين المتدينين وسواهم.
وجدير بالذكر في هذا المقام أن هذا التمييز بينهما على هذا النحو من الوضوح مما لم أجد أحداً سبق إليه، ولست أدعي مطلق صوابيته، ولكنه قد يفسر لنا بعض ذلك التداخل الذي وصل أحياناً حد الترادف عند بعض الباحثين قديماً وحديثاً. ولذلك فلن يكون مستغرباً أن نجد عالماً بقامة أبي حامد الغزالي لا يلتفت إلى التفريق بين الحدس والإلهام فتراه يتحدث عن حصول الحدس تارة عقيب طلب واشتياق بأن يكون نفساً شريفاً قوية مستضيئة في نفسها، فيحصل له العلوم ابتداءً كأنه ما تخلى إلى اختياره، يكاد زيتها يضئ ضوء الفطرة، ولو لم تمسسه نار الفكرة، ولا يفارق طريق الإلهام والحدس طريق الاكتساب والفكر في نفس العلم ولا في محله، ولا في سببه، محل العلم النفس.
ونجد أن مجمع اللغة العربية يميل إلى عدم التفريق بينهما، فبعد أن يعرف الحدس، ويوضح نوعية العقلي والحسي نراه يستنتج أن طريقة إدراك الحقائق
Perhaps reaching this point in the research imposes a logical question dictated by what we indicated of overlap among some researchers between the terms ilham and hads: is there truly overlap between them, and to what extent does each meet the other? The truth is that we have no escape from reminding of the essential distinction between each of hads and ilham, where we previously hinted that the essential characteristic of hads is that it is based on prediction, foresight, and anticipation without straining the senses or the mind in performing deliberate sensory or mental perceptual operations, and its correctness is not immediate and instantaneous merely upon its occurrence in one's mind. This is contrary to ilham, for ilham is characterized by immediacy that overwhelms a person with an idea, and he finds himself speaking about it with certainty, or applying it, assured of its soundness automatically. This gives ilham a religious element, and it can be kindled by purely devotional acts such as dhikr and sincere love of God Almighty. Based on this, this religious element is what makes ilham an Islamic faith characteristic, while hads is a common denominator between the religious and others.
It is worth mentioning in this context that this distinction between them with this degree of clarity is something I have not found anyone before me to have attained, and I do not claim absolute correctness, but it may explain for us some of that overlap which sometimes reached synonymy among some researchers past and present. Therefore, it will not be surprising to find a scholar of the stature of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali not paying attention to the difference between hads and ilham; you see him speaking about the occurrence of hads sometimes following a request and yearning as being a noble soul strong and illuminated in itself, so sciences occur to it initially as if it has not abandoned its choice, its oil almost shines with the light of innate disposition even if a fire of thought does not touch it, and the path of ilham and hads does not deviate from the path of acquisition and thought in the science itself, nor in its location, nor in its cause, the location of science being the soul.
We find that the Arabic Language Academy tends not to differentiate between them, for after it defines hads and clarifies its intellectual and sensory type, we see it concludes that the method of perceiving realities...
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis distinguishing two cognitive sources: ilham (inspiration) and hads (intuition). The author begins by posing a logical question about the perceived overlap between these two terms among researchers. He then asserts the necessity of recalling the fundamental difference. For hads, the key characteristic is that it operates on prediction, foresight, and anticipation without deliberate cognitive effort, and its validation is not immediate; it takes time. In contrast, ilham is characterized by immediacy: the idea overwhelms the person instantly with certainty, and the person automatically feels assured of its correctness. This immediacy gives ilham a religious coloring, as it can be stimulated by devotional acts like dhikr and love of God. Thus, ilham becomes a mark of Islamic faith, while hads is universal, shared by religious and non-religious alike. The author then claims originality for this clear distinction, noting that he has not seen it before, but acknowledges it may not be absolutely correct. He uses it to explain why some classical and modern scholars treated the terms as synonyms. He cites al-Ghazali as an example of conflation, quoting Ghazali's view that hads can occur after effort and is like an innate illumination, and that the path of ilham and hads does not differ from intellectual acquisition. Finally, the author notes that the Arabic Language Academy also fails to distinguish, as it defines hads and then concludes that the method of perceiving realities (apparently lumps them together). Throughout, the author follows a question-answer structure: raising the problem, providing a clear distinction, supporting it with evidence, and acknowledging exceptions.

الإلهام
الحدس
التدين

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the essential difference between ilham and hads?
A) Ilham is learned while hads is innate.
B) Ilham is immediate and religiously colored, while hads is based on foresight and not immediate.
C) Ilham is only for prophets, while hads is for everyone.
D) Ilham requires intellectual effort, while hads comes effortlessly.

Key Takeaway
Ilham is distinguished from hads by its immediacy, automatic certainty, and religious character rooted in devotional acts.

The Author's Critique of Conflating Ilham and Hads
Author critiques conflation of ilham and hads by others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the author's claim of originality and evaluate his critique of scholars like al-Ghazali who treat ilham and hads as similar.

Core Idea
The author claims priority in clearly distinguishing ilham from hads, challenging past and present scholars.

Having laid out the distinction, the author now confronts the confusion found in earlier thinkers. He claims his clear separation is novel and uses al-Ghazali and the Arabic Language Academy as examples of conflation. Let's examine his argument and see how he supports his originality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim of originality
In the second paragraph, find the phrase 'لم أجد أحداً سبق إليه' (I have not found anyone before me). This indicates the author believes his distinction is unprecedented.
2
Find the targets of his critique
The author mentions two examples: al-Ghazali and the Arabic Language Academy. For al-Ghazali, he quotes a passage where Ghazali speaks about hads and ilham together without separating them. For the Academy, he notes that after defining hads, they conclude about 'perceiving realities' without distinguishing.
The quote from al-Ghazali is given in the text; identify the key sentence that shows conflating.
3
Analyze the author's use of evidence
The author uses these examples to show that even great scholars and institutions fell into overlap. He then offers his own distinction as an explanation for why such confusion occurred.
He says 'قد يفسر لنا بعض ذلك التداخل' (it may explain for us some of that overlap).
4
Assess the author's humility and caution
Note the author's caution: 'ولست أدعي مطلق صوابيته' (I do not claim absolute correctness). This shows scholarly humility and invites further scrutiny.
5
Evaluate the strength of the critique
Consider whether the author's examples truly support his claim. Does al-Ghazali's quote really conflate the two? The passage from Ghazali might be interpreted differently. Weigh the evidence.

ولعلّ بلوغ هذا الموضع من البحث يفرض سؤالاً منطقيا أملاه ما أشرنا إليه من تداخل لدى بعض الباحثين بين مصطلحي الإلهام والحدس فهل حقا ثمة تداخل بينهما، وإلى أي مدى يلتقي كل منهما بالآخر؟ والحق أنه لا مناص أمامنا من التذكير بالفارق الجوهري بين كل من الحدس والإلهام، حيث سبق الإلماح إلى أن السمة الجوهرية للحدس هي قيامه على أساس من التنبؤ والاستشراف والترقب من غير إجهاد للحس أو العقل في القيام بعمليات إدراكية حسية أو عقلية مقصودة والتأكد من صحته ليس لحظياً آنياً، بمجرد انقداحه في ذهن المرء، وذلك بعكس الإلهام، إذ إن انتسام هذا الأخير بالآنية التي تغمر المرء بالفكرة، فيجد نفسه متحدثًا بها على سبيل اليقين، أو مطبقاً لها، متأكداً من سلامتها تلقائيا يضفي على الإلهام عنصر التدين، ويمكن أن يذكى بموجبات تعبدية محضة كالذكر والحب الصادق لله - تعالى. وعلى هذا فإن عنصر التدين هذا هو الذي يجعل من الإلهام سمة إسلامية إيمانية، في حين أن الحدس قاسم مشترك بين المتدينين وسواهم.
وجدير بالذكر في هذا المقام أن هذا التمييز بينهما على هذا النحو من الوضوح مما لم أجد أحداً سبق إليه، ولست أدعي مطلق صوابيته، ولكنه قد يفسر لنا بعض ذلك التداخل الذي وصل أحياناً حد الترادف عند بعض الباحثين قديماً وحديثاً. ولذلك فلن يكون مستغرباً أن نجد عالماً بقامة أبي حامد الغزالي لا يلتفت إلى التفريق بين الحدس والإلهام فتراه يتحدث عن حصول الحدس تارة عقيب طلب واشتياق بأن يكون نفساً شريفاً قوية مستضيئة في نفسها، فيحصل له العلوم ابتداءً كأنه ما تخلى إلى اختياره، يكاد زيتها يضئ ضوء الفطرة، ولو لم تمسسه نار الفكرة، ولا يفارق طريق الإلهام والحدس طريق الاكتساب والفكر في نفس العلم ولا في محله، ولا في سببه، محل العلم النفس.
ونجد أن مجمع اللغة العربية يميل إلى عدم التفريق بينهما، فبعد أن يعرف الحدس، ويوضح نوعية العقلي والحسي نراه يستنتج أن طريقة إدراك الحقائق
Perhaps reaching this point in the research imposes a logical question dictated by what we indicated of overlap among some researchers between the terms ilham and hads: is there truly overlap between them, and to what extent does each meet the other? The truth is that we have no escape from reminding of the essential distinction between each of hads and ilham, where we previously hinted that the essential characteristic of hads is that it is based on prediction, foresight, and anticipation without straining the senses or the mind in performing deliberate sensory or mental perceptual operations, and its correctness is not immediate and instantaneous merely upon its occurrence in one's mind. This is contrary to ilham, for ilham is characterized by immediacy that overwhelms a person with an idea, and he finds himself speaking about it with certainty, or applying it, assured of its soundness automatically. This gives ilham a religious element, and it can be kindled by purely devotional acts such as dhikr and sincere love of God Almighty. Based on this, this religious element is what makes ilham an Islamic faith characteristic, while hads is a common denominator between the religious and others.
It is worth mentioning in this context that this distinction between them with this degree of clarity is something I have not found anyone before me to have attained, and I do not claim absolute correctness, but it may explain for us some of that overlap which sometimes reached synonymy among some researchers past and present. Therefore, it will not be surprising to find a scholar of the stature of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali not paying attention to the difference between hads and ilham; you see him speaking about the occurrence of hads sometimes following a request and yearning as being a noble soul strong and illuminated in itself, so sciences occur to it initially as if it has not abandoned its choice, its oil almost shines with the light of innate disposition even if a fire of thought does not touch it, and the path of ilham and hads does not deviate from the path of acquisition and thought in the science itself, nor in its location, nor in its cause, the location of science being the soul.
We find that the Arabic Language Academy tends not to differentiate between them, for after it defines hads and clarifies its intellectual and sensory type, we see it concludes that the method of perceiving realities...
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis distinguishing two cognitive sources: ilham (inspiration) and hads (intuition). The author begins by posing a logical question about the perceived overlap between these two terms among researchers. He then asserts the necessity of recalling the fundamental difference. For hads, the key characteristic is that it operates on prediction, foresight, and anticipation without deliberate cognitive effort, and its validation is not immediate; it takes time. In contrast, ilham is characterized by immediacy: the idea overwhelms the person instantly with certainty, and the person automatically feels assured of its correctness. This immediacy gives ilham a religious coloring, as it can be stimulated by devotional acts like dhikr and love of God. Thus, ilham becomes a mark of Islamic faith, while hads is universal, shared by religious and non-religious alike. The author then claims originality for this clear distinction, noting that he has not seen it before, but acknowledges it may not be absolutely correct. He uses it to explain why some classical and modern scholars treated the terms as synonyms. He cites al-Ghazali as an example of conflation, quoting Ghazali's view that hads can occur after effort and is like an innate illumination, and that the path of ilham and hads does not differ from intellectual acquisition. Finally, the author notes that the Arabic Language Academy also fails to distinguish, as it defines hads and then concludes that the method of perceiving realities (apparently lumps them together). Throughout, the author follows a question-answer structure: raising the problem, providing a clear distinction, supporting it with evidence, and acknowledging exceptions.

Essential Characteristics of Hads and Ilham
Author defines hads and ilham and explains their core differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of hads (intuition) and ilham (inspiration) and contrast them based on immediacy, certainty, and religious element.

Core Idea
Hads predicts without immediate verification; ilham brings instantaneous certainty with a religious dimension.

The author opens by recalling the essential distinction between hads and ilham. In this lesson, you will extract their core definitions and understand what sets them apart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining features of hads
Look for words like 'التنبؤ والاستشراف والترقب' (prediction, foresight, anticipation) and 'ليس لحظياً آنياً' (not instantaneous). Hads is a cognition that arises without deliberate effort and its correctness is verified later.
The author emphasizes that hads is not accompanied by immediate certainty.
2
Identify the defining features of ilham
Look for 'الآنية' (immediacy), 'اليقين' (certainty), and 'عنصر التدين' (religious element). Ilham overwhelms the person with an idea that is instantly accepted as true and often arises from devotional acts.
The religious element is the key differentiator.
3
Contrast hads and ilham
Compare their verification process and religious dimension. Hads is predictive and may be wrong; ilham is immediate and carries certainty. Hads is universal; ilham is specifically Islamic/faith-based.
This contrast is the author's original contribution.

يستنتج أن طريقة إدراك الحقائق التجريبية والعقلية وأمور أخرى لا سبيل إلى الكشف عنها إلا عن طريق الحدس ولذلك فهو أشبه بالرؤية المباشرة والإلهام. ولئن حصل هذا التداخل في الفكر التربوي الإسلامي رغم امتلاك بعض النصوص القرآنية والنبوية المساعدة على إدراك أوجه الاتفاق أو الافتراق بين الحدس والإلهام فمن باب أولى أن يقع الشيء ذاته وربما بصورة أكثر تداخلاً في الفكر التربوي الغربي. لقد أثر هنري برجسون بنظريته البصيرة أو الحدس. ويتأكد هذا المعنى حين يخرج علينا منادٍ يصيح فينا بضرورة أن نعتقد أن برجسون إمام في الإلهام. مع أن برجسون اشتهر بنظريته الحدسية وليس الإلهامية ويضرب صاحب هذه الدعوة لذلك مثالاً يؤكد الاضطراب السائد بين مفهومي الحدس والإلهام في الفكر التربوي الغربي يقول: إنك إذا التقيت رجلاً لأول مرّة، وقام أحد الأصدقاء بتعريفه لك فإنك تشعر بنفور خفي منه، ومن ثم تشعر على التو أن هذا الإنسان غير صالح للصداقة معه، ثم يتبين في الأخير صواب ذلك الإلهام.
Reminding of the essential difference between intuition and inspiration, as previously indicated that the essential characteristic of intuition is its basis on prediction, foresight, and anticipation without straining the senses or mind in performing intended perceptual or mental operations, and its verification is not instantaneous, at the moment it flashes in one's mind, contrary to inspiration, for the characteristic of inspiration is immediacy that overwhelms the person with an idea, so he finds himself speaking with certainty or applying it, automatically assured of its soundness, which gives inspiration a religious element, and it can be enkindled by purely devotional acts such as remembrance and sincere love of God. On this basis, it is this religious element that makes inspiration an Islamic, faith-based characteristic, whereas intuition is a common ground between religious people and others. It is worth noting here that this distinction between them with such clarity is something I have not found anyone to have preceded me in, and I do not claim absolute correctness, but it may explain some of that overlap which sometimes reached synonymity among some researchers, past and present. Therefore, it is not surprising to find a scholar of the stature of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali not paying attention to the distinction between intuition and inspiration. You see him speak about the occurrence of intuition sometimes after seeking and yearning, that the soul be noble, strong, and illuminated in itself, so sciences occur to it initially as if it had no choice; its oil almost shines by the light of innate nature even if no fire of thought touched it, and the path of inspiration and intuition does not differ from the path of acquisition and thought in the science itself, nor in its place, nor in its cause: 'the place of science is the soul.' We find that the Arabic Language Academy tends not to differentiate between them. After defining intuition and explaining its mental and sensory types, it concludes that the method of perceiving empirical and intellectual truths and other matters cannot be revealed except through intuition, so it is akin to direct vision and inspiration. If this overlap occurred in Islamic educational thought despite possessing some Quranic and prophetic texts that help perceive the points of agreement or divergence between intuition and inspiration, it is even more likely that the same thing, perhaps with greater overlap, occurs in Western educational thought. Henri Bergson influenced Western educational thought with his intuition theory, transcending its French milieu to Europe and America, where guidance from revealed knowledge was absent because of the conflict that swept Europe during the so-called Middle Ages and the subsequent transition toward the Renaissance after the complete exclusion of revealed knowledge that had become distorted and corrupted. And although inspiration is included among the sources and methods of religious knowledge in Western education, some of its most prominent researchers used it in the sense of insight or intuition. This meaning is confirmed when a caller declares to us that we must believe that Bergson is an imam in inspiration, whereas Bergson is famous for his intuition theory, not his inspiration theory. The proponent of this claim gives an example confirming the prevalent confusion between the concepts of intuition and inspiration in Western educational thought: 'If you meet a person for the first time, and a friend introduces him to you, you feel a hidden aversion from him, and then you immediately feel that this man is not suitable for friendship, and finally the correctness of that inspiration becomes evident.'
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and comparative analysis of two concepts: 'hads' (intuition) and 'ilham' (inspiration). The author begins by recalling the essential difference: hads is based on prediction and anticipation without conscious effort, and its correctness is not instantly verified; ilham is characterized by immediacy, overwhelming certainty, and a religious element that can be ignited by devotional acts like dhikr and sincere love of God. The author then notes that this religious component makes ilham specifically Islamic and faith-based, whereas hads is universal. The passage then addresses the historical confusion between these terms. The author claims that this clear distinction is original, and its absence explains why some scholars, including al-Ghazali, treat them synonymously. A quote from al-Ghazali is provided to illustrate how he describes the occurrence of hads and seems to equate it with ilham. The Arabic Language Academy is also cited as conflating the two, describing hads as akin to direct vision and ilham. The author then argues that if such confusion exists in Islamic thought despite having Quranic and prophetic texts that could clarify the distinction, it is even more prevalent in Western educational thought. Henri Bergson's theory of intuition is mentioned as influential, yet in Western contexts, the term 'ilham' is often used synonymously with 'hads' or 'insight'. An example is given of a person feeling an instantaneous aversion upon first meeting someone, which is later confirmed, to illustrate how Western thought employs 'ilham' in the sense of a sudden insight, thereby conflating it with hads.

Historical Conflation of Hads and Ilham
Author documents historical examples of conflation between hads and ilham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify instances of conflation between hads and ilham in Islamic and Western thought and explain why such confusion occurs.

Core Idea
Despite clear definitions, scholars like al-Ghazali and modern institutions have conflated hads and ilham, and this confusion is even more acute in the West.

After establishing the definitions, the author turns to examples of conflation. In this lesson, you will examine al-Ghazali's ambiguous usage, the Arabic Language Academy's blurring, and the misattribution of ilham to Bergson's intuition theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflation in al-Ghazali
Find the quote where al-Ghazali describes the occurrence of hads and seems to treat it as ilham. He says the soul obtains sciences 'as if it had no choice' and does not distinguish the path of inspiration from that of acquisition.
Al-Ghazali's description blurs the line between intuitive and inspired knowledge.
2
Identify the conflation in the Arabic Language Academy
Notice that after defining hads, the Academy concludes it is 'akin to direct vision and inspiration', effectively equating the two.
This shows institutional confusion even in linguistic authorities.
3
Analyze the Western conflation with Bergson
The author notes that Bergson's theory of intuition is often labeled 'ilham' in Western educational thought, despite Bergson not claiming inspiration. An example is given of a feeling of aversion being called 'ilham'.
The absence of revealed knowledge in the West exacerbates conceptual confusion.

بعض ذلك التداخل الذي وصل أحياناً حد الترادف عند بعض الباحثين قديماً وحديثاً. ولذلك فلن يكون مستغرباً أن نجد عالماً بقامة أبي حامد ساعة الغزالي لا يلتفت إلى التفريق بين الحدس والإلهام فتراه يتحدث عن حصول الحدس تارة عقيب طلب واشتياق بأن يكون نفساً شريفاً قوية مستضيئة في نفسها، فيحصل له العلوم ابتداءً كأنه ما تخلى إلى اختياره، يكاد زيتها يضئ ضؤ الفطرة، ولو لم تمسسه نار الفكرة، ولا يفارق طريق الإلهام والحدس طريق الاكتساب والفكر في نفس العلم ولا في محله، ولا في سببه، محل العلم النفس. ونجد أن مجمع اللغة العربية يميل إلى عدم التفريق بينهما، فبعد أن يعرف الحدس، ويوضح نوعية العقلي والحسي نراه يستنتج أن طريقة إدراك الحقائق التجريبية والعقلية وأمور أخرى لا سبيل إلى الكشف عنها إلا عن طريق الحدس ولذلك فهو أشبه بالرؤية المباشرة والإلهام. ولئن حصل هذا التداخل في الفكر التربوي الإسلامي رغم امتلاك بعض النصوص القرآنية والنبوية المساعدة على إدراك أوجه الاتفاق أو الافتراق بين الحدس والإلهام فمن باب أولى أن يقع الشيء ذاته وربما بصورة أكثر تداخلاً في الفكر التربوي الغربي. لقد أثر هنري برجسون بنظريته الحدسية على الفكر التربوي الغربي فتجاوز محيطه الفرنسي إلى ربوع أوروبا وأمريكا، حيث انعدم هنالك الاستهداء بمعارف الوحي من جراء الصراع الذي غشى أوروبا إبان ما عُرف هناك بالقرون الوسطى، وما تلاها من الانتقال نحو عنصر النهضة بعد إقصاء كامل لمعارف الوحي التي غدت محرفة مشوهة. والإلهام وإن كان مدرجاً ضمن مصادر المعرفة الدينية وطرائقها بالنسبة للتربية الغربية إلا أن من أبرز باحثيها من استخدمه بمعنى البصيرة أو الحدس. ويتأكد هذا المعنى حين يخرج علينا منادٍ يصيح فينا بضرورة أن نعتقد أن برجسون إمام في الإلهام. مع أن برجسون اشتهر بنظريته الحدسية وليس الإلهامية ويضرب صاحب هذه الدعوة لذلك مثالاً يؤكد الاضطراب السائد بين مفهومي الحدس والإلهام في الفكر التربوي الغربي يقول: إنك إذا التقيت رجلاً لأول مرّة، وقام أحد الأصدقاء بتعريفه لك فإنك تشعر بنفور خفي منه، ومن ثم تشعر على التو أن هذا الإنسان غير صالح للصداقة معه، ثم يتبين في الأخير صواب ذلك الإلهام.
Reminding of the essential difference between intuition and inspiration, as previously indicated that the essential characteristic of intuition is its basis on prediction, foresight, and anticipation without straining the senses or mind in performing intended perceptual or mental operations, and its verification is not instantaneous, at the moment it flashes in one's mind, contrary to inspiration, for the characteristic of inspiration is immediacy that overwhelms the person with an idea, so he finds himself speaking with certainty or applying it, automatically assured of its soundness, which gives inspiration a religious element, and it can be enkindled by purely devotional acts such as remembrance and sincere love of God. On this basis, it is this religious element that makes inspiration an Islamic, faith-based characteristic, whereas intuition is a common ground between religious people and others. It is worth noting here that this distinction between them with such clarity is something I have not found anyone to have preceded me in, and I do not claim absolute correctness, but it may explain some of that overlap which sometimes reached synonymity among some researchers, past and present. Therefore, it is not surprising to find a scholar of the stature of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali not paying attention to the distinction between intuition and inspiration. You see him speak about the occurrence of intuition sometimes after seeking and yearning, that the soul be noble, strong, and illuminated in itself, so sciences occur to it initially as if it had no choice; its oil almost shines by the light of innate nature even if no fire of thought touched it, and the path of inspiration and intuition does not differ from the path of acquisition and thought in the science itself, nor in its place, nor in its cause: 'the place of science is the soul.' We find that the Arabic Language Academy tends not to differentiate between them. After defining intuition and explaining its mental and sensory types, it concludes that the method of perceiving empirical and intellectual truths and other matters cannot be revealed except through intuition, so it is akin to direct vision and inspiration. If this overlap occurred in Islamic educational thought despite possessing some Quranic and prophetic texts that help perceive the points of agreement or divergence between intuition and inspiration, it is even more likely that the same thing, perhaps with greater overlap, occurs in Western educational thought. Henri Bergson influenced Western educational thought with his intuition theory, transcending its French milieu to Europe and America, where guidance from revealed knowledge was absent because of the conflict that swept Europe during the so-called Middle Ages and the subsequent transition toward the Renaissance after the complete exclusion of revealed knowledge that had become distorted and corrupted. And although inspiration is included among the sources and methods of religious knowledge in Western education, some of its most prominent researchers used it in the sense of insight or intuition. This meaning is confirmed when a caller declares to us that we must believe that Bergson is an imam in inspiration, whereas Bergson is famous for his intuition theory, not his inspiration theory. The proponent of this claim gives an example confirming the prevalent confusion between the concepts of intuition and inspiration in Western educational thought: 'If you meet a person for the first time, and a friend introduces him to you, you feel a hidden aversion from him, and then you immediately feel that this man is not suitable for friendship, and finally the correctness of that inspiration becomes evident.'
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and comparative analysis of two concepts: 'hads' (intuition) and 'ilham' (inspiration). The author begins by recalling the essential difference: hads is based on prediction and anticipation without conscious effort, and its correctness is not instantly verified; ilham is characterized by immediacy, overwhelming certainty, and a religious element that can be ignited by devotional acts like dhikr and sincere love of God. The author then notes that this religious component makes ilham specifically Islamic and faith-based, whereas hads is universal. The passage then addresses the historical confusion between these terms. The author claims that this clear distinction is original, and its absence explains why some scholars, including al-Ghazali, treat them synonymously. A quote from al-Ghazali is provided to illustrate how he describes the occurrence of hads and seems to equate it with ilham. The Arabic Language Academy is also cited as conflating the two, describing hads as akin to direct vision and ilham. The author then argues that if such confusion exists in Islamic thought despite having Quranic and prophetic texts that could clarify the distinction, it is even more prevalent in Western educational thought. Henri Bergson's theory of intuition is mentioned as influential, yet in Western contexts, the term 'ilham' is often used synonymously with 'hads' or 'insight'. An example is given of a person feeling an instantaneous aversion upon first meeting someone, which is later confirmed, to illustrate how Western thought employs 'ilham' in the sense of a sudden insight, thereby conflating it with hads.

Key Terms
التداخل
الترادف
النظرية الحدسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason for the greater conflation of hads and ilham in Western educational thought?
A) Western scholars intentionally ignore the distinction.
B) The absence of revealed knowledge in the West leads to conceptual confusion.
C) Bergson's theory explicitly equates intuition and inspiration.
D) The Arabic Language Academy influenced Western thought.

Key Takeaway
Historical conflation of hads and ilham occurs in both Islamic and Western thought, but is more pronounced where revealed knowledge is absent.

?

The Hierarchy of Sources: Primary vs. Subsidiary
Author defines subsidiary sources and their dependency on primary sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish subsidiary sources (ilham, hads, ru'ya saliha) from primary sources (wahy, aql, hiss) and explain the dependency of the former on the latter.

Core Idea
Subsidiary sources derive legitimacy from primary sources; they are not independent.

The author introduces the concept of subsidiary sources of knowledge, stressing that inspiration (ilham), intuition (hads), and the good dream (ru'ya saliha) lack the independent epistemic power of revelation, reason, and sense perception. This lesson breaks down that hierarchy and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary sources
Find in the passage the three primary sources/methods of knowledge the author lists. They are revelation (wahy), reason (aql), and sense (hiss).
2
List the subsidiary sources mentioned
Note that the author mentions intuition (hads), inspiration (ilham), and the good dream (ru'ya saliha) as secondary.
3
Determine the relationship
The author says subsidiary sources 'must find support from one of the primary sources.' This means they are not self-sufficient; their validity depends on alignment with wahy, aql, or hiss.
4
Understand the epistemic ranking
The author asserts subsidiary sources do not possess the same power as primary ones. This is an epistemological hierarchy: primary sources yield certainty, subsidiaries yield only conditional knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أن نتساءل هل حقا هذه هي طبيعة العلاقة التي تحكم القلب بالإلهام؟ والجواب نسترجعه من منزلة هذه المصادر التي قلنا عنها في أول الحديث إنها تبعية ثانوية، ولابد أن تجد لها سنداً من واحد من المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة للمعرفة وهي: الوحي أو العقل أو الحس. وهذا هو جوهر الفرق بينها وبين ما بالكشف والذوق وسواهما. ولذلك فإن أياً من الحدس أو الإلهام أو الرؤيا الصالحة لا تمتلك القوة ذاتها التي تمتلكها المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة أو أي واحد منها. وبناءً على هذا فإن من غير المسلّم به القول بأن القلب المعنوي قد لصاحبه بحقيقة ما لا يجد عليها دليلاً منطقياً من القلب المادي والعكس! ذلك لأن تقديم الموضوع بهذا القالب يتضمن نتيجة منطقية هي أنه لا لقاء بين الإلهام والعقل أو أن وجود الإلهام يقتضي إقصاء العقل والعكس صحيح وعلاوة على أنه لا مستند لهذا الفصل غير الموضوعي بين القلب وتشطيره إلى معنوي ومادي فإنه لن يوصل إلا إلى نتيجة غير موضوعية، ربما لم تكن لتخطر ببال أولئك الذين نحوا هذا المنحى، وهي تلك اللوازم السابقة. وهنا يجب التفريق بين حصول الإلهام فجأة من غير مقدمات منطقية، وبين مصادمته لبراهين العقل أو نتائجه الراجحة، فالأولى سمة أساسية في الإلهام، في حين أن الثانية مجرد خاطر عابر لا يلتفت إليه. وقد عزا الإمام الغزالي حصول الإلهام أو الحدس - كعادته في عدم التفريق بينهما - إلى العقل الفعّال، وردّ على من يشتبه عليه الأمر في كون هذه القوة موجودة في غير النبي، ومن ثم فليست خاصية له بأن عقل النبي فوق العقول كلّها. وأما بالنسبة إلى الحواس فكما أنه ليس للعقل تدخل مباشر في حصول الإلهام فإنه ليس للحواس أيضا قدرة مباشرة في حصول الإلهام، ولكنه يتطلب إرهافاً للحس واستعداداً بالفطرة مع المران والعمل والضبط والتنظيم. موقف القرآن من الإلهام لعله بات من اليسير الآن من خلال العرض السابق استخلاص مشروعية هذا المصدر في التصور الإسلامي، وإن كان ثمة تشديد ملح على ضرورة تقيده بما لا يخالف المصادر الرئيسية الثلاثة من وحي وعقل وحس، بحيث يكون رديفاً تبعياً لها لا مناقضاً لبعض أحكامها وفي هذه الحال - حال التقيد - يغدو من المقبول وصفه بمصدر مشروع للمعرفة. ولعل فيما ورد من الإشارات القرآنية بهذا الخصوص ما يؤكد هذا المعنى، ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا وَإِنَّ اللهَ لَمَعَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٦٩/٢٩]. وَمَنْ يَتْقِ اللهَ يَجْعَلْ لَهُ مَخْرَجاً، وَيَرْزُقْهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ لا يَحْتَسِب [الطلاق: ٢/٦٥-٣]. ويا أيها الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِن تَتَّقُوا اللَّهَ يَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ فُرْقانا ... [الأنفال: ٢٩/٨]. وهذه الآيات تشير إلى عاملي المجاهدة والتقوى كشرط لازب للحصول المعرفة الإلهامية.
We may wonder: is this truly the nature of the relationship that governs the heart with inspiration? The answer we retrieve from the status of these sources, which we said at the beginning of the discussion are secondary subsidiary, and they must find support from one of the primary sources and methods of knowledge, namely: revelation, reason, or sense. This is the essence of the difference between them and what is by unveiling, taste, and others. Therefore, none of intuition, inspiration, or the good dream possesses the same power that the primary sources and methods, or any one of them, possess. Based on this, it is not acceptable to say that the spiritual heart informs its possessor of a reality for which he finds no logical evidence from the physical heart, and vice versa. That is because presenting the topic in this mold entails a logical conclusion that there is no meeting between inspiration and reason, or that the existence of inspiration necessitates the exclusion of reason, and vice versa. Moreover, there is no basis for this non-objective division between the heart and its bifurcation into spiritual and material. It will only lead to a non-objective result, perhaps not even occurring to those who pursued this approach, namely those previous implications. Here one must distinguish between the occurrence of inspiration suddenly without logical premises, and its clashing with the proofs of reason or its preponderant conclusions. The first is an essential characteristic of inspiration, while the second is just a passing thought not to be heeded. Imam al-Ghazali attributed the occurrence of inspiration or intuition—as was his habit of not distinguishing between them—to the active intellect, and he responded to those who were confused about this power being present in other than the prophet, and thus it is not a special characteristic of him, because the prophet's intellect is above all intellects. As for the senses, just as reason has no direct involvement in the occurrence of inspiration, the senses also have no direct ability in the occurrence of inspiration. But it requires sharpening of sense and innate readiness with practice, work, discipline, and organization. The Qur'anic stance on inspiration: It has perhaps become easy now, through the previous presentation, to extract the legitimacy of this source in the Islamic conception, although there is an emphatic insistence on the necessity of it being restricted by not contradicting the three primary sources—revelation, reason, and sense—so that it becomes a subsidiary auxiliary to them, not opposing some of their rulings. In this case—the case of restriction—it becomes acceptable to describe it as a legitimate source of knowledge. Perhaps in what has come from the Qur'anic indications in this regard, that confirms this meaning. Among them is His statement, the Exalted: 'And those who strive for Us – We will surely guide them to Our ways. And indeed, Allah is with the doers of good' [Al-Ankabut 29:69]. 'And whoever fears Allah – He will make for him a way out. And will provide for him from where he does not expect' [At-Talaq 65:2-3]. And 'O you who have believed, if you fear Allah, He will grant you a criterion...' [Al-Anfal 8:29]. These verses indicate the two factors of striving and piety as a necessary condition for obtaining inspirational knowledge.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of subsidiary sources of knowledge, particularly inspiration (ilham), intuition (hads), and the good dream (ru'ya saliha). The author begins by asking about the true relationship between the heart and inspiration. He answers by reiterating that these sources are secondary and subsidiary; they must derive their validity from one of the three primary sources: revelation, reason, or sense perception. This distinguishes them from mystical 'unveiling' and 'taste' which the author implies are not similarly grounded. He asserts that none of the subsidiary sources possess the same epistemic power as the primary ones. The author then critiques a dualistic theory that splits the heart into a spiritual heart (al-qalb al-ma'nawi) and a material heart (al-qalb al-madi), claiming each can inform the other of realities without logical evidence. He argues that this view entails the false conclusion that inspiration and reason are incompatible, or that one excludes the other. This non-objective division, he warns, leads to unsupported conclusions. He makes a crucial distinction: inspiration's sudden occurrence without logical premises is its essential trait, but if it contradicts established proofs of reason, it is merely a passing thought to be dismissed. The author cites Imam al-Ghazali, who attributed inspiration to the 'active intellect' ('aql fa''al) and did not distinguish between inspiration and intuition. Ghazali defended the possibility of such power in non-prophets, though the prophet's intellect is superior. Regarding the senses, the author notes that neither reason nor senses directly cause inspiration, but inspiration requires sharpened sense and innate readiness honed through practice and discipline. Finally, the author turns to the Qur'anic stance. He argues that the legitimacy of inspiration in Islam is conditional on its not contradicting the three primary sources. When it adheres to this condition, it becomes a valid subsidiary source. He cites three verses: al-Ankabut 29:69 linking striving (jihad) to divine guidance, at-Talaq 65:2-3 linking piety (taqwa) to provision and a way out, and al-Anfal 8:29 linking piety to a criterion (furqan). These, he says, establish striving and piety as prerequisites for attaining inspirational knowledge.

Key Terms
الإلهام (al-ilhām): A sudden occurrence of knowledge in the heart without logical premises or direct sense perception, considered a subsidiary source of knowledge that must align with revelation, reason, and sense. [Note: In classical Islamic thought, ilham is often distinguished from wahy (revelation) as non-prophetic inspiration; here it is considered a legitimate subsidiary source when conditioned.]
الحدس (al-ḥads): Intuition, used interchangeably with ilham by al-Ghazali; a rapid apprehension of truth without syllogistic reasoning. [Note: In logic, hads often refers to 'acumen' or 'intuition' in the context of the middle term discovery; here it is treated as a type of inspiration.]
الرؤيا الصالحة (al-ruʾyā al-ṣāliḥa): The good dream, considered a subsidiary source of knowledge alongside inspiration and intuition. [Note: In hadith, true dreams are part of prophethood; here it is epistemologically ranked below primary sources.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Subsidiary sources like inspiration and intuition derive their epistemic value only when they align with revelation, reason, or sense perception.

Critique of Dualistic Heart Theory and False Dichotomy
Author critiques dualistic heart theory and false dichotomy between inspiration and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dualistic heart theory that separates spiritual and material hearts, and identify the logical consequences of assuming inspiration and reason are incompatible.

Core Idea
Inspiration does not oppose reason; sudden occurrence is not contradiction.

The author critiques a theory that bifurcates the heart into spiritual and material, which implies that inspiration and reason cannot coexist. This lesson dissects that critique, showing how a false dichotomy leads to unsupported conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dualistic theory under critique
Find the description of a theory that splits the heart into a spiritual heart (al-qalb al-ma'nawi) and a material heart (al-qalb al-madi), each informing its possessor without logical evidence from the other.
2
Extract the logical consequence
The author states that this theory entails the conclusion that there is no meeting between inspiration and reason, or that one excludes the other.
3
Note the author's objection
The author says this division is non-objective and leads to non-objective results that its proponents may not have intended.
4
Distinguish between sudden occurrence and contradiction
The author makes a key distinction: inspiration's suddenness (without premises) is its essential trait, but if it clashes with rational proofs, it is just a passing thought to be ignored.
