⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أن نتساءل هل حقا هذه هي طبيعة العلاقة التي تحكم القلب بالإلهام؟ والجواب نسترجعه من منزلة هذه المصادر التي قلنا عنها في أول الحديث إنها تبعية ثانوية، ولابد أن تجد لها سنداً من واحد من المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة للمعرفة وهي: الوحي أو العقل أو الحس. وهذا هو جوهر الفرق بينها وبين ما بالكشف والذوق وسواهما. ولذلك فإن أياً من الحدس أو الإلهام أو الرؤيا الصالحة لا تمتلك القوة ذاتها التي تمتلكها المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة أو أي واحد منها. وبناءً على هذا فإن من غير المسلّم به القول بأن القلب المعنوي قد لصاحبه بحقيقة ما لا يجد عليها دليلاً منطقياً من القلب المادي والعكس! ذلك لأن تقديم الموضوع بهذا القالب يتضمن نتيجة منطقية هي أنه لا لقاء بين الإلهام والعقل أو أن وجود الإلهام يقتضي إقصاء العقل والعكس صحيح وعلاوة على أنه لا مستند لهذا الفصل غير الموضوعي بين القلب وتشطيره إلى معنوي ومادي فإنه لن يوصل إلا إلى نتيجة غير موضوعية، ربما لم تكن لتخطر ببال أولئك الذين نحوا هذا المنحى، وهي تلك اللوازم السابقة. وهنا يجب التفريق بين حصول الإلهام فجأة من غير مقدمات منطقية، وبين مصادمته لبراهين العقل أو نتائجه الراجحة، فالأولى سمة أساسية في الإلهام، في حين أن الثانية مجرد خاطر عابر لا يلتفت إليه. وقد عزا الإمام الغزالي حصول الإلهام أو الحدس - كعادته في عدم التفريق بينهما - إلى العقل الفعّال، وردّ على من يشتبه عليه الأمر في كون هذه القوة موجودة في غير النبي، ومن ثم فليست خاصية له بأن عقل النبي فوق العقول كلّها. وأما بالنسبة إلى الحواس فكما أنه ليس للعقل تدخل مباشر في حصول الإلهام فإنه ليس للحواس أيضا قدرة مباشرة في حصول الإلهام، ولكنه يتطلب إرهافاً للحس واستعداداً بالفطرة مع المران والعمل والضبط والتنظيم. موقف القرآن من الإلهام لعله بات من اليسير الآن من خلال العرض السابق استخلاص مشروعية هذا المصدر في التصور الإسلامي، وإن كان ثمة تشديد ملح على ضرورة تقيده بما لا يخالف المصادر الرئيسية الثلاثة من وحي وعقل وحس، بحيث يكون رديفاً تبعياً لها لا مناقضاً لبعض أحكامها وفي هذه الحال - حال التقيد - يغدو من المقبول وصفه بمصدر مشروع للمعرفة. ولعل فيما ورد من الإشارات القرآنية بهذا الخصوص ما يؤكد هذا المعنى، ومن ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا وَإِنَّ اللهَ لَمَعَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٦٩/٢٩]. وَمَنْ يَتْقِ اللهَ يَجْعَلْ لَهُ مَخْرَجاً، وَيَرْزُقْهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ لا يَحْتَسِب [الطلاق: ٢/٦٥-٣]. ويا أيها الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِن تَتَّقُوا اللَّهَ يَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ فُرْقانا ... [الأنفال: ٢٩/٨]. وهذه الآيات تشير إلى عاملي المجاهدة والتقوى كشرط لازب للحصول المعرفة الإلهامية.
We may wonder: is this truly the nature of the relationship that governs the heart with inspiration? The answer we retrieve from the status of these sources, which we said at the beginning of the discussion are secondary subsidiary, and they must find support from one of the primary sources and methods of knowledge, namely: revelation, reason, or sense. This is the essence of the difference between them and what is by unveiling, taste, and others. Therefore, none of intuition, inspiration, or the good dream possesses the same power that the primary sources and methods, or any one of them, possess. Based on this, it is not acceptable to say that the spiritual heart informs its possessor of a reality for which he finds no logical evidence from the physical heart, and vice versa. That is because presenting the topic in this mold entails a logical conclusion that there is no meeting between inspiration and reason, or that the existence of inspiration necessitates the exclusion of reason, and vice versa. Moreover, there is no basis for this non-objective division between the heart and its bifurcation into spiritual and material. It will only lead to a non-objective result, perhaps not even occurring to those who pursued this approach, namely those previous implications. Here one must distinguish between the occurrence of inspiration suddenly without logical premises, and its clashing with the proofs of reason or its preponderant conclusions. The first is an essential characteristic of inspiration, while the second is just a passing thought not to be heeded. Imam al-Ghazali attributed the occurrence of inspiration or intuition—as was his habit of not distinguishing between them—to the active intellect, and he responded to those who were confused about this power being present in other than the prophet, and thus it is not a special characteristic of him, because the prophet's intellect is above all intellects. As for the senses, just as reason has no direct involvement in the occurrence of inspiration, the senses also have no direct ability in the occurrence of inspiration. But it requires sharpening of sense and innate readiness with practice, work, discipline, and organization. The Qur'anic stance on inspiration: It has perhaps become easy now, through the previous presentation, to extract the legitimacy of this source in the Islamic conception, although there is an emphatic insistence on the necessity of it being restricted by not contradicting the three primary sources—revelation, reason, and sense—so that it becomes a subsidiary auxiliary to them, not opposing some of their rulings. In this case—the case of restriction—it becomes acceptable to describe it as a legitimate source of knowledge. Perhaps in what has come from the Qur'anic indications in this regard, that confirms this meaning. Among them is His statement, the Exalted: 'And those who strive for Us – We will surely guide them to Our ways. And indeed, Allah is with the doers of good' [Al-Ankabut 29:69]. 'And whoever fears Allah – He will make for him a way out. And will provide for him from where he does not expect' [At-Talaq 65:2-3]. And 'O you who have believed, if you fear Allah, He will grant you a criterion...' [Al-Anfal 8:29]. These verses indicate the two factors of striving and piety as a necessary condition for obtaining inspirational knowledge.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of subsidiary sources of knowledge, particularly inspiration (ilham), intuition (hads), and the good dream (ru'ya saliha). The author begins by asking about the true relationship between the heart and inspiration. He answers by reiterating that these sources are secondary and subsidiary; they must derive their validity from one of the three primary sources: revelation, reason, or sense perception. This distinguishes them from mystical 'unveiling' and 'taste' which the author implies are not similarly grounded. He asserts that none of the subsidiary sources possess the same epistemic power as the primary ones. The author then critiques a dualistic theory that splits the heart into a spiritual heart (al-qalb al-ma'nawi) and a material heart (al-qalb al-madi), claiming each can inform the other of realities without logical evidence. He argues that this view entails the false conclusion that inspiration and reason are incompatible, or that one excludes the other. This non-objective division, he warns, leads to unsupported conclusions. He makes a crucial distinction: inspiration's sudden occurrence without logical premises is its essential trait, but if it contradicts established proofs of reason, it is merely a passing thought to be dismissed. The author cites Imam al-Ghazali, who attributed inspiration to the 'active intellect' ('aql fa''al) and did not distinguish between inspiration and intuition. Ghazali defended the possibility of such power in non-prophets, though the prophet's intellect is superior. Regarding the senses, the author notes that neither reason nor senses directly cause inspiration, but inspiration requires sharpened sense and innate readiness honed through practice and discipline. Finally, the author turns to the Qur'anic stance. He argues that the legitimacy of inspiration in Islam is conditional on its not contradicting the three primary sources. When it adheres to this condition, it becomes a valid subsidiary source. He cites three verses: al-Ankabut 29:69 linking striving (jihad) to divine guidance, at-Talaq 65:2-3 linking piety (taqwa) to provision and a way out, and al-Anfal 8:29 linking piety to a criterion (furqan). These, he says, establish striving and piety as prerequisites for attaining inspirational knowledge.

Key Terms
الإلهام (al-ilhām): A sudden occurrence of knowledge in the heart without logical premises or direct sense perception, considered a subsidiary source of knowledge that must align with revelation, reason, and sense. [Note: In classical Islamic thought, ilham is often distinguished from wahy (revelation) as non-prophetic inspiration; here it is considered a legitimate subsidiary source when conditioned.]
القلب المعنوي (al-qalb al-maʿnawī): The spiritual heart, which in the dualistic theory is said to inform its possessor of realities without logical evidence from the material heart. [Note: This term is used in the context of the critique; not a standard term in Islamic epistemology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the essential characteristic of inspiration (ilham)?
A) It is always opposed to reason.
B) It occurs suddenly without logical premises.
C) It requires logical evidence from the material heart.
D) It only occurs in prophets.

Key Takeaway
Inspiration's sudden occurrence is not a license to contradict reason; the two are not inherently incompatible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The spiritual heart knows truths that the rational mind cannot grasp, so reason is irrelevant for spiritual knowledge.' Identify the dualistic assumption in this statement and explain how the author would critique it.
?
Using the author's distinction between sudden occurrence and contradiction, evaluate a claimed inspiration that directly contradicts a clear rational or revealed ruling. Why does the author consider it a 'passing thought'?

Qur'anic Conditions for Inspirational Knowledge
Author establishes Qur'anic basis for ilham conditioned on striving and piety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Qur'anic conditions (jihad and taqwa) for obtaining legitimate inspirational knowledge and explain how they ensure harmony with primary sources.

Core Idea
Striving and piety are the keys to legitimate inspirational knowledge.

The author concludes by grounding the legitimacy of inspiration in the Quran, citing verses that link divine guidance to striving (jihad) and piety (taqwa). This lesson unpacks those conditions and their role in ensuring inspirational knowledge aligns with revelation, reason, and sense.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Qur'anic verses cited
Find the three verses: al-Ankabut 29:69, at-Talaq 65:2-3, and al-Anfal 8:29. They are quoted at the end of the passage.
2
Extract the two conditions
The author explicitly states that these verses indicate 'the two factors of striving and piety' (mujahada and taqwa) as necessary conditions.
3
Explain the link to primary sources
The author earlier insisted that inspirational knowledge must not contradict the three primary sources. Striving and piety ensure that one's inspiration is in line with divine will and rational principles.
4
Assess the legitimacy
Only when these conditions are met does inspiration become a 'legitimate source of knowledge'. Otherwise, it is rejected.

Inspiration and Scientific Thinking: No Contradiction
Author argues inspiration and scientific thinking are compatible, citing scientists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's argument for the compatibility of inspiration and scientific thinking and evaluate the use of scientific testimonies in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Inspiration and science are complementary, not contradictory.

The author addresses a logical question: does accepting inspiration as a source of knowledge contradict scientific thinking? He argues there is no contradiction, and supports his position with both Quranic principles and testimonies from Nobel laureates. This lesson will help you analyze his argumentative strategy and understand how inspiration is framed as a legitimate secondary source within an Islamic epistemological framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial question
Find the rhetorical question the author poses at the beginning: does a contradiction arise when inspiration is accepted as a secondary source, especially given Quranic support? This sets up the problem.
2
Locate the thesis statement
The author explicitly states that there is no contradiction. Identify the sentence where he says 'التعارض منتف' (the contradiction is non-existent).
3
Extract the supporting reasons
The author gives two main supports: (1) The Quran supports inspiration with specific restrictions. (2) The scientific method itself cannot explain many phenomena, including inspiration, and scientists acknowledge non-sensory knowledge. List these reasons.
4
Analyze the scientist quotes
Read the quotes from Charles Piché, Brinton, and Alexis Carrel. How does each quote support the claim that inspiration is a valid source? Note that Carrel explicitly connects great discoveries to what was once called 'inspiration' or 'revelation'.
Consider the rhetorical force of citing Nobel prize winners in a religious epistemological argument.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
Assess whether the author successfully proves compatibility. Does he rely on authority (scientists) or rational reasoning? Is the argument convincing from an Islamic perspective? What assumptions does he make about the nature of scientific thinking?
Think about how a classical scholar like Ibn Taymiyya might respond to this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثمة سؤال منطقي ينشأ هنا وهو: ألا يقع قدر من التعارض كبيراً كان أو صغيراً عند إقرار مصدرية ثانوية للمعرفة هي الإلهام، خاصة حينما يساند ذلك القرآن الكريم ومنهج التفكير العلمي؟. والحق أن التعارض بين الإلهام ومنهج التفكير العلمي منتف، أو هو مما لا وجود له أساساً بينهما. ومن هذا الباب جاءت مساندة القرآن الكريم للإلهام وفق القيود المشار إليها سلفاً. وليس مما يقلل من أهمية هذا المصدر كونه لم يقع تحت نظرية اختبارية أو عقلية نتيجة طريقتها التي تجعل من الاتصال الداخلي بين الإنسان والحقيقة الكونية أو (الله) أو عالم الأرواح مبنية على نوع من التخاطب الروحي بين الفرد وبين الكائنات الروحية بشرية كانت أو ذات وجود أولي مستقل(1). وكون منهج التفكير العلمي لا يملك تفسيراً محدداً لمصدرية الإلهام فليس ذلك دليلاً على أن الإلهام ليس منهجاً علمياً. إن منهج التفكير العلمي يقف اليوم عاجزاً أمام ظواهر شتى لا يجد لها تفسيراً علمياً تجريبياً أو عقلياً، ولكنه في الوقت ذاته لا يملك إلا إعلان العجز حالياً، والاستعداد لمزيد من البحث مستقبلاً. يقول شارل بيشه الحائز على جائزة نوبل في الفيزيولوجيا: ((لقد استطعت في (أدنبره) أن أؤكد أمام مئة من علماء الجسم أن حواسنا الخمس ليست هي الوسائل الوحيدة للمعرفة، بل إن ثمة شذرات من الحقيقة، ترد أحياناً للإدراك من طرق أخرى))(1). ويقول (برينتون): (( إن ثمة شيئاً ضمن عقل الإنسان والحيوان، لا هو بالعقل ولا بالشعور بل أعمق من كليهما، يمكن أن يكون وصف الإلهام ملائماً له))(2) ويقول الكسيس (كاريل) الحائز على جائزة نوبل في الطب: (إن الحقيقة المستمدة من العلم تختلف اختلافاً تاماً عن تلك المستمدة من الإيمان. فالأخيرة أكثر عمقاً، ولا يمكن التشكيك فيها بالمجادلات، إنها تشبه الحقيقة التي يعطيها البصر المغناطيسي، ولكن مما يدعو للغرابة أن هذه الحقيقة ليست غريبة على العلم. إذ من الواضح أن الاكتشافات الكبيرة ليست نتاج العقل فقط. فإن العباقرة يمتلكون إلى جانب قوتهم على الملاحظة والفهم صفات أخرى مثل البصيرة والخيال المبتدع، فعن طريق البصيرة يتعلمون أشياء يجهلها الآخرون، ويدركون العلاقات بين الظواهر شبه المنفصلة كما يحسون بطريقة لا شعورية بوجود الكنز غير المعروف، وجميع عظماء الرجال وهبهم الله بصيرة، فهم يعرفون دون تحليل أو تفكير ما هي الأشياء الهادفة التي يجب عليهم أن يعرفوها ... أما العالم النابغة فيسلك بالغريزة الطريق المؤدي إلى الاكتشاف. ولقد كان يطلق على هذه الظاهرة اسم الإلهام أو الوحي في الأزمنة السابقة)(3). كما ويؤكد (كاريل) على أن الحس ليس وحده مصدراً أو وسيلة للمعرفة
A logical question arises here: Is there a degree of contradiction, large or small, when establishing a secondary source of knowledge which is inspiration, especially since the Noble Qur'an and the scientific thinking method support that? The truth is that the contradiction between inspiration and the scientific thinking method is non-existent, or it has no existence between them at all. And from this door came the support of the Noble Qur'an for inspiration according to the restrictions previously mentioned. And it is not among the things that diminish the importance of this source that it was not subjected to an experimental or rational theory as a result of its method that makes the internal connection between man and the cosmic truth or (God) or the world of spirits based on a type of spiritual communication between the individual and spiritual beings, whether human or having independent primary existence (1). And the fact that the scientific thinking method does not have a specific explanation for the source of inspiration is not evidence that inspiration is not a scientific method. Indeed, the scientific thinking method today stands incapable before various phenomena for which it finds no experimental or rational scientific explanation, but at the same time it can only declare current incapability and prepare for more research in the future. Charles Piché, Nobel Prize winner in physiology, says: 'In Edinburgh, I was able to affirm before a hundred scientists that our five senses are not the only means of knowledge, but rather there are fragments of truth that sometimes come to perception through other ways.' (1) And Brinton says: 'There is something within the mind of man and animal, not the mind nor consciousness but deeper than both, that the description of inspiration may be suitable for it.' (2) And Alexis Carrel, Nobel Prize winner in medicine, says: 'The truth derived from science differs completely from that derived from faith. The latter is deeper and cannot be doubted by arguments; it resembles the truth given by magnetic vision, but strangely enough this truth is not foreign to science. It is clear that great discoveries are not the product of the mind alone. Geniuses possess, besides their power of observation and understanding, other qualities such as insight and creative imagination. Through insight they learn things that others are ignorant of, and perceive relationships between seemingly separate phenomena, and they feel unconsciously the existence of the unknown treasure. All great men are gifted by God with insight; they know without analysis or thinking what purposeful things they must know... The genius scientist instinctively follows the path leading to discovery. In former times this phenomenon was called inspiration or revelation.' (3) And Carrel also emphasizes that the senses are not the only source or means of knowledge.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the status of inspiration (ilham) as a source of knowledge and its relationship with scientific thinking. The author begins by posing a logical question: does a contradiction arise when inspiration is accepted as a secondary source, especially when both the Quran and scientific method seem to support it? The answer is emphatic: there is no contradiction whatsoever. The author then explains that the Quran's support for inspiration comes with specific restrictions (mentioned earlier). The fact that inspiration cannot be verified by experimental or rational methods does not diminish its value; rather, the scientific method itself is often incapable of explaining many phenomena, including inspiration. To bolster this argument, the author quotes three Western scientists: Charles Piché (Nobel in physiology), Brinton, and Alexis Carrel (Nobel in medicine). All three attest to the existence of non-sensory ways of knowing—such as insight or inspiration—and acknowledge that great discoveries often involve these extra-rational faculties. Carrel explicitly equates this with what was historically called inspiration or revelation. The passage concludes by reinforcing that sense perception is not the only source of knowledge. The argument flows from a rhetorical question, to a denial of conflict, to a justification using authoritative scientific testimony, and finally to a reaffirmation of inspiration's epistemic legitimacy.

Key Terms
الإلهام
التفكير العلمي
المصادر التبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between inspiration (ilham) and scientific thinking?
A) They are in complete conflict.
B) There is no contradiction between them.
C) Inspiration supersedes scientific thinking.
D) Scientific thinking invalidates inspiration.

Key Takeaway
Inspiration is a legitimate secondary source of knowledge that does not contradict scientific thinking; it is complementary and acknowledged even by prominent scientists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the main claim and supporting evidence in the passage. Then, find a similar argument in a classical Islamic text about the validity of *kashf* as a source of knowledge (e.g., al-Ghazali's *Ihya'*).
?
Construct a brief response from the perspective of an Ibn Taymiyya-style critic: what objections might he raise against the author's use of non-Muslim scientists as authorities?

Non-Sensory Knowledge in Modern Science and Islamic Epistemology
Author argues for non-sensory knowledge sources, citing Western scientists and parapsychology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the non-sensory sources of knowledge cited in the passage, explain the author's argument from authority, and relate these concepts to the Islamic epistemological tradition.

Core Idea
The senses are not the only path to knowledge; inspiration and extrasensory perception are presented as valid sources.

In this passage, the author draws on Western scientists to argue that knowledge can come through channels beyond the five senses. We encounter terms like inspiration, telepathy, and parapsychology. This lesson will help you analyze the argument and see how these concepts resonate with Islamic ideas of ilham and kashf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of non-sensory knowledge
The passage mentions inspiration (al-ilham), telepathy (al-tilbathi), and parapsychology (al-barasikuluji). Find the definitions given in the text for each.
Note that inspiration is described as a deep faculty beyond intellect and feeling.
2
Examine the evidence provided
The author relies on testimonies from Nobel laureates. Write down each testimony and the specific claim it supports.
Consider quoting Charles Biche, Brinton, and Alexis Carrel.
3
Connect to Islamic epistemology
Consider how these concepts relate to Islamic sources of knowledge: ilham, kashf, and the role of reason. Note the shift: the passage uses 'revelation' interchangeably with 'inspiration', but Islamic tradition distinguishes wahy from ilham.
Think about the debate among Islamic scholars about the validity of ilham as a source.

كما ويؤكد (كاريل) على أن الحس ليس وحده مصدراً أو وسيلة للمعرفة فيقول: وهكذا فإن معرفة العالم الخارجي قد تصل إلى الإنسان عن طريق مصادر أخرى غير أعضاء الحس. ومن المحقق أن الفكر قد ينتقل من فرد إلى آخر، ولو كانت تفصل بينهما مسافة كبيرة. وهذه الحقائق التي تنتمي إلى علم ما وراء النفس الجديد يجب أن تقبل على علاتها. إنها تكون جزءاً من الحقيقة، وتعبّر عن جانب قادر يكاد يكون غير معروف من أنفسنا. ومن الجائز أنها مسؤولة عن الدّقة العقلية الحادثة التي تلاحظ في أفراد معينين. ياله من اتحاد غير عادي ذلك الذي ينتج من اتحاد العقل النظامي والاستعداد التلباني حقاً) (۱) إن ما يشير إليه (كاريل) من انتماء هذه الحقائق إلى علم ما وراء النفس هو ما غدا اليوم معروفاً بعلم الباراسيكولوجي Parapsychology أو (الإدراك خارج الحواس) (۲) اختصاراً لجملة Extra Sensory Perception ، أما إشارته إلى ما أسماه بالاستعداد التلبـائي Telepathy فإشارة إلى جملة الظواهر التي أصبحت تدرس عن طريق التلباثي. ويعني: نقل الرغبات والصور أو الخواطر دون استخدام الحواس الخمس)).
ومن المؤسف أن طرائق التفكير القائمة على متطلبات الوضعية المتزمتة ما تزال تمثل عائقاً يحول دون تحقيق أهداف كل من (الباراسيكولوجي) و(التلباثي) لأن طرائقها المستخدمة تماثل طرائق الإدراك أو البصر مثلاً، مما يؤدي إلى الوقوع في العجز عن الإحاطة بهذه الظواهر غير العادية، فضلاً عن أنه فشل في أن يعكس الطابع البحثي المتعدّد لهذا المجال.
ولذلك فقد نشأ علم جديد هو علم (السيكوترونات) الذي يدرس هذه الظواهر من خلال تناول تشترك فيه فروع العلم المختلفة كالفيزياء والبيولوجيا وعلم النفس.
Chapter Three: Section Four - Subsidiary Sources
Charles Biche, Nobel Prize winner in physiology, says: “In Edinburgh I was able to affirm before a hundred body scientists that our five senses are not the only means of knowledge, but rather there are fragments of truth that sometimes come to perception through other ways.”
And (Brinton) says: “There is something within the mind of man and animal, neither intellect nor feeling but deeper than both, for which the description 'inspiration' can be suitable.”
And Alexis Carrel, Nobel Prize winner in medicine, says: “Truth derived from science differs completely from that derived from faith. The latter is deeper, and cannot be doubted through arguments. It resembles the truth given by magnetic vision, but oddly this truth is not alien to science. It is clear that great discoveries are not only the product of intellect. Geniuses possess, alongside their power of observation and understanding, other qualities such as insight and creative imagination. Through insight they learn things others ignore, perceive relationships between semi-separate phenomena, and sense in an unconscious way the existence of unknown treasure. All great men God has given insight; they know without analysis or thought what purposeful things they must know... The brilliant scientist instinctively follows the path leading to discovery. In former times this phenomenon was called inspiration or revelation.”
Carrel also affirms that sense is not the only source or means of knowledge, saying: “Thus knowledge of the external world may reach man through other sources than sense organs. It is proven that thought can transfer from one individual to another even if a great distance separates them. These facts, which belong to the new science of metaphysics, must be accepted as they are. They form part of the truth, expressing a powerful side almost unknown of ourselves. It is possible that they are responsible for the emerged mental precision observed in certain individuals. What an extraordinary union produced by the union of systematic mind and telepathic readiness indeed!” (1) What Carrel refers to as belonging to the science of metaphysics is today known as Parapsychology or Extra Sensory Perception (2), abbreviated from the phrase Extra Sensory Perception. As for his reference to what he called telepathic readiness (Telepathy), it refers to the set of phenomena studied through telepathy. It means: the transfer of desires, images, or thoughts without using the five senses.
It is regrettable that methods of thinking based on the demands of rigid positivism still represent an obstacle preventing the achievement of the goals of both parapsychology and telepathy, because the methods used resemble those of perception or sight, for example, leading to inability to comprehend these extraordinary phenomena, besides failing to reflect the multifaceted research character of this field.
Therefore, a new science has emerged: psychotronics, which studies these phenomena through an approach in which various branches of science participate, such as physics, biology, and psychology.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion arguing for subsidiary sources of knowledge beyond the five senses. The author begins by quoting Charles Biche, who claims to have demonstrated before scientists that the senses are not the only means of knowledge; fragments of truth sometimes come through other ways. Then Brinton is quoted positing a deeper faculty in mind beyond intellect and feeling, for which 'inspiration' is apt. Alexis Carrel is then extensively quoted, asserting that scientific truth differs from faith truth, which is deeper and indubitable; great discoveries involve insight and imagination, not just intellect. He calls earlier terminology for this 'inspiration' or 'revelation'. Carrel further argues that knowledge can come through non-sensory channels, such as thought transfer over distance, and that these facts belong to metaphysics (now parapsychology) and must be accepted. The author explains that Carrel's 'telepathic readiness' refers to telepathy, the transfer of desires and thoughts without senses. The passage then criticizes positivist methods as obstacles to studying these phenomena because they use sensory-based methods. Finally, it mentions psychotronics as a new interdisciplinary science studying these phenomena. The commentary notes that the passage uses Western scientific authorities to support non-sensory knowledge, which parallels Islamic concepts of ilham (inspiration) and kashf (unveiling) as valid epistemological sources in traditional Islamic thought.

Key Terms
الإلهام
التلباثي
الباراسيكولوجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Inspiration or revelation
C) Telepathy
D) Theoretical reason

Key Takeaway
The passage presents inspiration, telepathy, and parapsychology as valid extrasensory knowledge sources, using Western scientific authority, inviting comparison with Islamic concepts.

Secondary Sources of Knowledge: Telepathy, Parapsychology, and Inspiration
Author discusses telepathy and inspiration as non-sensory knowledge sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate telepathy and inspiration as sources of knowledge by distinguishing psychological, scientific, and religious perspectives.

Core Idea
Knowledge can reach humans beyond the five senses, but its validity must be assessed through Islamic epistemology.

This passage presents telepathy, parapsychology, and inspiration as real but non-sensory sources of knowledge. The author argues that rigid positivist methods fail to capture these phenomena, and he contrasts psychological explanations with a religious interpretation that attributes inspiration to Allah's will. As students of Islamic intellectual tradition, you will learn to critically assess such claims by applying the epistemological criteria used in Kalam and Usul al-Din.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about thought transfer
Find the sentence where the author states that thought can transfer between individuals over a distance. This is the foundational claim for telepathy.
The author cites Carrel and modern parapsychology.
2
Analyze the critique of positivist methods
Locate the paragraph where the author criticizes 'rigid positivism.' What is the specific objection? Why does the author think these methods fail?
The author says positivist methods resemble ordinary perception and cannot capture extraordinary phenomena.
3
Compare psychological and religious explanations of inspiration
Read the section on inspiration. Note the points of agreement (physiological and psychological processes) and the additional religious claim (Allah's direct involvement).
The religious explanation adds that Allah may inspire truths outside creative thinking.
4
Evaluate the epistemological status
Assess whether telepathy and inspiration, as described, meet the criteria for certain knowledge (yaqin) in the Islamic tradition. Consider the role of sense perception, reason, and revelation.
Classical Islamic epistemology typically requires sense, reason, or authentic report for certainty.

الباب الثالث: الفصل الرابع - المصادر التبعية

فيقول: وهكذا فإن معرفة العالم الخارجي قد تصل إلى الإنسان عن طريق مصادر أخرى غير أعضاء الحس. ومن المحقق أن الفكر قد ينتقل من فرد إلى آخر، ولو كانت تفصل بينهما مسافة كبيرة. وهذه الحقائق التي تنتمي إلى علم ما وراء النفس الجديد يجب أن تقبل على علاتها. إنها تكون جزءاً من الحقيقة، وتعبّر عن جانب قادر يكاد يكون غير معروف من أنفسنا. ومن الجائز أنها مسؤولة عن الدّقة العقلية الحادثة التي تلاحظ في أفراد معينين. ياله من اتحاد غير عادي ذلك الذي ينتج من اتحاد العقل النظامي والاستعداد التلباني حقاً) (۱) إن ما يشير إليه (كاريل) من انتماء هذه الحقائق إلى علم ما وراء النفس هو ما غدا اليوم معروفاً بعلم الباراسيكولوجي) Parapsychology أو (الإدراك خارج الحواس) (۲) اختصاراً لجملة: Extra Sensory Perception ، أما إشارته إلى ما أسماه بالاستعداد التلبـائي (Telepathy) فإشارة إلى جملة الظواهر التي أصبحت تدرس عن طريق التلباثي. ويعني: نقل الرغبات والصور أو الخواطر دون استخدام الحواس الخمس)) (۳).

ومن المؤسف أن طرائق التفكير القائمة على متطلبات الوضعية المتزمتة ما تزال تمثل عائقاً يحول دون تحقيق أهداف كل من (الباراسيكولوجي) و(التلباثي) لأن طرائقها المستخدمة تماثل طرائق الإدراك أو البصر مثلاً، مما يؤدي إلى الوقوع في العجز عن الإحاطة بهذه الظواهر غير العادية، فضلاً عن أنه فشل في أن يعكس الطابع البحثي المتعدّد لهذا المجال.

ولذلك فقد نشأ علم جديد هـو علـم (السيكوترونات) الذي يدرس هذه الظواهر من خلال تناول تشترك فيه فروع العلم المختلفة كالفيزياء والبيولوجيا وعلم النفس (٤).

ومن الناحية النفسية البحتة فليس ثمة تعارض بين التفسير العلمي والإلهام لدى علماء النفس المحدثين وبين التفسير الديني له، من حيث إن الجميع متفقون على أنه يحدث نتيجة لعمليات فسيولوجية تتم في المخ وعمليات سيكولوجية مصاحبة لها، ولكن التفسير الديني يتجاوز ذلك المستوى رغم الاتفاق عليه إلى هذا الحدّ - إلى القول بأن الله سبحانه - هو المتصرف في كل شيء في الكون، بما في ذلك عملية الإلهام ذاتها، فقد يهدي الله - تعالى - تفكير بعض الناس لاكتشاف بعض الحقائق التي شاءت إرادته إلهامهم إياها، فتبدوا لهم كما لو أنها أشرقت في عقولهم فجأة، بل إن إرادة الله ومشيئته قـد تقتضي إحداث هذا الإلهام في غير أوقات التفكير المبدع (۱).

بل ويذهب علماء النفس في تفسيرهم لكيفية حصول الإلهام إلى أنه يتم بعد المرور بالخطوات التالية:

١- عند عدم الاهتداء إلى حل المشكلة عادة ما يترك الباحث هذه الفكرة فترة، ثم يعود إلى محاولة حلها بعد راحة تشبه فترة الحضانة، يخلص فيها تفكيره من بعض الشوائب المعيقة.

-
- يرتاح الذهن من الاجتهاد الذي يصيبه من جراء مواصلة التفكير في حلّ المشكلة، ليعود أكثر صفاء.

٣- يتمكن الذهن من تنظيم المعلومات فيؤدي إلى اكتشاف بعض العلاقات التي لم تكن واضحة من قبل، فتتجلى بعض الأفكار الجديدة، ومن ثم يهتدي إلى حل المشكلة (٢).
Chapter Three, Section Four: Secondary Sources

He says: Thus, knowledge of the external world may reach humans through sources other than the sense organs. It is established that thought can transfer from one individual to another, even if a great distance separates them. These facts, which belong to the new science of parapsychology, must be accepted as they are. They are part of reality and express a powerful, almost unknown aspect of ourselves. It is possible that they are responsible for the acute mental precision observed in certain individuals. What an extraordinary union produced by the union of the systematic mind and telepathic readiness! (1) What Carrel refers to as belonging to the science of parapsychology is now known as parapsychology or extrasensory perception (2), abbreviated as Extra Sensory Perception. As for his reference to what he called telepathic readiness (Telepathy), it refers to the set of phenomena studied through telepathy. It means: the transfer of desires, images, or thoughts without using the five senses (3).

Unfortunately, methods of thinking based on the demands of rigid positivism still constitute an obstacle hindering the achievement of the goals of both parapsychology and telepathy, because their used methods resemble the methods of perception or sight, for example, leading to an inability to encompass these extraordinary phenomena, besides failing to reflect the multi-research nature of this field.

Therefore, a new science emerged: psychotronics, which studies these phenomena through an approach involving various branches of science such as physics, biology, and psychology (4).

From a purely psychological perspective, there is no contradiction between the scientific explanation of inspiration among modern psychologists and the religious explanation thereof, in that all agree that it occurs as a result of physiological processes in the brain and accompanying psychological processes. However, the religious explanation goes beyond that level, despite agreement on it, to saying that Allah—glory be to Him—is the disposer of everything in the universe, including the very process of inspiration. So Allah, the Exalted, may guide the thinking of some people to discover certain truths that His will intended to inspire them with, so they appear to them as if they suddenly shone in their minds. Indeed, Allah's will and decree may necessitate the occurrence of this inspiration at times other than creative thinking (1).

Moreover, psychologists go in their interpretation of how inspiration occurs that it happens after going through the following steps:

1. When a solution to a problem is not found, the researcher usually leaves this idea for a period, then returns to trying to solve it after a rest resembling an incubation period, in which his thinking is cleared of some obstructive impurities.

- The mind rests from the effort it suffers due to continuous thinking about solving the problem, to return clearer.

3. The mind is able to organize information, leading to the discovery of some relationships that were not clear before, so some new ideas become manifest, and then he is guided to a solution of the problem (2).
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of secondary sources of knowledge, specifically telepathy, parapsychology, and inspiration. The author begins by asserting that humans can acquire knowledge of the external world through sources beyond the five senses, such as thought transference between individuals separated by distance. These phenomena are studied by the new science of parapsychology (علم ما وراء النفس). The text then notes that rigid positivist methodologies hinder the study of these extraordinary phenomena because they apply methods suitable for ordinary perception, failing to capture their multi-disciplinary nature. To address this, a new field called psychotronics (السيكوترونات) has emerged, integrating physics, biology, and psychology. Next, the author shifts to discuss inspiration (الإلهام). From a psychological viewpoint, inspiration is explained as resulting from physiological brain processes and psychological processes, with which the religious explanation agrees. However, the religious explanation adds that Allah is the ultimate disposer of all things, including inspiration—He may guide certain individuals to discover truths as if they suddenly appear in their minds, even outside times of creative thinking. Finally, psychologists describe the process of inspiration through steps: (1) leaving the problem temporarily (incubation), (2) allowing the mind to rest, and (3) organizing information to discover new relationships, leading to a solution.

Key Terms
علم ما وراء النفس
التلباثي
الإلهام

Key Takeaway
Telepathy and inspiration are presented as real phenomena that positivist methods fail to capture, but their validity as knowledge sources ultimately depends on one's epistemological framework.

Distinguishing Prophetic and Non-Prophetic True Dreams
Author distinguishes two levels of true dreams: prophetic as wahy, believers as part of nubuwwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any reported true dream as either prophetic revelation (wahy) or a part of prophethood (ajza' al-nubuwwa) based on the criteria given in the passage.

Core Idea
Prophetic dreams are revelation; believers' dreams are a part of prophethood, not revelation.

In this passage, the author systematically distinguishes two levels of true dreams, elevating prophetic dreams to the status of primary sources due to their direct link to revelation. Understanding this classification is essential for properly situating the epistemological weight of dreams in Islamic thought and for avoiding the error of treating all dreams as equally authoritative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two levels
The passage explicitly divides true dreams into two levels: the level of prophets and the level of ordinary believing Muslims. Locate the sentences that state this division.
The division is introduced at the beginning: 'There are levels of the dream: the level of the dream regarding the prophets... and the level of the dream regarding the generality of truthful believers.'
2
Extract the criterion for prophetic dreams
What makes prophetic dreams unique? The passage says they 'do not exit the circle of revelation' and are 'derived directly from revelation.' Also, a footnote explains that prophetic dreams are 'protected from error' unlike others that may be influenced by Satan.
The criterion is direct derivation from wahi and immunity from satanic interference.
3
Note the evidence given
The passage provides Quranic examples: Joseph's dream, Abraham's dream, the dream at Hudaybiyyah, and the hadith of Aisha about the Prophet's dreams being 'like the break of dawn.' These serve as proof for the first level.
For the second level, the passage mentions authentic hadiths but notes no Quranic reference.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a description of a dream, determine whether it belongs to the first or second level based on the criteria: is the dreamer a prophet? Is the dream claimed to be direct revelation?
Only prophetic dreams carry the weight of wahi; believers' dreams are 'a part of prophethood' but not revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستويات الرؤيا الصادقة

هناك مستويات من الرؤيا: مستوى الرؤيا في حق الأنبياء - عليهم السلام- ومستوى الرؤيا في حق عموم المؤمنين الصادقين. وذلك يستلزم إيضاحاً على النحو التالي:

المستوى الأول: مستوى الرؤيا في حق الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - وهذا المستوى يرقى إلى مستوى المصادر الرئيسة، إذ إنه لا يمثل مجرد مصدر ثانوي للمعرفة لكونه مستمداً من الوحي مباشرة، فرؤيا الأنبياء لا تخرج عن دائرة الوحي(1).

ومن ذلك - على سبيل المثال ما جاء في قصة يوسف- عليه السلام- وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿قالَ يُوسُفُ لأَبِيهِ يا أَبَتِ إِنِّي رَأَيْتُ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ كَوْكَبَاً وَالشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ رَأَيْتُهُمْ لِي سَاجِدِينَ ﴾ [يوسف: ٤/١٢].

وبعد نهاية محنة يوسف قال لأبيه: ﴿وَقالَ يا أَبَتِ هَذا تَأْوِيلُ رُؤْيَايَ [يوسف: ١٠٠/١٢].

وقال يوسف أيضاً في سياق تفسيره لرؤيا السجينين إن تفسيره من وحي الله له: قالَ لا يَأْتِيكُما طَعَامٌ تُرْزَقَانِهِ إِلا نَبَّأتكما بتأويلِهِ قَبْلَ أَن يَأْتِيَكُما ذَلِكُما مِمَّا عَلْمَنِي رَبِّي... [يوسف: ٣٧/١٢].

(۱) فهم بعض المفسرين من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَما كان لِبَشَرٍ أَن يُكَلَّمَهُ اللهُ إِلَّا وَحْيَا أَوْ مِنْ وَرَاءِ حِجاب [الشورى: ٥١/٤٢] أن ذلك في المنام، إذ رؤيا الأنبياء حق بخلاف غيرهم، لأنها وحي، والوحي لا يدخله خلل لأنه محروس، أما رؤيا غيرهم فقد يحضرها الشيطان. انظر: فتح الباري لابن حجر، ٣٥٤/١٢.

ومن ذلك رؤيا إبراهيم عليه السلام- التي بموجبها امتثل لأمر اللـه (الوحي)، وكاد أن ينفذ الذبح في ولده إسماعيل لولا النداء الذي جاءه بالكف ذلك مادام أنه قد صدق الرؤيا (الوحي): ﴿وَنَادَيْنَاهُ أَنْ يا إِبْرَاهِيمُ قَدْ صَدَّقْتَ الرُّؤْيا إِنَّا كَذَلِكَ نَجْزِي الْمُحْسِنِينَ عن الصافات: ١٠٤/٣٧-١٠٥].

وفي الحديبية أنزل تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ صَدَقَ اللَّهُ رَسُولَهُ الرُّؤْيا بِالْحَقِّ لَتَدْخُلُنَّ الْمَسْجِدَ الْحَرَامَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ آمِنِينَ مُحَلِّقِينَ رُؤُوسَكُمْ وَمُقَصِّرِينَ لا تَخَافُونَ فَعَلِمَ مَا لَمْ تَعْلَمُوا فَجَعَلَ مِنْ دُونِ ذَلِكَ فَتحاً قَرِيباً ﴾ [الفتح: ٢٧/٤٨).

وعن عائشة: إن أول ما بدئ به رسول الله - ل - من الوحي الرؤيا الصادقة في النوم، فكان لا يرى رؤيا إلا جاءته مثل فلق الصبح»). إن هذا المستوى من الرؤيا يمثل وحياً حقيقياً بالنسبة إلى التربية الإسلامية، ومصدرية تمثل جزءاً من مصدرية الوحي بلا فرق. وهنا يأتي دور المنهج في بلورة مضامين هذا المصدر والتأكيد على أهميته، وعلو مرتبته، وتميزه الحقيقي عن المستوى الثاني.

المستوى الثاني: الرؤيا الصادقة في حق عموم المؤمنين وهذا المستوى من الرؤيا هو الذي وردت به الأحاديث الصحيحة مصرحة باعتباره من أجزاء النبوة - على اختلاف بين ألفاظ الروايات، ولكننا لا نعثر في الآيات القرآنية على إشارة إليه ألبتة (۳). أما تلك الأحاديث الواردة بألفاظ مختلفة فمنها :
Levels of the true dream: There are levels of the dream: the level of the dream regarding the prophets - peace be upon them - and the level of the dream regarding the generality of truthful believers. This requires clarification as follows:

The first level: the level of the dream regarding the prophets - peace be upon them - and this level rises to the level of the primary sources, for it does not merely represent a secondary source of knowledge because it is derived directly from revelation (wahi). Thus, the dreams of the prophets do not exit the circle of revelation (1).

One example of that is what came in the story of Joseph - peace be upon him - namely the statement of the Most High: 'Joseph said to his father: O my father, indeed I have seen eleven stars and the sun and the moon; I saw them prostrating to me' [Yusuf 12:4].

And after the end of Joseph's ordeal, he said to his father: 'He said: O my father, this is the interpretation of my dream' [Yusuf 12:100].

And Joseph also said, in the context of his interpretation of the dream of the two prisoners, that his interpretation is from God's revelation to him: 'He said: There will not come to you any provision which you are granted except that I will inform you of its interpretation before it comes to you. That is from what my Lord has taught me...' [Yusuf 12:37].

(1) Some exegetes understood from the statement of the Most High: 'And it is not for any human being that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a partition' [Ash-Shura 42:51] that that occurs in sleep. For the dreams of the prophets are true, unlike others, because they are revelation, and revelation is not subject to flaw because it is guarded. As for the dreams of others, Satan may attend them. See: Fath al-Bari by Ibn Hajar, 12/354.

And among that is the dream of Abraham - peace be upon him - by virtue of which he complied with the command of God (the revelation), and he almost carried out the slaughter of his son Ishmael, were it not for the call that came to him to desist, because he had indeed confirmed the dream (the revelation): 'And We called him: O Abraham, you have confirmed the vision. Indeed, We thus reward the doers of good' [As-Saffat 37:104-105].

And at Hudaybiyyah, the Most High revealed: 'Indeed, Allah has fulfilled for His Messenger the vision in truth. You will surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if Allah wills, in safety, with your heads shaved and [hair] shortened, not fearing. But He knew what you did not know and has arranged before that a victory near at hand' [Al-Fath 48:27].

And from Aisha: 'Indeed, the first thing that began with the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, from revelation was the true dream in sleep, so he would never see a dream except that it came like the break of dawn.' That level of dream represents real revelation for Islamic education, and its source represents a part of the source of revelation without distinction. And here comes the role of the methodology in crystallizing the contents of this source and emphasizing its importance, its high status, and its real distinction from the second level.

The second level: the true dream regarding the generality of believers. And this level of dream is that which is reported in authentic hadiths explicitly stating that it is part of prophethood - despite differences in the wording of the narrations - but we do not find in the Quranic verses any reference to it at all (3). As for those hadiths reported with different wordings, among them are:
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological classification of true dreams (ru'ya sadiqa) as a subsidiary source of knowledge. The author first states that there are two levels: one for prophets and one for ordinary believers. The first level, prophetic dreams, is elevated to the status of primary sources because it is directly derived from revelation (wahi). The author supports this with examples from the Quran: Joseph's dream of the eleven stars, his later interpretation of his own dream, and his explanation of the prisoners' dreams as being from God's teaching. A footnote clarifies that some exegetes interpret the verse about God speaking only through revelation or behind a partition as referring to prophetic dreams, which are true and protected from error, unlike the dreams of others which may be influenced by Satan. The author also cites Abraham's dream that led to the command to sacrifice his son, and the dream at Hudaybiyyah that predicted the conquest of Mecca. The hadith of Aisha about the Prophet's true dreams beginning with 'like the break of dawn' is also given. The author concludes that this level of dream is true revelation for Islamic education and is part of the source of revelation without difference. The second level concerns the true dreams of ordinary believers. These are attested to in authentic hadiths as being a part of prophethood, though the author notes that the Quran does not mention this level. The passage thus establishes a clear epistemological hierarchy: prophetic dreams are wahy (revelation), while believers' dreams are a part of nubuwwa (prophethood) but not revelation. The author emphasizes the importance of methodology in understanding and utilizing this source.

وحي
أجزاء النبوة

The Three Types of Dreams in Islamic Tradition
Classification of dreams and their sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the three types of dreams (divine, psychological, satanic) according to al-Harawi's terminology and distinguish ru'yā from other dreams.

Core Idea
Dreams are tripartite: divine, psychological, and satanic; only the first is part of prophethood.

In this passage, the author presents a clear taxonomy of dreams based on their origin and purpose. This classification, attributed to al-Harawi, helps distinguish between the righteous dream that is a part of prophethood, dreams arising from daytime concerns, and terrifying dreams from Satan. Understanding this classification is essential for correct dream interpretation in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the passage, list the three categories: righteous/divine (al-nawʿ al-raḥmānī), psychological (al-nawʿ al-nafsānī), and satanic (al-nawʿ al-shayṭānī). Note the distinct origin and effect of each.
The first is from Allah, the second from the soul's daytime concerns, the third from Satan to cause sadness.
2
Examine the distinguishing features
For each type, identify the source (Allah, self, Satan) and purpose (guidance, reflection, terrify). Notice that only the first is called ru'yā in the Quran; the others are aḍghāth aḥlām.
3
Relate to dream interpretation criteria
Look at Ibn Sirin's method: a true dream has coherent, consistent meaning; a confused dream carries various meanings. This matches the classification: true dreams are from the intellectual soul, confused dreams from memory.
This step connects the classification to the practice of ta'bir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول: رؤيا صالحة، والتي هي جزء من ستة وأربعين جزءاً من النبوة. وقد أطلق عليها الهروي النوع الرحماني والذي هو من أسباب الهداية. النوع الثاني: هموم اليقظة التي تتحول إلى رؤيا منامية، وأطلق عليها الهروي النوع النفساني. النوع الثالث: رؤيا تهويلية من الشيطان لتحزين الإنسان. وأطلق عليها الهروي النوع الشيطاني. إن هذا التقسيم يجلي لنا الفرق بين الرؤيا - كما يعبر عنها القرآن الكريم - والأحلام التي يعبر عنها علماء النفس المحدثون، وفي مقدمتهم (فرويد) حينما يعدون الأحلام مجرد طريقة رمزية للتعبير عن دوافع الإنسان اللاشعورية. أما الرؤيا في القرآن فلا ترد إلا بالمعنى الأول من غير إغفال للنوعين الثاني (النفساني) والثالث (الشيطاني) بل يعبر عن هذين النوعين بـ (أضغاث الأحلام). وهذا ما نجد النص عليه في قول الله تعالى - على لسان حاشية ملك مصر: قالُوا أَضْغَاثُ أَحْلامٍ وَمَا نَحْنُ بِتَأْوِيلِ الأَحْلامِ بِعالِمِينَ) [يوسف: ٤٤/١٢]. وقوله سبحانه على لسان أعداء النبي - - : بَلْ قَالُوا أَضْغَاثُ أَحْلامٍ بَلِ افْتَراهُ بَلْ هُوَ شَاعِرٌ ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٥/٢١]. والضغث لغة التباس الشيء بعضه ببعض، ومنه قيل للأحلام الملتبسة أضغاث. وأصلها في القرآن مأخوذ من الضغث وهو قبضة ريحان أو حشيش أو قضبان، وجمعه أضغاث. قال : وخذ بيدك ضغثا و به شبه الأحلام المختلطة التي لا يتبين حقائقها وقالوا أضغاث أحلام : حزم أخلاط من الأحلام. ويقرر ابن خلدون (ت: ۸۰۸ هـ) أن تعبير الرؤيا علم من العلوم الشرعية، وقال: ومن هذا التقرير يظهر لك الفرق بين الرؤيا الصادقة وأضغاث الأحلام الكاذبة، فإنها كلّها صور في الخيال لحالة النوم. لكن إن كانت تلك الصور متنزلة من الروح العقلي المدرك فهي رؤيا، وإن كانت مأخوذة من الصور التي في الحافظة، التي كان الخيال أودعها إياها منذ اليقظة فهي أضغاث الأحلام. وإلى هذا المعنى أشار محمد بن سیرین (ت: ۱۱۰ هـ) حين قال: وينبغي للمعبر أن يفهم كلام صاحب الرؤيا ويعرضها على الأصول فإن كان كلاماً صحيحاً يشبه بعضه بعضا ويدل على معان مستقيمة فهي الرؤيا الصحيحة، وإن كان يحمل على معان مختلفة نظر إلى ما هو أولى بألفاظها وأقرب إلى الأصل.
The first type: a righteous dream, which is a part of forty-six parts of prophethood. Al-Harawi called it the divine type, which is one of the means of guidance. The second type: the concerns of wakefulness that turn into a dream in sleep, and al-Harawi called it the psychological type. The third type: a terrifying dream from Satan to sadden a person, and al-Harawi called it the satanic type. This classification clarifies for us the difference between ru'yā (as expressed in the noble Quran) and dreams as expressed by modern psychologists, foremost among them (Freud), when they consider dreams merely a symbolic method of expressing a person's unconscious motives. As for ru'yā in the Quran, it is only used in the first meaning, without neglecting the second (psychological) and third (satanic) types, but rather these two types are expressed as 'aḍghāth aḥlām' (confused dreams). This is what we find stated in the saying of Allah Almighty - on the tongue of the retinue of the king of Egypt: 'They said: Confused dreams, and we are not knowledgeable of the interpretation of dreams' [Yusuf 12:44]. And His saying, exalted, on the tongue of the enemies of the Prophet -: 'But they say: Confused dreams, rather he has fabricated it, rather he is a poet' [Al-Anbiya 21:5]. Al-ḍaghth linguistically means the intermingling of things with each other, and from this, confused dreams are called aḍghāth. Its origin in the Quran is taken from al-ḍaghth, which is a handful of basil, grass, or twigs, and its plural is aḍghāth. He said: 'And take in your hand a handful' [38:44] and with it mixed dreams whose realities are not discerned are likened. And they said: 'Aḍghāth aḥlām: bundles of mixtures of dreams.' Ibn Khaldun (d. 808 AH) affirms that dream interpretation is a science among the religious sciences, and said: From this exposition, the difference between truthful dreams and false confused dreams becomes clear to you, for all of them are images in the imagination during the state of sleep. But if those images descend from the intellectual soul that perceives, then it is a ru'yā; and if they are taken from the images in the memory, which the imagination had deposited in it since wakefulness, then they are aḍghāth aḥlām. Muhammad ibn Sirin (d. 110 AH) indicated this meaning when he said: The interpreter must understand the speech of the dreamer and present it to the principles. If it is correct speech, resembling each other, and indicates upright meanings, then it is the correct ru'yā; but if it carries various meanings, he looks to what is most appropriate for its expressions and closest to the principle.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies dreams (ru'yā) into three types based on their origin, drawing from al-Harawi's terminology. It then contrasts the Quranic concept of ru'yā with modern psychological views, particularly Freud's, and introduces the Quranic term 'aḍghāth aḥlām' (confused dreams) for the second and third types. The passage proceeds to explain the linguistic meaning of 'ḍaghth' and cites Quranic verses. It then quotes Ibn Khaldun and Ibn Sirin to elaborate on the epistemological distinction between true dreams and confused ones. The author follows a clear classification: first type (divine/righteous), second type (psychological/daytime concerns), third type (satanic). All examples are from Islamic sources: Quranic verses, hadith (implicitly from the forty-sixth part of prophethood), and statements by classical scholars. The commentary emphasizes that the Quran uses ru'yā only for truthful dreams, while the other types are termed 'aḍghāth aḥlām'.

Understanding 'Aḍghāth Aḥlām' in the Quran
Explains the term 'aḍghāth aḥlām' and its Quranic usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the linguistic meaning of 'aḍghāth aḥlām', identify its Quranic contexts, and apply Ibn Khaldun's criterion to distinguish true dreams from confused ones.

Core Idea
'Aḍghāth aḥlām' means confused dreams, used in the Quran for non-divine dreams arising from the soul or Satan.

The passage introduces the term 'aḍghāth aḥlām' as the Quranic designation for dreams that are not righteous. It provides the linguistic root (ḍaghth) meaning intermingling, and cites Ibn Khaldun's epistemological distinction. This lesson unpacks the meaning and significance of this term.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic verses
Find the two verses cited: Yusuf 12:44 and Al-Anbiya 21:5. In both, the term 'aḍghāth aḥlām' is used to dismiss dreams as confused and unreliable.
In Yusuf, the courtiers say the king's dream is aḍghāth aḥlām; in Al-Anbiya, opponents call the Prophet's message aḍghāth aḥlām.
2
Analyze the linguistic meaning
The passage explains that ḍaghth means a handful of mixed plants (basil, grass, twigs), implying confusion and mixture. Thus 'aḍghāth aḥlām' literally means 'mixed, tangled dreams'.
The figurative sense is that such dreams lack clear, coherent meaning.
3
Apply Ibn Khaldun's epistemological criterion
Ibn Khaldun distinguishes true dreams (from the intellectual soul) from confused dreams (from memory of daytime images). Use this to test: if a dream's images are derived from recent experiences, it is aḍghāth aḥlām.
This criterion helps in practical dream interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول: رؤيا صالحة، والتي هي جزء من ستة وأربعين جزءاً من النبوة. وقد أطلق عليها الهروي النوع الرحماني والذي هو من أسباب الهداية. النوع الثاني: هموم اليقظة التي تتحول إلى رؤيا منامية، وأطلق عليها الهروي النوع النفساني. النوع الثالث: رؤيا تهويلية من الشيطان لتحزين الإنسان. وأطلق عليها الهروي النوع الشيطاني. إن هذا التقسيم يجلي لنا الفرق بين الرؤيا - كما يعبر عنها القرآن الكريم - والأحلام التي يعبر عنها علماء النفس المحدثون، وفي مقدمتهم (فرويد) حينما يعدون الأحلام مجرد طريقة رمزية للتعبير عن دوافع الإنسان اللاشعورية. أما الرؤيا في القرآن فلا ترد إلا بالمعنى الأول من غير إغفال للنوعين الثاني (النفساني) والثالث (الشيطاني) بل يعبر عن هذين النوعين بـ (أضغاث الأحلام). وهذا ما نجد النص عليه في قول الله تعالى - على لسان حاشية ملك مصر: قالُوا أَضْغَاثُ أَحْلامٍ وَمَا نَحْنُ بِتَأْوِيلِ الأَحْلامِ بِعالِمِينَ) [يوسف: ٤٤/١٢]. وقوله سبحانه على لسان أعداء النبي - - : بَلْ قَالُوا أَضْغَاثُ أَحْلامٍ بَلِ افْتَراهُ بَلْ هُوَ شَاعِرٌ ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٥/٢١]. والضغث لغة التباس الشيء بعضه ببعض، ومنه قيل للأحلام الملتبسة أضغاث. وأصلها في القرآن مأخوذ من الضغث وهو قبضة ريحان أو حشيش أو قضبان، وجمعه أضغاث. قال : وخذ بيدك ضغثا و به شبه الأحلام المختلطة التي لا يتبين حقائقها وقالوا أضغاث أحلام : حزم أخلاط من الأحلام. ويقرر ابن خلدون (ت: ۸۰۸ هـ) أن تعبير الرؤيا علم من العلوم الشرعية، وقال: ومن هذا التقرير يظهر لك الفرق بين الرؤيا الصادقة وأضغاث الأحلام الكاذبة، فإنها كلّها صور في الخيال لحالة النوم. لكن إن كانت تلك الصور متنزلة من الروح العقلي المدرك فهي رؤيا، وإن كانت مأخوذة من الصور التي في الحافظة، التي كان الخيال أودعها إياها منذ اليقظة فهي أضغاث الأحلام. وإلى هذا المعنى أشار محمد بن سیرین (ت: ۱۱۰ هـ) حين قال: وينبغي للمعبر أن يفهم كلام صاحب الرؤيا ويعرضها على الأصول فإن كان كلاماً صحيحاً يشبه بعضه بعضا ويدل على معان مستقيمة فهي الرؤيا الصحيحة، وإن كان يحمل على معان مختلفة نظر إلى ما هو أولى بألفاظها وأقرب إلى الأصل.
The first type: a righteous dream, which is a part of forty-six parts of prophethood. Al-Harawi called it the divine type, which is one of the means of guidance. The second type: the concerns of wakefulness that turn into a dream in sleep, and al-Harawi called it the psychological type. The third type: a terrifying dream from Satan to sadden a person, and al-Harawi called it the satanic type. This classification clarifies for us the difference between ru'yā (as expressed in the noble Quran) and dreams as expressed by modern psychologists, foremost among them (Freud), when they consider dreams merely a symbolic method of expressing a person's unconscious motives. As for ru'yā in the Quran, it is only used in the first meaning, without neglecting the second (psychological) and third (satanic) types, but rather these two types are expressed as 'aḍghāth aḥlām' (confused dreams). This is what we find stated in the saying of Allah Almighty - on the tongue of the retinue of the king of Egypt: 'They said: Confused dreams, and we are not knowledgeable of the interpretation of dreams' [Yusuf 12:44]. And His saying, exalted, on the tongue of the enemies of the Prophet -: 'But they say: Confused dreams, rather he has fabricated it, rather he is a poet' [Al-Anbiya 21:5]. Al-ḍaghth linguistically means the intermingling of things with each other, and from this, confused dreams are called aḍghāth. Its origin in the Quran is taken from al-ḍaghth, which is a handful of basil, grass, or twigs, and its plural is aḍghāth. He said: 'And take in your hand a handful' [38:44] and with it mixed dreams whose realities are not discerned are likened. And they said: 'Aḍghāth aḥlām: bundles of mixtures of dreams.' Ibn Khaldun (d. 808 AH) affirms that dream interpretation is a science among the religious sciences, and said: From this exposition, the difference between truthful dreams and false confused dreams becomes clear to you, for all of them are images in the imagination during the state of sleep. But if those images descend from the intellectual soul that perceives, then it is a ru'yā; and if they are taken from the images in the memory, which the imagination had deposited in it since wakefulness, then they are aḍghāth aḥlām. Muhammad ibn Sirin (d. 110 AH) indicated this meaning when he said: The interpreter must understand the speech of the dreamer and present it to the principles. If it is correct speech, resembling each other, and indicates upright meanings, then it is the correct ru'yā; but if it carries various meanings, he looks to what is most appropriate for its expressions and closest to the principle.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies dreams (ru'yā) into three types based on their origin, drawing from al-Harawi's terminology. It then contrasts the Quranic concept of ru'yā with modern psychological views, particularly Freud's, and introduces the Quranic term 'aḍghāth aḥlām' (confused dreams) for the second and third types. The passage proceeds to explain the linguistic meaning of 'ḍaghth' and cites Quranic verses. It then quotes Ibn Khaldun and Ibn Sirin to elaborate on the epistemological distinction between true dreams and confused ones. The author follows a clear classification: first type (divine/righteous), second type (psychological/daytime concerns), third type (satanic). All examples are from Islamic sources: Quranic verses, hadith (implicitly from the forty-sixth part of prophethood), and statements by classical scholars. The commentary emphasizes that the Quran uses ru'yā only for truthful dreams, while the other types are termed 'aḍghāth aḥlām'.

Resolving Apparent Contradiction in Dream Classifications
Author resolves apparent contradiction between dream classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconcile the tripartite and bipartite classifications of dreams by identifying how the bipartite is a summary.

Core Idea
Classifications are not contradictory but complementary; one summarizes the other.

In this passage, the author addresses a confusion: earlier three types of dreams were mentioned, but here only two are given. You might think there is a contradiction. The author explains that the bipartite division is a simplification: it combines two of the three types into one category. Understanding this resolution will help you see how dream typologies in Islamic tradition are coherent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two classifications
First, note the tripartite classification (true dream, from Shaytan, from waking worries) and the bipartite classification (true dream, confused dreams). The passage presents both.
The tripartite is implied from earlier context; the bipartite is stated explicitly.
2
Locate the apparent contradiction
A reader may think the author contradicts himself by listing three types then reducing to two. The passage begins by acknowledging this confusion.
3
Extract the author's resolution
The author says the bipartite is a summary: the two sub-types (whispers from Shaytan and waking worries) are both confused dreams. So the bipartite actually encompasses the tripartite.
Bipartite: true dream + confused dreams (which includes two subcategories).
4
Validate with hadith
The author supports his resolution with prophetic hadith distinguishing true dreams from Shaytan's dreams. This reinforces the bipartite classification as a practical summary.
Hadith: 'The true dream is from God, the bad dream from Shaytan.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيحملها عليه، وإن كانت الرؤيا كلّها مختلفة لا تلتئم على الأصول فهي أضغاث أحلام. ربِّ قائل يشتبه عليه الأمر فيتخيّل تعارضا بين ما تقدم من أنواع الرؤيا وأنها ثلاثة وبين هذا التقسيم الثنائي الذي أجملها في قسمين: رؤيا صادقة وأضغاث الأحلام. والحق أن لا تعارض بين التقسيمين، فبوسعنا تفصيل الأمر فنقول: إن الرؤيا من حيث مؤدّاها ونتائجها قسمان :رئیسان رؤيا صالحة من الله، وأحلام تشمل قسمين فرعيين أحدهما وساوس وخواطر من الشيطان والآخر هموم يعانيها المرء في يقظته فيجدها في منامه وكلا هذين النوعين غير الرؤيا الصادقة فهما أضغاث أحلام. ويسند هذا الاستخلاص للتوفيق بين التقسيمات السابقة قوله - - الرؤيا الصالحة من الله والحلم من الشيطان فإذا حلم أحدكم فليتعوذ منه، وليبصق عن شماله فإنها لن تضره. وقوله: من رآني في النوم فقد رآني، إنه لا ينبغي للشيطان أن يتمثل في صورتي. وقال إذا حَلَم أحدكم فلا يخبر بتلعب الشيطان به في المنام.
And he interprets it, and if the entire dream is disparate and does not align with the principles, then it is a confused dream. O someone who finds the matter confusing and imagines a contradiction between what has been mentioned of the types of dreams—that they are three—and this bipartite division that summarized them into two parts: a true dream and confused dreams. The truth is that there is no contradiction between the two divisions, for we can detail the matter and say: Dreams, in terms of their outcomes and results, are two main types: a good dream from God, and dreams that include two subcategories: one is whisperings and thoughts from the Devil, and the other is worries that a person experiences in his wakefulness and finds in his sleep. Both of these types are distinct from the true dream, so they are confused dreams. This conclusion is supported to reconcile the previous divisions by his saying: 'The true dream is from God, and the bad dream is from the Devil; if one of you has a bad dream, let him seek refuge from it and spit to his left, for it will not harm him.' And his saying: 'Whoever sees me in a dream has indeed seen me, for the Devil cannot impersonate my form.' And he said: 'If one of you has a bad dream, let him not tell of the Devil's play with him in the dream.'
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage addresses an apparent contradiction in dream classifications. Earlier, the author mentioned three types of dreams, but here presents a bipartite division into true dreams and confused dreams (adghath ahlam). The author argues there is no contradiction because the bipartite division is a summary: the two subcategories (whispers from Shaytan and waking worries) are both classified as confused dreams. This is supported by prophetic hadith that distinguish dreams from God and from Shaytan. The passage thus clarifies that the two classifications are compatible, with the tripartite being a detailed version of the bipartite.

The Epistemic Status of Dreams in Islam
Author argues that dreams are a secondary source of knowledge but not a binding legal proof except for prophets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prophetic and non-prophetic dreams in terms of their probative value and explain why only prophetic dreams constitute a binding legal proof (hujja).

Core Idea
Dreams are a secondary source, not hujja—except for prophets, whose dreams are revelation.

This passage addresses a common confusion: the tendency to treat dreams as authoritative. The author clarifies that dreams, along with intuition and inspiration, are secondary sources that require corroboration from primary sources. The sole exception is prophetic dreams, which are a form of wahy. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for avoiding erroneous reliance on dreams in religious matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis
What is the author's main claim about dreams? He asserts that dreams are a secondary source of knowledge, not a binding legal proof for non-prophets.
2
Locate the exception
What is the only type of dream that carries binding authority? Prophetic dreams, because they are considered a form of revelation (wahy).
3
Analyze the reasoning for the exception
Why are prophetic dreams treated differently? Prophets are infallible, so their dreams are protected from devilish interference, unlike those of non-prophets.
The author quotes Ibn Hajar and al-Khattabi to support this.
4
Connect to Islamic epistemology
How does this classification relate to the hierarchy of sources in Islam? Primary sources (Quran, Sunnah) are binding; secondary sources like dreams need external support from primary sources.
5
Evaluate the practical implication
How should Muslims treat their own dreams? Not as proof, but as personal guidance only if consistent with primary sources. They cannot be used to derive religious rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موقف الإسلام من الرؤيا لا ريب أن موقف الإسلام يمكن أن يستشف من خلال النصوص سالفة الذكر. وحسب تصوري فإنها قد أثبتت الرؤيا مصدراً ثانوياً للمعرفة وفق التفصيل الذي تقدّم. بيد أن ثمة إشكالاً عادة ما يثار عند الحديث عن الرؤيا، ولا سيما حينما يرفع بعض المولعين بترديد وادّعاء تعبير كل ما يراه النائم أهمية الرؤيا حتى ليخيل إلى المرء أنها تكاد تصنف ضمن المصادر الرئيسة من حيث إلزام نتائجها وما تؤول إليه تعبيراتها. ويتلخص هذا الإشكال في التساؤل حول إمكان التمييز بين ما هو رؤيا صادقة وبين أضغاث الأحلام؟ وغالب الظن أن مبعث هذا التساؤل تصورهم أن ثمة إلزاماً أو حجة فيما يطلق عليه الرؤيا الصادقة. وهنا أجدني مضطراً إلى التأكيد على ما سبق أن أشرت إليه قبلاً مـن أن هذه المصادر سواء كانت حدساً أم إلهاماً أم رؤيا صادقة لا تمتلك في حد ذاتها حجة شرعية عامة ملزمة، إذ لابد أن تجد لها سنداً من المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة. وما يستثنى من ذلك لا يعدو المستوى الأول من الرؤيا الصادقة وهو رؤيا الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - وهو ما يعد صورة من صور الوحي المعتبرة. وفي هذا قال ابن حجر (ت: ٨٥٢ هـ ) - نقلاً عن أهل التفسير - ورؤيا الأنبياء وحي غير الأنبياء، فإنها قد يحضرها الشيطان. بخلاف غيرهم، فالوحي لا يدخله خلل لأنه محروس، بخلاف رؤيا كان على ذلك يؤكد الخطابي (ت: ۳۸۸هـ) أن الرؤيا - أي رؤيا - لا تمتلك حجة النبوة فتكون جزءاً من أجزائها إلا في حق الأنبياء دون غيرهم، لأن الأنبياء يوحى إليهم في منامهم كما يوحى إليهم في اليقظة. وبعض العلماء يعد مرائي الصالحين نوعًا من الإلهام، ولكنها ليست بحجة في الدين لأن غير المعصوم لا يوثق بصحة خواطره، إذ هو عرضة لوساوس الشيطان فليس معصوماً. والسؤال الذي يفترض طرحه هنا : لماذا غدت أكثر رؤى الناس اليوم من قبيل أضغاث الأحلام، بحيث إن حجيتها منتفية تماماً إذا كانت كذلك بالفعل؟
The position of Islam on dreams: There is no doubt that the position of Islam can be inferred from the aforementioned texts. In my view, they have established the dream as a secondary source of knowledge according to the detail mentioned previously. However, there is usually a problem raised when discussing dreams, especially when some enthusiasts exaggerate its importance by repeating and claiming to interpret every dream, to the point that one might think it is nearly classified among the primary sources in terms of the binding nature of its results and the interpretations that follow. This problem is summarized in the question: How can one distinguish between a true dream and confused dreams? Most likely, the reason for this question is their assumption that there is some binding proof or argument in what is called a true dream. Here I find myself compelled to emphasize what I previously indicated: that these sources—whether intuition (hads), inspiration (ilham), or true dream—do not possess in themselves a general binding legal proof (hujja shar'iyya). Rather, they must find support from the primary sources and methods. The only exception to that is the first level of true dreams, which is the dreams of the prophets—peace be upon them—which is considered a form of revealed inspiration (wahy). In this regard, Ibn Hajar (d. 852 AH) reported from the exegetes: 'The dreams of the prophets are revelation; the dreams of others are not, for the devil can attend them.' Unlike others, revelation has no defect because it is guarded. Similarly, al-Khattabi (d. 388 AH) affirmed that a dream—any dream—does not possess the proof of prophethood so as to be part of its parts except in the case of prophets exclusively, because prophets receive revelation in their sleep just as they receive it while awake. And some scholars consider the visions of the righteous as a type of inspiration, but they are not a proof in religion because a non-infallible person cannot be trusted about the correctness of his thoughts, as he is susceptible to the whispers of the devil and is not infallible. And the question that should be raised here is: Why have most of people's dreams today become of the type of confused dreams, such that their probative value is completely negated if they are indeed so?
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological status of dreams in Islam. The author establishes that dreams are a secondary source of knowledge, not primary. The main issue raised is that some people treat dreams as authoritative, believing true dreams carry binding proof. The author refutes this by stressing that neither intuition, inspiration, nor true dreams possess a general binding legal proof (hujja shar'iyya); they must be corroborated by primary sources like the Qur'an and Sunnah. The only exception is the dreams of prophets, which are a form of revelation and thus infallible. This is supported by quotes from Ibn Hajar and al-Khattabi, indicating that non-prophets' dreams are susceptible to devilish interference. Therefore, dreams cannot be used as a basis for religious rulings.

Key Terms
حجة شرعية
مصدر ثانوي
وحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not from God.
B) Because they are subject to devilish interference and lack infallibility.
C) Because they are always confused dreams.
D) Because they contradict the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Only prophetic dreams are hujja; all other dreams are secondary and require confirmation from primary sources.
