Dreams as a Secondary Source: The Need for Evidence
Author distinguishes prophetic dreams as authoritative revelation from ordinary dreams as non-binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological status of dreams as a secondary source and explain why non-prophetic dreams require support from primary sources to be considered evidence.

Core Idea
Dreams are not autonomous; they need a foundation in primary revelation.

The author establishes that dreams, like intuition and inspiration, are secondary sources. Many people mistake vivid dreams as binding, but the author clarifies that only prophetic dreams are automatically authoritative. The rest must be validated by the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about secondary sources
The author states that sources like dreams do not inherently possess a binding shar‘i proof. Find the exact phrase: 'لا تمتلك في حد ذاتها حجة شرعية عامة ملزمة'.
This is the core epistemological position.
2
Understand the exception: prophetic dreams
The author notes that the only exception is the dreams of prophets, which are a form of revelation. Note the quote from Ibn Hajar: 'رؤيا الأنبياء وحي'.
3
Grasp the logical implication
If a dream (non-prophetic) is not supported by a primary source (Quran, Sunnah, consensus), it carries no legal authority, regardless of the dreamer's piety.
4
Apply to a hypothetical case
Suppose someone claims a dream suggests a new ruling. Using the author's criteria, ask: Is the dreamer a prophet? If no, does the dream align with established primary sources? If not, the claim is invalid.
This mirrors usul al-fiqh methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الرابع - المصادر التبعية

الذكر. وحسب تصوري فإنها قد أثبتت الرؤيا مصدراً ثانوياً للمعرفة وفق التفصيل الذي تقدّم. بيد أن ثمة إشكالاً عادة ما يثار عند الحديث عن الرؤيا، ولا سيما حينما يرفع بعض المولعين بترديد وادّعاء تعبير كل ما يراه النائم أهمية الرؤيا حتى ليخيل إلى المرء أنها تكاد تصنف ضمن المصادر الرئيسة من حيث إلزام نتائجها وما تؤول إليه تعبيراتها. ويتلخص هذا الإشكال في التساؤل حول إمكان التمييز بين ما هو رؤيا صادقة وبين أضغاث الأحلام؟ وغالب الظن أن مبعث هذا التساؤل تصورهم أن ثمة إلزاماً أو حجة فيما يطلق عليه الرؤيا الصادقة. وهنا أجدني مضطراً إلى التأكيد على ما سبق أن أشرت إليه قبلاً مـن أن هذه المصادر سواء كانت حدساً أم إلهاماً أم رؤيا صادقة لا تمتلك في حد ذاتها حجة شرعية عامة ملزمة، إذ لابد أن تجد لها سنداً من المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة. وما يستثنى من ذلك لا يعدو المستوى الأول من الرؤيا الصادقة وهو رؤيا الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - وهو ما يعد صورة من صور الوحي المعتبرة. وفي هذا قال ابن حجر (ت: ٨٥٢ هـ ) - نقلاً عن أهل التفسير - ورؤيا الأنبياء وحي غير الأنبياء، فإنها قد يحضرها الشيطان))(۱). بخلاف غيرهم، فالوحي لا يدخله خلل لأنه محروس، بخلاف رؤيا كان وعليه ذلك يؤكد الخطابي (ت: ۳۸۸هـ) أن الرؤيا - أي رؤيا - لا تمتلك حجة النبوة فتكون جزءاً من أجزائها إلا في حق الأنبياء دون غيرهم، لأن الأنبياء يوحى إليهم في منامهم كما يوحى إليهم في اليقظة). وبعض العلماء يعد مرائي الصالحين نوعًا من الإلهام، ولكنها ليست بحجة في الدين لأن غير المعصوم لا يوثق بصحة خواطره، إذ هو عرضة لوساوس الشيطان فليس معصوماً (۳). والسؤال الذي يفترض طرحه هنا : لماذا غدت أكثر رؤى الناس اليوم من قبيل أضغاث الأحلام، بحيث إن حجيتها منتفية تماماً إذا كانت كذلك بالفعل؟

ولعل ذلك مؤشر على خلل في الاستقامة لدى الفرد، إذ إن الأصل في رؤيا المؤمن الصالح الصادق أن تكون صادقة صالحة، بصرف النظر عن عدم حجيتها في إلزام الآخرين، أو الخطورة التي قد تلحق لمن جعل جل همه تتبع رؤيا المنام، في حقه أو في حق الآخرين إن ذلك يلقي بأسبابه على المناهج التعليمية وسائر الوسائط التربوية التي يفترض أن تتعاون في تحقيق هدف التربية في إخراج الإنسان الصالح. وهذا الصلاح يعني استقامة في الفكر والسلوك، وصفاء في الشكل والمضمون. وهو الأمر الذي سيؤهل بعد ذلك للإفادة من مصدرية الرؤيا، بحيث تكون صادقة حقاً لا مجرد وهم وتشبث بالمجهول. وهذا يدعو المؤسسات التربوية إلى التركيز على التربية النفسية الوجدانية الإيمانية القائمة على التزكية فهي تكاد تكون في حكم الفريضة الغائبة في الحياة التربوية لدى كل وسائط التربية رسمية وغير رسمية.

الموقف من رؤيا الرسول

ولكن لئن سلمنا بهذا في الرؤية العادية، فكيف الحال إذ رأى المرء الرسول - في منامه، وقد تضافرت نصوص السنة المطهرة على أن الشيطان عاجز عن التمثل بشخصه - عليه الصلاة والسلام -. ومن ذلك ما أخرجه البخاري ومسلم مجتمعين أو تفرد كل منهما بروايته من مثل: ما رواه أبو هريرة عن النبي : من رآني في المنام فسيراني في اليقظة، ولا يتمثل الشيطان بي. قال أبو عبد الله (البخاري) قال ابن سيرين: إذا رآه في صورته) (۱).
Chapter Three, Section Four: Secondary Sources. Remembrance. In my view, it has established the dream as a secondary source of knowledge according to the detail previously mentioned. However, there is a problematic issue usually raised when discussing dreams, especially when some enthusiasts of repeating and claiming to interpret everything the sleeper sees exaggerate the importance of the dream, to the extent that one might imagine it is nearly classified among the primary sources in terms of the binding nature of its results and the interpretations it leads to. This problematic issue is summarized in the question of the possibility of distinguishing between a true dream and confused dreams. The likely reason for this question is their perception that there is a binding force or a proof in what is called a true dream. Here I find myself compelled to emphasize what I previously indicated, that these sources, whether intuition, inspiration, or a true dream, do not in themselves possess a general binding shar‘i proof, for they must find support from the primary sources and methods. The only exception to that is the first level of true dreams, which is the dreams of the prophets (peace be upon them), and it is considered a form of valid revelation. Regarding this, Ibn Hajar (d. 852 AH) said, quoting the exegetes: 'The dream of the prophets is revelation; not so for others, for the devil may attend their dreams.' Unlike others, revelation cannot be corrupted because it is protected, unlike the dream of ... Similarly, Al-Khattabi (d. 388 AH) emphasizes that the dream – any dream – does not possess the proof of prophethood and becomes part of its parts except in the case of prophets, not others, because prophets receive revelation in their sleep as they receive it while awake. Some scholars consider the dreams of the righteous to be a kind of inspiration, but they are not a proof in religion because a non-infallible person is not trusted for the correctness of his thoughts, as he is subject to the whispers of Satan and is not infallible. The question that should be posed here is: why have most people's dreams today become of the type of confused dreams, such that their proof is completely invalid if they are indeed so? Perhaps this is an indicator of a defect in righteousness in the individual, for the original state of the dream of a righteous, truthful believer is that it should be truthful and sound, regardless of its lack of binding proof on others, or the danger that may befall one who makes his whole concern following his dreams, concerning himself or others. That casts its causes on the educational curricula and all other educational media that are supposed to cooperate in achieving the goal of education in producing a righteous human being. This righteousness means rectitude in thought and behavior, and purity in form and content. It is that which will afterward qualify for benefiting from the source-status of the dream, such that it is truly truthful, not mere illusion and clinging to the unknown. This calls educational institutions to focus on psychological, emotional, and faith-based education founded on self-purification, for it is nearly a missing obligation in the educational life of all educational media, formal and informal. The stance on seeing the Prophet. But if we accept this concerning ordinary dreams, what about when a person sees the Prophet in his dream? The texts of the pure Sunnah have concurred that the devil is unable to impersonate his person (peace and blessings be upon him). Among that is what Bukhari and Muslim both narrated, or each alone narrated, such as what Abu Hurairah reported from the Prophet: 'Whoever sees me in a dream will see me in wakefulness, and the devil cannot impersonate me.' Abu Abdullah (Al-Bukhari) said: Ibn Sirin said: 'If he saw him in his form.'
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and critical discussion about dreams as a secondary source of knowledge. The author begins by reaffirming that dreams are a secondary source, but immediately raises a problem: some people elevate dreams to the level of primary sources due to their perceived binding authority. The author questions how to distinguish true dreams from confused dreams (adghath ahlam), noting that people assume true dreams carry a proof (hujja). The author then forcefully argues that these sources—intuition, inspiration, true dreams—do not possess an independent, binding shar‘i proof on their own; they must be supported by primary sources and methods. The only exception is the highest level of true dreams: those of prophets, which are a form of valid revelation (wahy). The author quotes Ibn Hajar and al-Khattabi to affirm that prophetic dreams are revelation and thus authoritative, whereas others' dreams can be influenced by Satan. Even the dreams of the righteous are not binding proofs because non-infallible persons are susceptible to satanic whispers. The author then poses a rhetorical question: why are most dreams today mere confused dreams, void of authority? This is linked to a lack of righteousness, which educational institutions should address through spiritual education. Finally, the author turns to the specific case of dreaming of the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH): based on hadith, the devil cannot impersonate the Prophet in a dream. This makes such dreams trustworthy in content, but still not a general source of legislation. The overall argument maintains that dreams, while potentially meaningful, are not independent sources of knowledge unless they belong to prophets.

Key Terms
الرؤيا
أضغاث الأحلام
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition can a non-prophetic dream be considered a valid source of knowledge?
A) If the dreamer is a righteous scholar
B) If the dream is about the Prophet
C) If the dream is supported by primary sources and methods
D) If the dream comes true

Key Takeaway
Dreams, like other secondary sources, must be validated by primary revelation to be authoritative in Islamic rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Imagine a person claims that a specific charity is mandatory based on a dream. Using the author's criteria, explain why this claim is not automatically valid.
?
Task 2: Find a real example from Islamic history where a dream was cited in a legal context but required supporting evidence (e.g., the dream of the Prophet’s companions like Abu Bakr seeking guidance on the compilation of the Quran; though it was a dream, it was supported by consensus and revelation).

Prophetic Dreams vs Ordinary Dreams: Authority and Exception
Author distinguishes prophetic dreams as authoritative revelation from ordinary dreams as non-binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prophetic dreams (authoritative revelation) and ordinary dreams (non-authoritative) and explain why seeing the Prophet in a dream is a special case.

Core Idea
Prophetic dreams are revelation; others' dreams are not—only the Prophet’s dreams are immune from satanic interference.

The author, citing Ibn Hajar and al-Khattabi, establishes that the dreams of prophets are inviolable revelation, while others' dreams can be contaminated by the devil. Even righteous people's dreams are not binding because they are not infallible. The special case of dreaming of the Prophet himself is discussed based on hadith that the devil cannot impersonate the Prophet, thus lending reliability to such dreams, but not making them a source of legislation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The author distinguishes prophetic dreams (al-ru’ya al-nabawiyya) which are wahi, and non-prophetic dreams. Find the phrase 'رؤيا الأنبياء وحي' and note the exception.
2
Understand the reason for the difference
Prophetic revelation is protected (ma‘sum) from error; others are not. The author quotes al-Khattabi: 'غير الأنبياء قد يحضرها الشيطان'.
3
Examine the exception for dreaming of the Prophet
The hadith states that the devil cannot impersonate the Prophet in a dream. This is a special guarantee of truthfulness for such dreams, as mentioned by Ibn Hajar and others.
This does not make such dreams a source of legislation, only reliable in content.
4
Determine the epistemological status
Despite the reliability of seeing the Prophet in a dream, it still falls under the rule of secondary sources: it needs support from primary sources to be used as a basis for action. The author emphasizes that even such dreams are not a general proof (hujja) for legislation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the dreams of prophets considered revelation?
A) Because they are always pleasant
B) Because they are protected from satanic corruption
C) Because the dreamers are righteous
D) Because they are interpreted by scholars

Only prophets' dreams are infallible revelation; non-prophets' dreams, even if true, lack binding authority, but a dream of the Prophet himself is reliable due to his protected status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Task 2: Given the hadith about seeing the Prophet in a dream, could a person derive a new religious ruling from such a dream? Explain using the author's reasoning about secondary sources needing support.

The Epistemological Value of Dreams
Author evaluates the epistemological status of dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the truthfulness of a believer's dream and its lack of binding authority (hujjiyya) on others.

Core Idea
A dream can be true for the dreamer but is not a proof for others.

This passage discusses the nature of dreams for the righteous believer. While a true dream is truthful and sound, it cannot be used as a binding argument for others. The author emphasizes that righteousness is necessary for benefiting from dreams, and that educational institutions should focus on spiritual purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims about dreams
The author states that a righteous believer's true dream is truthful and sound (صادقة صالحة) but also that it lacks authority (حجية) in obligating others. Locate these two claims in the Arabic.
The term حجية is key in usul al-fiqh; here it is denied to dreams.
2
Analyze the condition of righteousness
The author links the truthfulness of a dream to the individual's rectitude (استقامة) and purification (تزكية). Why is righteousness a precondition?
Without purification, a dream may be mere illusion or mixed with satanic influence.
3
Understand the educational implication
The passage calls for educational focus on tazkiya. How does this connect to the epistemological reliability of dreams?
Proper education produces righteous individuals whose dreams can be trusted, even if not binding on others.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعل ذلك مؤشر على خلل في الاستقامة لدى الفرد، إذ إن الأصل في رؤيا المؤمن الصالح الصادق أن تكون صادقة صالحة، بصرف النظر عن عدم حجيتها في إلزام الآخرين، أو الخطورة التي قد تلحق لمن جعل جل همه تتبع رؤيا المنام، في حقه أو في حق الآخرين إن ذلك يلقي بأسبابه على المناهج التعليمية وسائر الوسائط التربوية التي يفترض أن تتعاون في تحقيق هدف التربية في إخراج الإنسان الصالح. وهذا الصلاح يعني استقامة في الفكر والسلوك، وصفاء في الشكل والمضمون. وهو الأمر الذي سيؤهل بعد ذلك للإفادة من مصدرية الرؤيا، بحيث تكون صادقة حقاً لا مجرد وهم وتشبث بالمجهول. وهذا يدعو المؤسسات التربوية إلى التركيز على التربية النفسية الوجدانية الإيمانية القائمة على التزكية فهي تكاد تكون في حكم الفريضة الغائبة في الحياة التربوية لدى كل وسائط التربية رسمية وغير رسمية.
الموقف من رؤيا الرسول
ولكن لئن سلمنا بهذا في الرؤية العادية، فكيف الحال إذ رأى المرء الرسول - في منامه، وقد تضافرت نصوص السنة المطهرة على أن الشيطان عاجز عن التمثل بشخصه - عليه الصلاة والسلام -. ومن ذلك ما أخرجه البخاري ومسلم مجتمعين أو تفرد كل منهما بروايته من مثل: ما رواه أبو هريرة عن النبي : من رآني في المنام فسيراني في اليقظة، ولا يتمثل الشيطان بي. قال أبو عبد الله (البخاري) قال ابن سيرين: إذا رآه في صورته).
Perhaps that indicates a defect in the individual's rectitude, for the principle concerning the true righteous believer's dream is that it is truthful and sound, regardless of its lack of authority in obligating others, or the danger that may befall the one who makes it his sole concern to pursue dreams, regarding his right or the rights of others. That casts its causes upon the educational curricula and all the educational media that are supposed to cooperate in achieving the goal of education in producing the righteous person. This righteousness means rectitude in thought and behavior, and purity in form and content. It is the matter that will then qualify one to benefit from the source of the dream, so that it is truly truthful, not mere illusion and clinging to the unknown. This calls educational institutions to focus on psychological, emotional, and faith-based education grounded in purification, for it is nearly a lost obligation in the educational life of all formal and informal educational media.
The Position on Seeing the Prophet
But if we accept this concerning ordinary vision, what about when one sees the Messenger in his dream, given that the texts of the purified Sunnah have concurred that Satan is incapable of impersonating his person - peace be upon him. Among that is what al-Bukhari and Muslim reported together or each individually narrated, such as what Abu Hurayrah narrated from the Prophet: "Whoever sees me in a dream will see me in wakefulness, and Satan cannot impersonate me." Abu Abdullah (al-Bukhari) said: Ibn Sirin said: "If he sees him in his form."
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the reliability of dreams as a source of knowledge, particularly regarding the vision of the Prophet Muhammad. The author begins by asserting that a righteous believer's true dream is truthful and sound, but notes that it is not a binding proof (hujja) on others. The danger lies in someone obsessively pursuing dreams, which can lead to harm. This problem is attributed to deficiencies in educational systems that should produce righteous individuals. Righteousness encompasses rectitude in thought and behavior, and purity. Once achieved, one can benefit from the source of the dream, making it genuinely truthful rather than illusion. Therefore, the author calls for educational institutions to focus on psychological and faith-based education grounded in purification (tazkiya), which is described as a nearly lost obligation.

Then the author transitions to a specific case: seeing the Prophet in a dream. The author notes that if the above applies to ordinary dreams, then the case of seeing the Prophet is even more significant because multiple hadith texts affirm that Satan is incapable of impersonating the Prophet. To support this, the author cites a hadith from Abu Hurayra: "Whoever sees me in a dream will see me in wakefulness, and Satan cannot impersonate me." Additionally, al-Bukhari quotes Ibn Sirin: "If he sees him in his form." Thus, the passage establishes that while ordinary dreams may not be authoritative, dreams of the Prophet have a special status because their content is protected from satanic deception.

Key Takeaway
A believer's true dream is truthful but not a binding proof on others.

Seeing the Prophet in a Dream
Author discusses the special case of seeing the Prophet in dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological privilege of dreams of the Prophet based on the hadith about Satan's inability to impersonate him.

Core Idea
Dreams of the Prophet are uniquely protected from satanic deception.

The passage transitions to a special case: seeing the Prophet in a dream. Multiple hadith texts affirm that Satan cannot impersonate the Prophet, making such dreams more reliable. However, the author notes that even for ordinary dreams, righteousness is key.

Guided Path

This hadith is widely transmitted and forms the basis for the special status of seeing the Prophet in dreams.
2

The author states that the texts of the purified Sunnah have concurred (تضافرت) that Satan is incapable of impersonating the Prophet. This means that if someone sees the Prophet in a dream, it is genuinely him, not a deceptive image.
This is a unique privilege not granted to other figures.
3

While ordinary dreams can be mixed with satanic influence, dreams of the Prophet are protected. However, the author still emphasizes that righteousness matters for benefiting from dreams. Why?

Two Scholarly Opinions on Seeing the Prophet in Dreams
Author presents two scholarly opinions on interpreting dreams of the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the restrictive and expansive opinions on dreams of the Prophet and explain the criterion that distinguishes a true vision from an interpretive dream.

Core Idea
A dream of the Prophet is either a true reality or an interpretive vision, depending on attribute correspondence.

How do we know if a dream of the Prophet is authentic? Scholars offer two main views. This passage from a classical text presents both, giving you the tools to evaluate reported dreams with scholarly rigor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Locate the first opinion (restrictive) and the second (expansive) in the Arabic. Note how each begins: 'الرأي الأول...' and 'الرأي الثاني...'
The first opinion requires correspondence between the dreamed attributes and known attributes of the Prophet.
2
Extract the key criterion
For the first opinion, the criterion is 'التطابق بين ما يراه الرائي وما عرف عنه من صفاته'. For the second, it is 'الحديث على ظاهره' – accepting the hadith at face value.
The second opinion argues that even if attributes differ, the essence is seen correctly; the attributes might be imagined.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The second opinion supports its view by noting that perception does not require precise visual details; the Prophet's essence is seen, while attributes may be imagined. It also cites the hadith 'فقد رأى الحق'.
This reasoning contrasts with the first opinion's emphasis on exact attribute matching.
4
Apply to a hypothetical case
Consider a dream where someone sees the Prophet with a white beard (known attribute is black). According to the first opinion, this is a dream of interpretation (ta'wil). According to the second, it is still a true vision (haqiqa).
Practice classifying different dream scenarios under each opinion.

Despite what has been said in the understanding (fiqh) of those hadiths by the predecessors, and what their understandings varied and branched, perhaps there is an opportunity to summarize the statement in that into two opinions:
The first opinion: Its adherents restrict the vision of the Prophet - - in a dream to the extent of correspondence between what the dreamer sees and what is known of his attributes; if he is seen contrary to them, it is a dream of interpretation (ru'ya ta'wil) not a dream of reality (ru'ya haqiqa).
The second opinion: Its adherents expand in understanding the meaning of those hadiths to the extent that they take the hadith at its apparent meaning, i.e., they hold that seeing him - - is true, not confused dreams nor from the imitations of the devil. And his saying - -: 'he has seen the truth' confirms this meaning, even if the dreamer sees him contrary to his known attribute, such as one who sees him with a white beard, or that two persons see him at one time, one in the east and the other in the west, and each sees him in his own place, because this is a mistake in his attributes, an imagination of them contrary to what they are, and a person may consider some imaginations as visible because what he imagines is linked to what he usually sees. So his essence - - is seen and his attributes are imagined, not seen.

Commentary
This passage presents two scholarly opinions regarding the interpretation of hadiths about seeing the Prophet in dreams. The author begins by acknowledging the diverse understandings of earlier scholars and then summarizes the matter into two main views. The first opinion restricts the validity of such a dream to a strict correspondence between the dreamer's vision and the known physical attributes of the Prophet; if the Prophet appears with different attributes, the dream is considered a dream of interpretation (ta'wil) rather than a literal reality (haqiqa). The second opinion takes a broader approach, accepting the hadith at face value and maintaining that even if the Prophet appears with unfamiliar attributes, the vision of his essence is true, while the attributes may be imagined. The author supports this by arguing that perception does not require exact visual details—what matters is the authenticity of seeing the Prophet himself, not the accuracy of his depicted features.

Analyzing an Inductive Argument: Contemporary Epistemology on Dreaming of the Prophet
Author summarizes a contemporary scholar's inductive conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the inductive argument used by a contemporary researcher to limit prophetic dreams to the Companions' era.

Core Idea
Inductive reasoning in Islamic epistemology requires complete enumeration for certainty.

This passage presents a contemporary scholar's inductive argument. He surveys all hadiths on dreaming of the Prophet, notes the consistent wording 'whoever sees me', and concludes that the promise applies only to those who knew the Prophet's physical form. We will dissect the inductive structure and assess its strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
What is the researcher trying to prove? He states that all hadiths on this topic are limited to the Prophet's era and companions, ending with the last Companion.
2
Extract the evidence
The first evidence: all hadiths use the wording 'ra'ani' (seeing me), with the pronoun referring to the Prophet's person, implying direct visual acquaintance.
The passage mentions multiple evidences but only gives this first one.
3
Determine the inductive type
The researcher claims to have surveyed all narrations, aiming for complete induction (istiqra' tamm).
Complete induction yields certainty if truly exhaustive.
4
Evaluate the strength
Check if the enumeration is indeed exhaustive. Also, does the wording necessarily imply limitation to contemporaries? Consider alternative interpretations.
The strength depends on completeness and linguistic analysis.
5
Assess epistemic certainty
According to classical logic, complete induction yields certainty (yaqin); incomplete yields probability (zann). Here the researcher claims completeness, but we must verify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك حاصل ما قيل في أدب السابقين وميراثهم الفقهي، بيد أن باحثاً معاصراً دفعه مسلك بعض المستغلين للمشاعر الدينية عند المسلمين ودهمـائهـم إلى تقصي روايات الحديث جميعها، وإعمال النظر الفقهي المزاوج لنظرتي العقل والشرع فيها، فخلص إلى أن كل الأحاديث الواردة في باب الرؤية للنبي - الله في المنام، خاصة بعصر الرسول - عليه الصلاة والسلام - وعصر صحابته الكرام من غير أن يكون لها امتداد في الزمان ولا في الأشخاص، وبذلك فقد انتهى حكمها بوفاة آخر صحابي من الذين عايشوا الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم - أو شاهده أحدهم ولو لمرة واحدة فعرف صورته الحقيقية. وقد استدل على استنتاجه هذا بجملة دلائل يمكن تلخيصها فيما يلي: ۱ - اتفقت جميع روايات الحديث النبوي على لفظ مـن (رآني)، وضمير المتكلم هنا عائد إلى الرسول - ه - أي إلى شخصه بذاتـه وهيئتـه وصـورتـه
That is the gist of what was said in the literature of the predecessors and their jurisprudential heritage. However, a contemporary researcher, driven by the conduct of some exploiters of religious sentiments among Muslims and their dupes, to investigate all hadith narrations and apply jurisprudential consideration combining the two perspectives of reason and revelation to them, concluded that all hadiths concerning the vision of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in a dream are specific to the era of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and the era of his noble Companions, having no extension in time or persons. Thus, their ruling ended with the death of the last Companion who lived with the Messenger (peace be upon him) or saw him even once and knew his real image. He supported his conclusion with a number of evidences that can be summarized as follows: 1. All narrations of the prophetic hadith agree on the wording 'whoever sees me' (ra'ani), and the first-person pronoun here refers to the Messenger (peace be upon him), i.e., to his person, his form, and his image.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological argument by a contemporary researcher (Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa). It begins by summarizing earlier opinions, then introduces a new view that restricts dreams of the Prophet to the time of the Companions. The researcher uses an inductive argument: after surveying all hadiths on this topic, he observes that the wording 'whoever sees me' is universal but the context suggests it was directed at those who had seen him in person. He concludes that the ruling (that the dream is true) applies only to those who had direct visual acquaintance with the Prophet's form. The passage lists the first evidence from the consistent wording of the hadiths. The argument moves from specific textual data to a general ruling, a form of induction (istiqra'). The author presents this as a contemporary position that aims to prevent exploitation of religious emotions.

Analyzing the Researcher's Categorical Syllogism on Prophetic Dream Visions
Author presents a contemporary researcher's argument limiting dream visions of the Prophet to Companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the categorical syllogism underlying the contemporary researcher's argument limiting prophetic dream visions to Companions.

Core Idea
Prophetic dream visions require prior knowledge of the Prophet's real image, which belongs only to Companions.

In this passage, a contemporary researcher presents a rigorous argument to limit the hadith 'whoever sees me in a dream has truly seen me' to the Companions. He employs a categorical syllogism rooted in linguistic and epistemological analysis. By reconstructing his reasoning, we can assess its logical validity and its implications for hadith interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conclusion (natija)
The researcher concludes that the hadiths about seeing the Prophet in dreams apply only to Companions and ended with their era. This is the conclusion he aims to prove.
2
Extract the major premise
The major premise is that valid prophetic dream visions require prior knowledge of the Prophet's real image (صورة حقيقية). This comes from interpreting 'رآني' as seeing the actual person and the statement that Satan cannot impersonate the Prophet's form.
3
Extract the minor premise
Knowledge of the Prophet's real image is possessed only by Companions who saw him in person. Only they can distinguish the real image from similitude.
4
Construct the categorical syllogism
Combine the premises: Major term: eligible for prophetic dream vision; Middle term: one who knows the Prophet's real image; Minor term: Companions. Syllogism: Everyone who knows the Prophet's real image is eligible. All Companions know the Prophet's real image. Therefore, all Companions are eligible. This is a valid first-figure (Barbara) syllogism.
5
Evaluate validity and examine potential objections
The syllogism is formally valid. However, one might question the major premise: does the hadith necessarily require prior knowledge? The researcher's linguistic analysis is key. Assess whether his interpretation is compelling and if alternative interpretations could undermine the premise.

ذلك حاصل ما قيل في أدب السابقين وميراثهم الفقهي، بيد أن باحثاً معاصراً دفعه مسلك بعض المستغلين للمشاعر الدينية عند المسلمين ودهمائهم إلى تقصي روايات الحديث جميعها، وإعمال النظر الفقهي المزاوج لنظرتي العقل والشرع فيها (۳)، فخلص إلى أن كل الأحاديث الواردة في باب الرؤية للنبي - صلى الله عليه وسلم - في المنام، خاصة بعصر الرسول - عليه الصلاة والسلام - وعصر صحابته الكرام من غير أن يكون لها امتداد في الزمان ولا في الأشخاص، وبذلك فقد انتهى حكمها بوفاة آخر صحابي من الذين عايشوا الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم - أو شاهده أحدهم ولو لمرة واحدة فعرف صورته الحقيقية (۳). وقد استدل على استنتاجه هذا بجملة دلائل يمكن تلخيصها فيما يلي:
۱ - اتفقت جميع روايات الحديث النبوي على لفظ من (رآني)، وضمير المتكلم هنا عائد إلى الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم - أي إلى شخصه بذاته وهيئته وصورته الحقيقية التي كان عليها، وهذا لا يتحقق إلا لمن يعرف صورته الحقيقية، ولن يكون من يعرف صورته إلا صحابياً عايشه أو رآه ولو لمرة واحدة. أما من لم يره ولا يعرف صورته الحقيقية؛ فلا يصح أن يقال إنه رآه. ويؤكد هذا أكثر ما جاء في بعض تلك الروايات من قوله - عليه الصلاة والسلام - .... أن يتمثل بي»، و «فإنه لا ينبغي للشيطان أن يتمثل في صورتي)). فجميعها تقصر الرؤيا على من رآه - صلى الله عليه وسلم - بهيئته وصورته الحقيقية التي يعرفها بها من قبل، ولا يكون ذلك إلا لمن لقيه.
۲ - إن اشتراط فريق من العلماء أن يكون الرائي قد رأى الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم - في منامه على الصفة المنقولة من حلاه وأوصافه؛ غاب عنهم أن ثمة أوصافاً قد تتحد أو تتقارب في أشخاص هيئاتهم مختلفة جداً، وذلك كسواد العيون، ولون البشرة، والشعر، وحجم الرأس، والقامة. وعليه فلا يصح أن يقال إن فلاناً قد رأى فلاناً بصورته الحقيقية إلا إذا كان يعرفه قبلاً. وهذا ما يفيده قوله - صلى الله عليه وسلم - فإن الشيطان لا يتمثل بي أو يتمثل في صورتي ومع ذلك كله فليس معنى هذا استحالة رؤية الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم - في المنام، فمن زعم رؤية النبي - صلى الله عليه وسلم - في منامه فإنه قد يكون صادقاً، وقد يكون كاذباً، ولكن هذا خارج عن مجمل أحاديث رؤيته - صلى الله عليه وسلم - والتي هي خاصة بالصحابة فقط بدلالة ألفاظ (رآني) (صورتي) يتمثل بي). ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا لمن لقيه وعرفه ولو مرة واحدة - كما أسلفنا - (٢).
Thus is the gist of what was said in the literature of the predecessors and their jurisprudential heritage, however a contemporary researcher was driven by the path of some who exploit religious sentiments among Muslims and their masses to investigate all the hadith narrations, and to apply jurisprudential consideration combining the views of reason and revelation in them (3), concluding that all the hadiths reported concerning the vision of the Prophet - peace be upon him - in dreams are specific to the era of the Messenger - peace be upon him - and the era of his noble Companions, without any extension in time or persons, and thus its ruling ended with the death of the last Companion who lived with the Messenger - peace be upon him - or saw him even once and thus knew his real image (3). And he supported his conclusion with a number of proofs that can be summarized as follows: 1 - All narrations of the prophetic hadith agree on the wording 'he saw me' (رآني), and the first-person pronoun here refers to the Messenger - peace be upon him - i.e., to his very person, his form, and his real image that he had, and this is only realized for one who knows his real image, and no one knows his image except a Companion who lived with him or saw him even once. As for one who did not see him and does not know his real image, it cannot be said that he saw him. This is further emphasized by what appears in some of those narrations of his saying - peace be upon him - '... he should appear in my form' and 'Verily it is not befitting for Satan to appear in my form'. All of these restrict the vision to one who saw him - peace be upon him - in his actual form and real image that he recognized beforehand, and that is only for one who met him. 2 - Indeed, the condition of a group of scholars that the dreamer must have seen the Messenger - peace be upon him - in his dream in the description transmitted of his beauty and attributes; they overlooked that there are attributes that may be identical or similar in persons whose forms are very different, such as blackness of eyes, skin color, hair, head size, and stature. Therefore, it cannot be said that so-and-so saw so-and-so in his real image unless he knew him before. And this is what his saying - peace be upon him - indicates: 'Verily Satan does not appear in me or appear in my form'. Nevertheless, this does not mean the impossibility of seeing the Messenger - peace be upon him - in a dream. So whoever claims to have seen the Prophet - peace be upon him - in his dream may be truthful or may be lying, but this is outside the totality of the hadiths of his vision - peace be upon him - which are specific only to the Companions by the indication of the words 'he saw me', 'my form', 'he should appear in my form'. And that is only attained by one who met him and knew him even once - as we have mentioned - (2).
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents a contemporary researcher's critical argument restricting prophetic dream visions exclusively to the Companions of the Prophet. The passage begins by contextualizing the researcher's motivation: to counter those who exploit religious sentiments. He conducts a thorough investigation of all hadiths concerning seeing the Prophet in dreams and concludes that these hadiths are time-bound to the era of the Prophet and his Companions, ending with the death of the last Companion. The researcher offers two proofs. The first proof analyzes the linguistic evidence: the hadith uses the first-person pronoun 'رآني' (he saw me), which implies the dreamer saw the Prophet's actual form (صورة حقيقية). Since only Companions saw the Prophet in person and thus knew his real image, only they can fulfill this condition. The hadith's additional phrase that Satan cannot impersonate the Prophet's form further supports this. The second proof addresses a common scholarly condition that the dreamer must see the Prophet in his known attributes. The researcher argues that many physical attributes (e.g., eye color, skin tone) can be shared by different people, so seeing such attributes does not guarantee it is the Prophet unless the dreamer previously knew the Prophet's unique image. Again, this knowledge is exclusive to Companions. The researcher clarifies that he does not deny the possibility of seeing the Prophet in dreams altogether; rather, he limits the specific hadith promises to those who met him. The argument relies on a categorical syllogism: major premise—valid prophetic dream visions require prior knowledge of the Prophet's real image; minor premise—only Companions possess this knowledge; conclusion—only Companions can have such visions.

الصورة الحقيقية
الصحابي

The Condition for True Visions: Knowing the Prophet's Form
Author argues that only companions can truly see the Prophet in dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only the companions can have a true vision of the Prophet in a dream, based on the epistemological condition of knowing his physical form.

Core Idea
True visions require prior knowledge of the Prophet's form—only companions fulfill this.

The author establishes a critical epistemological condition: to truly see the Prophet in a dream, one must already know his true physical appearance. This lesson unpacks that condition and the evidence from hadith that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence where the author states who can truly see the Prophet in a dream. What is the key condition?
2
Extract the rationale
Why is prior knowledge of the Prophet's form necessary? What evidence from hadith does the author use?
The author uses the hadith about the devil not being able to impersonate the Prophet.
3
Recognize the restriction
Notice that the author restricts this type of vision to the Companions. What makes them unique?
Only they have seen the Prophet's true form directly.
4
Summarize the epistemological condition
In your own words, state the condition for a true vision and who meets it.

Chapter Three: Section Four – Secondary Sources. The true form that he was upon, and this is only realized for one who knows his true form, and no one knows his form except a companion who lived with him or saw him even once. As for one who has not seen him and does not know his true form, it is not correct to say that he saw him. This is confirmed by what is found in some of those narrations from his saying, peace be upon him: '... that he appears to me' and 'Verily, it is not proper for the devil to appear in my form.' All of these restrict the vision to one who saw him - with his appearance and true form that he knew from before, and that is only for one who met him. 2 - The condition of a group of scholars that the seer must have seen the Messenger in his dream in the described attributes from his beauty and characteristics; they overlooked that there are attributes that may be shared or similar in persons whose appearances are very different, such as the blackness of eyes, skin color, hair, head size, and stature. Therefore, it is not correct to say that so-and-so saw so-and-so in his true form unless he knew him previously. This is what his saying indicates: 'Verily, the devil does not appear in my form' or 'appears in my form.' Yet all of this does not mean the impossibility of seeing the Messenger in a dream. Whoever claims to have seen the Prophet in his dream may be truthful or may be lying, but this is outside the scope of the hadiths about seeing him, which are specific to the companions only by the indication of the words 'he saw me', 'my form', 'appears to me'. And that is only achieved for one who met him and knew him even once - as we have already mentioned. (2) Prayer. As for similar visions in which people claim to have seen the Messenger, peace be upon him, assuming their authenticity, they do not go beyond ordinary visions, and they pertain to the seer's own righteousness or wickedness. In this case, they are not specific to seeing the Messenger only in a dream, but to any other vision, so they take the same ruling. (1) It has already been stated that visions are not authoritative and are not considered one of the sources of knowledge, except on condition that they do not contradict authentic transmitted knowledge and clear rational knowledge. The same applies to what is claimed about seeing the Prophet, peace be upon him; such a vision must be presented to the Shariah to be accepted or rejected. (2) Adopting the result of this understanding strengthens in the learner's psyche a preventive education that fortifies his thinking from the influence of superstitious thinking. This thinking represents a real problem that still affects the contemporary Muslim mind when it escapes from facing its continuous accumulated problems to the illusions of visions and seeing the Prophet in particular, with many arguments and claims. Also, adopting the result of this understanding completely closes the door to the advocates of charlatanism and deception. When they do not receive a response from individuals and groups, that means - among other things - the effectiveness of Islamic education and its ability to achieve its preventive goals in protecting the individual and society from deadly social diseases such as charlatanism, superstition, and ignorance. It is worth noting - at the conclusion of the discussion - that this juristic conclusion was not brought by its author alone - although he has the merit of ijtihad and the reward of conveying and detailing the explanation, God willing - as he was preceded in its summary by Ibn Juzayy al-Maliki (d. 741 AH) who said at the end of his discussion of visions: 'Note: He said - peace be upon him - whoever sees me in a dream has indeed seen me, for the devil cannot appear in my form. And the scholars said that seeing the Prophet in a dream is not absolutely valid.'
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and critical discussion about the nature of seeing the Prophet Muhammad in dreams and its validity as a source of knowledge. The author argues that only the companions, who knew the Prophet's true physical form during his lifetime, can truly see him in a dream, because the devil cannot impersonate him. The author first establishes that knowledge of the Prophet's real appearance is necessary to claim having seen him; this is only possible for those who lived with him. He then critiques a condition set by some scholars that the seer must see the Prophet in his described attributes, pointing out that many physical traits are shared among people, so recognition requires prior acquaintance. The author emphasizes that the hadiths about seeing the Prophet in dreams are restricted to the companions, as indicated by the words 'he saw me' and 'my form'. However, he clarifies that this does not mean it is impossible for non-companions to have such dreams—they may be truthful or false—but those dreams are not the same as the ones mentioned in the hadiths. Moving to the epistemological value, the author states that even if such a vision is authentic, it is not a source of knowledge (hujja) unless it conforms to the Quran, Sunnah, and reason. Such dreams must be evaluated by the Shariah. The author then highlights the educational benefit of this understanding: it protects Muslims from superstition and charlatanism, strengthening their critical thinking. Finally, he notes that his conclusion is not novel; it was anticipated by Ibn Juzayy al-Maliki.

The Epistemological Status of Dreams of the Prophet
Author argues that even true visions of the Prophet are not authoritative unless they conform to Shariah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the epistemological authority of claims of seeing the Prophet in dreams, applying the principle that such visions must conform to Shariah.

Core Idea
Even if a vision is genuine, it carries no authority unless it agrees with revelation and reason.

The author moves beyond the condition of who can have a true vision to address its epistemological weight. He asserts that even authentic dreams are not a valid source of knowledge unless they align with the Quran, Sunnah, and rational certainty. This lesson examines that crucial principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the critique of the 'described attributes' condition
Why does the author reject the condition that a seer must see the Prophet in his described features? What is his argument about shared attributes?
2
Differentiate between true visions and ordinary claims
The author distinguishes between the hadith-specific visions (for Companions) and ordinary claims. How does he frame this distinction?
3
Identify the epistemological principle
Find the sentence where the author states that visions are not authoritative (hujja). What is the exception?
The exception is non-contradiction with Shariah and reason.
4
Apply the principle to claims of seeing the Prophet
According to the author, what must be done with any claimed vision of the Prophet?
It must be presented to Shariah for acceptance or rejection.

الباب الثالث: الفصل الرابع – المصادر التبعية
الحقيقية التي كان عليها، وهذا لا يتحقق إلا لمن يعرف صورته الحقيقية، ولـن يكون من يعرف صورته إلا صحابيًا عايشه أو رآه ولو لمرة واحدة. أما مـن لــم يره ولا يعرف صورته الحقيقية؛ فلا يصح أن يقال إنه رآه. ويؤكد هذا أكثر ما جاء في بعض تلك الروايات من قوله - عليه الصلاة والسلام - .... أن يتمثل بي»، و «فإنه لا ينبغي للشيطان أن يتمثل في صورتي)). فجميعها تقصر الرؤيا على من رآه - ل - بهيئته وصورته الحقيقية التي يعرفها بها من قبل، ولا يكون ذلك إلا لمن لقيه.
۲ - إن اشتراط فريق من العلماء أن يكون الرائي قد رأى الرسول - - في منامه على الصفة المنقولة من حلاه وأوصافه؛ غاب عنهم أن ثمة أوصافـا قـد تتحد أو تتقارب في أشخاص هيئاتهم مختلفة جداً، وذلك كسواد العيون، ولــون البشرة، والشعر، وحجم الرأس، والقامة. وعليه فلا يصح أن يقال إن فلانـا قـد رأى فلانا بصورته الحقيقية إلا إذا كان يعرفه قبلاً. وهذا ما يفيده قوله - - فإن الشيطان لا يتمثل بي أو يتمثل في صورتي ومع ذلك كله فليس معنى هذا استحالة رؤية الرسول - ل - في المنام، فمن زعم رؤية النبي - - في منامه فإنه قد يكون صادقًا، وقد يكون كاذبًا، ولكن هذا خارج عن مجمل أحاديث رؤيته - - والتي هي خاصة بالصحابة فقط بدلالة ألفاظ (رآني) (صورتي) يتمثل بي). ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا لمن لقيه وعرفه ولو مرة واحدة - كما أسلفنا - (٢).
الصلاة
أما أمثال هذه الرؤيا التي يزعم فيها أصحابها رؤيتهم الرسول - عليه والسلام - فعلى فرض صحتها فلا تخرج عن الرؤيا العادية، وهي تتعلق بذات الرائي من حيث صلاحه أو فسقه وفي هذه الحالة فإنها لا تختص برؤية الرسول - ه - فقط في المنام، بل بأي رؤية أخرى، فتأخذ الحكم نفسه (۱). وقد تقدّم القول بعدم حجية الرؤيا واعتبارها أحد مصادر المعرفة، إلا بشرط عدم مخالفتها لصحيح المنقول وصريح المعقول، وكذلك الحال حتى فيما يدعى من رؤية النبي - عليه الصلاة والسلام - فإن هذه الرؤيا لابد وأن تعرض على الشرع لتقبل أو ترفض ) . (۲)
والأخذ بنتيجة هذا الفهم يعزز في نفسية المتعلم اتجاه التربية الوقائية المحصنة لتفكيره من التأثر بالتفكير الخرافي. هذا التفكير الذي يمثل مشكلة حقيقية، ما تزال تفعل فعلها في العقل المسلم المعاصر، حين يهرب من مواجهة مشكلاته المستمرة المتراكمة إلى أوهام الرؤيا ورؤيا النبي - - على وجه الخصوص، بحجج ودعاوى كثيرة. كما أن في الأخذ بنتيجة هذا الفهم ما يغلق الباب تماماً أمام دعاة الدجل والتضليل، وحين لا يلقون استجابة من الأفراد والجماعات فإن ذلك يعني - فيما يعني - فاعلية التربية الإسلامية وقدرتها على تحقيق أهدافها الوقائية في تحصين الفرد والمجتمع من الأمراض الاجتماعية الفتاكة كالدجل والخرافة والتجهيل.
وخليق بالإشارة - في ختام الحديث - أنّ هذا الاستنتاج الفقهي لم يأت به صاحبه وحده - وإن كان له فضل الاجتهاد وأجر التبليغ وتفصيل البيان إن شاء الله - إذ سبقه إلى خلاصة ذلك ابن جُزَي المالكي (ت: ٧٤١هـ) حيث قال في نهاية حديثه عن الرؤيا: ((تنبيه): قال - ل - من رآني في المنام فقد رآني، فإن الشيطان لا يتمثل بي. وقال العلماء لا تصح رؤيا النبي - - قطعا
Chapter Three: Section Four – Secondary Sources. The true form that he was upon, and this is only realized for one who knows his true form, and no one knows his form except a companion who lived with him or saw him even once. As for one who has not seen him and does not know his true form, it is not correct to say that he saw him. This is confirmed by what is found in some of those narrations from his saying, peace be upon him: '... that he appears to me' and 'Verily, it is not proper for the devil to appear in my form.' All of these restrict the vision to one who saw him - with his appearance and true form that he knew from before, and that is only for one who met him. 2 - The condition of a group of scholars that the seer must have seen the Messenger in his dream in the described attributes from his beauty and characteristics; they overlooked that there are attributes that may be shared or similar in persons whose appearances are very different, such as the blackness of eyes, skin color, hair, head size, and stature. Therefore, it is not correct to say that so-and-so saw so-and-so in his true form unless he knew him previously. This is what his saying indicates: 'Verily, the devil does not appear in my form' or 'appears in my form.' Yet all of this does not mean the impossibility of seeing the Messenger in a dream. Whoever claims to have seen the Prophet in his dream may be truthful or may be lying, but this is outside the scope of the hadiths about seeing him, which are specific to the companions only by the indication of the words 'he saw me', 'my form', 'appears to me'. And that is only achieved for one who met him and knew him even once - as we have already mentioned. (2) Prayer. As for similar visions in which people claim to have seen the Messenger, peace be upon him, assuming their authenticity, they do not go beyond ordinary visions, and they pertain to the seer's own righteousness or wickedness. In this case, they are not specific to seeing the Messenger only in a dream, but to any other vision, so they take the same ruling. (1) It has already been stated that visions are not authoritative and are not considered one of the sources of knowledge, except on condition that they do not contradict authentic transmitted knowledge and clear rational knowledge. The same applies to what is claimed about seeing the Prophet, peace be upon him; such a vision must be presented to the Shariah to be accepted or rejected. (2) Adopting the result of this understanding strengthens in the learner's psyche a preventive education that fortifies his thinking from the influence of superstitious thinking. This thinking represents a real problem that still affects the contemporary Muslim mind when it escapes from facing its continuous accumulated problems to the illusions of visions and seeing the Prophet in particular, with many arguments and claims. Also, adopting the result of this understanding completely closes the door to the advocates of charlatanism and deception. When they do not receive a response from individuals and groups, that means - among other things - the effectiveness of Islamic education and its ability to achieve its preventive goals in protecting the individual and society from deadly social diseases such as charlatanism, superstition, and ignorance. It is worth noting - at the conclusion of the discussion - that this juristic conclusion was not brought by its author alone - although he has the merit of ijtihad and the reward of conveying and detailing the explanation, God willing - as he was preceded in its summary by Ibn Juzayy al-Maliki (d. 741 AH) who said at the end of his discussion of visions: 'Note: He said - peace be upon him - whoever sees me in a dream has indeed seen me, for the devil cannot appear in my form. And the scholars said that seeing the Prophet in a dream is not absolutely valid.'
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and critical discussion about the nature of seeing the Prophet Muhammad in dreams and its validity as a source of knowledge. The author argues that only the companions, who knew the Prophet's true physical form during his lifetime, can truly see him in a dream, because the devil cannot impersonate him. The author first establishes that knowledge of the Prophet's real appearance is necessary to claim having seen him; this is only possible for those who lived with him. He then critiques a condition set by some scholars that the seer must see the Prophet in his described attributes, pointing out that many physical traits are shared among people, so recognition requires prior acquaintance. The author emphasizes that the hadiths about seeing the Prophet in dreams are restricted to the companions, as indicated by the words 'he saw me' and 'my form'. However, he clarifies that this does not mean it is impossible for non-companions to have such dreams—they may be truthful or false—but those dreams are not the same as the ones mentioned in the hadiths. Moving to the epistemological value, the author states that even if such a vision is authentic, it is not a source of knowledge (hujja) unless it conforms to the Quran, Sunnah, and reason. Such dreams must be evaluated by the Shariah. The author then highlights the educational benefit of this understanding: it protects Muslims from superstition and charlatanism, strengthening their critical thinking. Finally, he notes that his conclusion is not novel; it was anticipated by Ibn Juzayy al-Maliki.

Key Terms
الشرع
الرؤيا العادية
الحجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the condition under which a vision of the Prophet can be considered a source of knowledge?
A) It must be seen by a pious person.
B) It must not contradict authentic transmitted knowledge and clear rational knowledge.
C) It must be shared with others.
D) It must occur during the night.

Key Takeaway
Even authentic visions of the Prophet are not binding unless they conform to the Quran, Sunnah, and rational certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A person claims that in a dream the Prophet told him that a certain sin is now permissible. Using the author's epistemological condition, explain why this claim must be rejected, and what criteria should be used to evaluate it.

Educational Benefits of Restricting Dream Authority
Author explains the educational and social benefits of his epistemological position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the preventive educational value of the author's epistemological position in combating superstition and charlatanism.

Core Idea
Restricting dream authority fortifies the mind against superstition and protects the community.

The author concludes by highlighting the practical benefits of his analysis for Islamic education. He argues that a proper understanding of dreams of the Prophet immunizes Muslims against superstitious thinking and closes the door to charlatans. This lesson explores that protective dimension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the educational goal
What does the author say about the effect of this understanding on the learner's psyche?
2
Connect to the social problem
How does this understanding combat superstition and charlatanism according to the author?
3
Recognize the scholarly precedent
Who does the author cite as having preceded him in this conclusion? What does that add to the argument?
Ibn Juzayy al-Maliki.
4
Summarize the overall benefit

Dreams as a Secondary Source in Islamic Education
Author discusses dreams as a secondary source in Islamic education

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary and secondary sources of knowledge in Islamic education and explain the role of dreams as a secondary source.

Core Idea
Dreams are a secondary source; they complement but do not replace transmitted, sensory, and rational sources.

In this passage, the author clarifies the status of dreams (ru'ya) within the Islamic educational framework. While dreams have significance in Islamic spirituality, they are not independent sources of knowledge. The author emphasizes the need to balance spiritual insights with the established primary sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary sources
The passage mentions three primary sources: transmitted (naqli), sensory (hissi), and rational (aqli). Note that these are the standard epistemological sources in Islam.
2
Locate the term 'secondary source'
The passage explicitly calls dreams a 'secondary source' (masdar tabi'i). Understand that this means dreams are supplementary and not foundational for deriving knowledge.
3
Understand the function of dreams
Dreams are linked to spiritual education (tarbiya ruhiyya). The passage states that they must be balanced with the three primary sources to achieve holistic education.
4
Consider implications for the educational process
The passage mentions curriculum, teacher, and learner as parties that must work together to maintain this balance. Reflect on how overemphasis on dreams could undermine rational or transmitted knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الرابع - المصادر التبعية
إن هذا النقاش الواسع نسبياً لمفهوم الرؤيا ودلالاتها لا يُغير من كونها مصدراً ثانوياً معتبراً له انعكاساته على مختلف أطراف العملية التعليمية التربوية من منهج ومعلم ومتعلم، بحيث تعمل جميعاً على إيجاد التوازن بين مصادر التربية النقلية والحسية والعقلية من جهة وبين الرؤيا باعتبارها من جوانب التربية الروحية من جهة أخرى، وبما يحقق ذلك التوازن المنشود.
Chapter Three, Section Four: Secondary Sources. This relatively extensive discussion of the concept of dreams and their implications does not change the fact that it is considered a secondary source with repercussions on the various aspects of the educational process: curriculum, teacher, and learner, such that all work together to achieve a balance between the transmitted, sensory, and rational sources of education on one hand, and dreams as aspects of spiritual education on the other hand, thus achieving the desired balance.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is from a discussion on secondary sources in Islamic education. The author asserts that despite a thorough analysis of dreams and their implications, they remain a secondary source. The passage emphasizes that the educational system must balance three primary categories of sources: transmitted (naqliyya), sensory (hissiyya), and rational (aqliyya) with the spiritual dimension represented by dreams (ru'ya). The goal is a holistic equilibrium in education, integrating both external and internal forms of knowledge.

Key Terms
الرؤيا
المصادر التبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of dreams (al-ru'ya) in Islamic education?
A) They are a primary source alongside transmitted sources.
B) They are a secondary source that must be balanced with transmitted, sensory, and rational sources.
C) They are the only source for spiritual education.
D) They are irrelevant to the educational process.

Key Takeaway
Dreams are a secondary source in Islamic education, valid only when balanced with transmitted, sensory, and rational sources.

Distinguishing Absolute and Methodological Doubt
Author classifies doubt into absolute and methodological types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute doubt and methodological doubt, and evaluate their suitability for Islamic scholarly inquiry.

Core Idea
Doubt can either paralyze or purify; methodological doubt builds certainty where absolute doubt destroys.

In this passage, the author classifies doubt into two types: absolute doubt, which is excessive, impractical, and leads to nihilism, and methodological doubt, a disciplined tool used by scholars like al-Ghazali to attain certainty. Understanding this distinction is essential for navigating epistemological challenges in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of doubt
From the passage, extract the two categories: al-shakk al-mutlaq (absolute doubt) and al-shakk al-manhaji (methodological doubt).
2
Analyze characteristics of absolute doubt
List the attributes: excessive, comprehensive, impractical, destructive to constants and givens, leads to nihilism and sophistry.
The author strongly warns against this type, especially in education.
3
Analyze characteristics of methodological doubt
Note its purpose: a tool for complete examination, accepting only what is certain, used by al-Ghazali and Descartes to free the mind from false ideas.
This type builds and does not destroy.
4
Compare consequences
Absolute doubt leads to intellectual tearing, psychological disturbance, and nihilism. Methodological doubt leads to certainty and builds knowledge.
The author explicitly says methodological doubt 'builds and does not destroy'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشك قسمان: الشك المطلق (Absolute Doubt): وهو شك مفرط شامل عند ديكارت يمتد إلى كل شيء، كما أنه نظري لا يصح أن يطبق في الحياة العملية. ومن الناحية التربوية المباشرة فإنه اتجاه تدميري للثوابت والمسلّمات التي يفترض أن تقوم عليها الأبحاث العلمية، وأن ينشأ عليها المتعلمون في مراحلهم التعليمية الأولى، حيث تكون عملية النمو الجسمي والعقلي المعرفي غير مؤهلة لإقامة مثل هذا الشك فتكون نتيجة إلزامهم أو التزامهم به بمثابة عملية انتحارية للعقل والروح والنفس حيث يبدأ التمزق الفكري والاضطراب النفسي، والخواء الروحي، والقلق الوجداني، في السيطرة على كيان المتعلم شيئاً فشيئاً، إلى أن يُسلِمَهُ إلى العدمية والعبث، ومن ثم إعادة أشكال السوفسطائية بلا أدريتها، أو عنديتها، أو عناديتها وكلها اتجاهات امتلكت المعرفة دون شك ولكنها لم تلامس الحكمة، كما يعرف ذلك الدارسون للفلسفة، وإن شئت التعبير التربوي فقل: لم تلامس حكمة المربين. ٢- الشك المنهجي (Methodological Doubt): ويمثل مرحلة من مراحل منهج البحث في الفلسفة وقوامها تمحيص المعاني والأحكام تمحيصاً تاماً، بحيث لا يقبل منها إلا ما ثبت يقينه، ومن أبرز القائلين بها الغزالي ثم ديكارت، وذلك بغرض تحرير عقل الباحث من الأفكار الخاطئة، وأن لا يقبل إلا ما ثبت للعقل بالبداهة. ومثل هذا الشك يبني ولا يهدم
Doubt is of two types: Absolute Doubt: It is excessive, comprehensive doubt according to Descartes that extends to everything, and it is theoretical and not valid to be applied in practical life. From a direct educational perspective, it is a destructive orientation towards the constants and givens that scientific research is supposed to be based upon, and upon which learners should be raised in their early educational stages, where the process of physical, mental, and cognitive growth is not qualified to establish such doubt. So forcing them or their commitment to it is like a suicidal operation for the mind, spirit, and soul, where intellectual tearing, psychological disturbance, spiritual emptiness, and emotional anxiety begin to control the learner's entity little by little, until it delivers him to nihilism and absurdity, and then to a return of sophistry in its agnosticism, stubbornness, or obstinacy, all of which are orientations that possessed knowledge without doubt but did not touch wisdom, as students of philosophy know, or if you like an educational expression: they did not touch the wisdom of educators. 2. Methodological Doubt: It represents a stage of the research method in philosophy, consisting of a complete examination of meanings and judgments, such that nothing is accepted except what is certain. Among the most prominent proponents are al-Ghazali and then Descartes, with the aim of freeing the researcher's mind from false ideas and not accepting except what is proven to the mind by intuition. And such doubt builds and does not destroy.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage classifies doubt into two distinct types: absolute doubt and methodological doubt. Absolute doubt is portrayed as excessive, impractical, and destructive, especially in educational contexts, leading to nihilism and sophistry. Methodological doubt, on the other hand, is a disciplined tool for inquiry, used by al-Ghazali and Descartes, to attain certainty by clearing away false assumptions. The author emphasizes that methodological doubt is constructive, while absolute doubt is harmful.

Distinguishing Absolute Doubt from Methodological Doubt
Author classifies and evaluates types of doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute doubt and methodological doubt and evaluate their appropriateness in Islamic learning contexts.

Core Idea
Not all doubt is equal: absolute doubt destroys, methodological doubt builds certainty.

This passage from a contemporary work on Quranic epistemology classifies doubt into two fundamental types. The author warns against the destructive educational effects of absolute doubt while endorsing methodological doubt as a disciplined tool for arriving at certainty. Understanding this distinction is crucial for any Muslim intellectual engaging with philosophical or theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of doubt
Read the first paragraph of the Arabic passage. The author states: 'الشك قسمان: الشك المطلق ... الشك المنهجي'. Identify these two terms.
2
Compare the author's descriptions
For each type, note the key attributes mentioned. Absolute doubt: 'شك مفرط شامل', 'نظري لا يصح تطبيقه في الحياة العملية'. Methodological doubt: 'مرحلة من مراحل منهج البحث', 'تمحيص المعاني والأحكام', 'لا يقبل إلا ما ثبت يقينه'.
3
Extract the author's critique of absolute doubt
The author describes absolute doubt as 'اتجاه تدميري للثوابت' and says it leads to 'التمزق الفكري والاضطراب النفسي' and ultimately 'العدم والعبث'. Identify the educational and psychological harms mentioned.
4
Note the endorsement of methodological doubt
The author says methodological doubt 'يبني ولا يهدم' and is beneficial when applied in specific fields with mature learners. Find the conditions for its use.
5
Understand the author's stance on pathological doubt
The author mentions that some classify pathological doubt separately, but disagrees, stating it is 'مندرجة في إطار الشك المطلق'. Why does the author consider it a subset?

الباب الرابع الفصل الأول - المعرفة بين الشك واليقين
أقسام الشك
الشك قسمان:
الشك المطلق Absolute Doubt):
وهو شك مفرط شامل عند ديكارت يمتد إلى كل شيء، كما أنه نظري لا يصح أن يطبق في الحياة العملية. ومن الناحية التربوية المباشرة فإنه اتجاه تدميري للثوابت والمسلّمات التي يفترض أن تقوم عليها الأبحاث العلمية، وأن ينشأ عليها المتعلمون في مراحلهم التعليمية الأولى، حيث تكون عملية النمو الجسمي والعقلي المعرفي غير مؤهلة لإقامة مثل هذا الشك فتكون نتيجة إلزامهم أو التزامهم به بمثابة عملية انتحارية للعقل والروح والنفس حيث يبدأ التمزق الفكري والاضطراب النفسي، والخواء الروحي، والقلق الوجداني، في السيطرة على كيان المتعلم شيئاً فشيئاً، إلى أن يُسلِمَهُ إلى العدمية والعبث، ومن ثم إعادة أشكال السوفسطائية بلا أدريتها، أو عنديتها، أو عناديتها وكلها اتجاهات امتلكت المعرفة دون شك ولكنها لم تلامس الحكمة، كما يعرف ذلك الدارسون للفلسفة، وإن شئت التعبير التربوي فقل: لم تلامس حكمة المربين.
٢- الشك المنهجي: Methodological Doubt):
ويمثل مرحلة من مراحل منهج البحث في الفلسفة وقوامها تمحيص المعاني والأحكام تمحيصاً تاماً، بحيث لا يقبل منها إلا ما ثبت يقينه، ومن أبرز القائلين بها الغزالي ثم ديكارت، وذلك بغرض تحرير عقل الباحث من الأفكار الخاطئة، وأن لا يقبل إلا ما ثبت للعقل بالبداهة .(۱) ومثل هذا الشك يبني ولا يهدم
ومن المفيد بل قد يكون من الضروري أحياناً تشجيع مثل هذا المنهج، في مجالات علمية محددة والمتعلمين ذوي تخصصات واتجاهات معينة، وفي مستوى عمري ومعرفي يؤهلان لتطبيقه.
وفي سياق الحديث عن أقسام الشك يعد أحد الباحثين الشك المطلق غير الشك المرضي، فعنده أن الشك المطلق هو أخطر أنواع الشك إذ هو شك في وسائل المعرفة، أما الشك المرضي فمثل الشك الذي لا يزول مجرد إبعاده كالذي يتوضأ ثم لا ينفك يشك أنه توضاً. وهو أي الباحث بهذه الإضافة الشك أنواعاً ثلاثة، فإلى جانب الشك المنهجي هناك شك مرضي قد جعل من وآخر مطلق (۱).
ومع عدم التسليم بهذه الوجهة، إذ الشك المرضي مندرج في إطار الشك المطلق عند التأمل، فإنّ الباحث صاحب هذا التصنيف قد كفانا مؤونة المناقشة إذ اعتبر الشك المرضي خارج نطاق البحث في نظرية المعرفة (٢).

Chapter Four, Section One - Knowledge between Doubt and Certainty
Divisions of Doubt
Doubt is of two types:
1. Absolute Doubt:
It is an excessive and comprehensive doubt according to Descartes, extending to everything, and it is theoretical and not valid for application in practical life. From a direct educational perspective, it is a destructive tendency towards the constants and postulates that scientific research is supposed to be based upon, and upon which learners are supposed to be raised in their early educational stages, where the process of physical, mental, and cognitive growth is not qualified to establish such doubt. So the result of imposing it upon them or their adherence to it is like a suicidal operation for the mind, spirit, and soul, where intellectual fragmentation, psychological disturbance, spiritual emptiness, and emotional anxiety begin to control the learner's being little by little, until it delivers him to nihilism and absurdity, and then the re-emergence of forms of sophism with its agnosticism, or its stubbornness, or its obstinacy, all of which are tendencies that possessed knowledge without doubt but did not touch wisdom, as students of philosophy know, or if you wish an educational expression, say: they did not touch the wisdom of educators.
2. Methodological Doubt:
It represents a stage of the research method in philosophy, consisting of thoroughly examining meanings and judgments, so that only that whose certainty is established is accepted. Among the most prominent proponents of it are al-Ghazali and then Descartes, with the purpose of freeing the researcher's mind from erroneous ideas, and not accepting except what is established for the mind by intuition.(1) Such doubt builds and does not destroy.
It is beneficial, and may sometimes be necessary, to encourage such a method in specific scientific fields and for learners with particular specializations and orientations, and at an age and knowledge level that qualifies them to apply it.
In the context of discussing the divisions of doubt, one researcher considers absolute doubt to be other than pathological doubt. According to him, absolute doubt is the most dangerous type of doubt because it is doubt in the means of knowledge. As for pathological doubt, it is like doubt that does not disappear simply by removing it, like one who performs ablution and then continues to doubt that he performed ablution. This researcher, with this addition, made doubt into three types: alongside methodological doubt, there is pathological doubt and another absolute (doubt).(1)
And while not agreeing with this viewpoint, since pathological doubt is included within the framework of absolute doubt upon reflection, this researcher who made this classification has spared us the trouble of discussion, as he considered pathological doubt outside the scope of research in the theory of knowledge.(2)
The Position of the Quran on Doubt
If we want to know the position of the Quran on the issue of doubt and its proponents, it is good for us to present the Quran's view of the human being in terms of the subjective capability for obtaining knowledge, and in terms of the qualities that qualify him for acquiring it, as follows:
First: Subjective Capability
The Holy Quran affirms the inherent subjective qualities of the human being, which in their totality indicate - as we will see - the possibility of his obtaining knowledge. He, even though created devoid of any
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological classification and critique of different types of doubt, ending with an introduction to the Quranic perspective on human knowledge capabilities. The author begins by dividing doubt into two main types: absolute doubt (al-shak al-mutlaq) and methodological doubt (al-shak al-manhaji). Absolute doubt, associated with Descartes, is characterized as excessive, all-encompassing, and purely theoretical, with no place in practical life. The author strongly critiques this type from an educational viewpoint, arguing that imposing it on young learners is destructive, leading to intellectual, psychological, and spiritual harm, ultimately resulting in nihilism and a return to sophism. This type of doubt, though it may appear to possess knowledge, lacks wisdom. Methodological doubt, on the other hand, is presented as a valuable research tool practiced by al-Ghazali and Descartes. It involves a thorough examination of all ideas and judgments, accepting only those that achieve certainty. This doubt is constructive and can be beneficial when applied appropriately to mature learners in specific fields. The author then introduces a third category mentioned by some researchers: pathological doubt (al-shak al-maradi), exemplified by obsessive doubt about actions like ablution. However, the author disagrees with this classification, arguing that pathological doubt is actually a subset of absolute doubt and is excluded from the theory of knowledge proper. Finally, the passage transitions to the Quranic perspective, starting with the concept of subjective capability (al-imkan al-dhati), affirming that humans possess innate qualities that enable them to acquire knowledge, even though they are created initially devoid of it. This sets the stage for a deeper exploration of the Islamic view on knowledge and certainty.

Key Terms
الشك المطلق
الشك المنهجي
الشك المرضي
الإمكان الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It extends to everything.
B) It is destructive to education.
C) It is a stage of research that examines meanings and judgments to accept only what is certain.
D) It is pathological and does not disappear.

Key Takeaway
Absolute doubt is destructive and unsuitable for education, while methodological doubt is a constructive tool for achieving certainty, and the Quran affirms human capacity to acquire knowledge.
