Adam's Knowledge of All Names: Interpretations and Evidence
Author interprets the Quranic verse on teaching Adam the names to explain human knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the various interpretations of the 'names' taught to Adam and identify the author's preponderant view along with his evidence.

Core Idea
Adam's knowledge of all names is the foundation of human acquired potential, encompassing properties of all things.

The Quranic verse 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them' is a key text for understanding human epistemology in Islam. Scholars have offered several interpretations of what 'names' means. In this passage, the author presents these views, weighs them, and concludes that Adam was taught the names of everything, including properties and attributes, not just religious terms. He supports this with a hadith and addresses a linguistic objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and its context
Locate the verse (Al-Baqarah 2:31) in the passage. Note that the author uses it to discuss the beginning of human knowledge potential.
The verse is central to the author's argument.
2
List the three interpretations of 'names'
Find the three scholarly interpretations mentioned: (1) Adam knew the names of all creatures, (2) God instilled necessary knowledge of words and meanings, (3) it was merely teaching attributes. The author presents them without immediate judgment.
These represent different views on what was taught.
3
Identify the author's preponderant view and evidence
Notice that the author favors the first interpretation (names of everything) and cites a hadith from Bukhari where Adam is described as being taught 'the names of everything'.
This hadith is the key evidence.
4
Analyze the linguistic objection about the pronoun
The author addresses an objection: why does the verse use the masculine plural 'them' (hum) when presenting things that include non-rational beings? The answer: rational beings (angels, jinn, humans) are among them, so Arabic custom gives precedence to the rational.
This shows the author's attention to linguistic details.
5
Summarize the author's conclusion on the nature of knowledge
The author concludes that the knowledge taught is of properties and attributes, not essences. But this does not diminish its value; it establishes the human potential to learn about all things on earth.
This connects to the theme of acquired potential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً - الإمكان المكتسب
يبدأ إمكان الإنسان في التعامل مع المعرفة واكتسابها بابتدأ نشأته في الوجود، واستخلافه على الأرض، وتمييزه عن سواه من الكائنات بجملة خصائص يبرزها القرآن الكريم في قصة تعليم آدم (الأسماء كلها). إن وقفة مع قول الله تعالى ﴿وَعَلَّمَ آدَمَ الأَسْمَاءَ كُلَّهَا ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُمْ عَلَى الْمَلَائِكَةِ فَقَالَ أَنبِئُونِي بِأَسْمَاءِ هَؤُلاءِ إِن كُنتُمْ صادِقِينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٣١/٢] كفيلة بإيضاح حقيقة المعرفة عند الإنسان الأول آدم عليه السلام)، ومن ثم الانتقال إلى الحديث عن أبرز خصائص ذريته. فالآية إشارة إلى معرفة آدم أسماء جميع المخلوقات والكائنات الحية وغير الحية، أو أن الله تعالى علم آدم اسم كل شيء (۱) ، أو أنه خلق فيه علماً ضرورياً بتلك الألفاظ وتلك المعاني، وهذا ما قال به جمهرة من العلماء (۳). فهي معرفة عامة تشمل المعقولات والمحسوسات والمتخيلات والموهومات وألهمه معرفة ذوات الأشياء وخواصها وأسمائها، وأصول العلوم وقوانين الصناعات، وكيفية آلاتها (۳). وإما أن يكون ذلك مجرد تعليم لصفات وخواص الأشياء، لأنها تدل على ماهياتها، فصح أن يكون المراد الأسماء والصفات، وذلك جائز لغة (٤) . ولكن يترجح المعنى الأول وهو أن الله تعالى علم آدم أسماء كل شيء بدليل ما أخرجه البخاري أن رسول الله الله قال: (يجتمع المؤمنون يوم القيامة فيقولون لو استشفعنا إلى ربنا، فيأتون آدم فيقولون: أنت أبو الناس خلقك الله بيده، وأسجد لك ملائكته وعلمك أسماء كل شيء، فاشفع لنا عند ربك))(۱).
أما أن يعترض معترض على سبب تعليم الله آدم أنواع المسميات جميعها، وكان فيها ما ليس عاقلاً ، حيث قال (عرضهم) ولم يقل (عرضها) فإنه لما كان في جملتها الملائكة والإنس والجن وهم عقلاء فغلب الأكمل، على عادة العرب تغليب الكامل على الناقص (٢) . وإنه لما كان العرض إنما هو للمسميات لا للأسماء، حيث إن عرض الأسماء يقتضي لغوياً- قول (عرضها) فإن استخدام (هؤلاء) و (عرضهم يدلان على أن المعروض إنما هو المسميات. وعليه فإن الإنسان الذي جاء الحديث كله عن خلافته على الأرض قـد علـم الأشياء التي يستخدمها على الأرض عن طريق تعليم آدم كنوع ممثل، وليس كفرد أو نفس عينة من الأشياء التي يستخدمها الإنسان في حياته إلى يوم القيامة، وسواء أكانت هذه الأشياء حيوانات أو نباتات أو مخترعات، وذلك منذ آدم إلى أن يرث الله الأرض ومن عليها، ولكن معرفة خصائصها وصفاتها وعناصرها وكيفياتها، أما الحقائق فتعرف عن طريق تحليل هذه الصفات والعناصر والمكونات. وهذا يردّ على من ذهب إلى أن الأسماء المشار إليها في الآية خاصة بأسماء الله الحسنى، أو أسماء الملائكة، مع أن الحديث ليس خاصاً بمجال المعرفة الدينية حتى يقال هذا، ولكنه شامل لكل معارف الإنسان، ولذلك فلما أدرك الملائكة أن الأمر لا يعنيهم لأنه ليس في محال المعرفة الدينية فحسب زال (۳). وإذا رجحنا أن المعرفة في هذه الأسماء لا تعدو معرفة الخصائص والصفات وليس معرفة الماهية والكنه؛ فإن هذا لا يتعارض مع منهج البحث
Second: Acquired Potential. The human potential for dealing with and acquiring knowledge begins with the beginning of his existence in the world, his succession on earth, and his distinction from other creatures by a set of characteristics that the Noble Quran highlights in the story of teaching Adam 'all the names'. Indeed, a pause with the saying of God Almighty: 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them; then He presented them to the angels and said: Inform Me of the names of these, if you are truthful' [Al-Baqarah: 31] suffices to clarify the reality of knowledge in the first human, Adam (peace be upon him), and then to move to discussing the most prominent characteristics of his offspring. So the verse indicates Adam's knowledge of the names of all creatures, living and non-living, or that God Almighty taught Adam the name of everything (1), or that He created in him necessary knowledge of those words and those meanings, and this is what the majority of scholars said (3). It is a general knowledge that includes intelligibles, sensibles, imaginables, and estimables, and He inspired him with knowledge of the essences of things, their properties, and their names, and the principles of sciences, the laws of industries, and the modality of their instruments (3). Or it might be merely a teaching of the attributes and properties of things, because they indicate their quiddities, so it is correct that the intended meaning is the names and attributes, and that is linguistically permissible (4). But the first meaning is preponderant, that God Almighty taught Adam the names of everything, evidenced by what Al-Bukhari narrated that the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: 'The believers will gather on the Day of Resurrection and say: If only we could seek intercession from our Lord. So they will come to Adam and say: You are the father of mankind; God created you with His hand, and made His angels prostrate to you, and taught you the names of everything, so intercede for us with your Lord' (1).
As for an objector objecting to the reason for God teaching Adam all types of named things, including what is not rational, since He said 'presented them' (ʿaraḍahum) and did not say 'presented them' (ʿaraḍahā) – it is because among them were angels, humans, and jinn, who are rational, so the more complete predominates, according to the custom of the Arabs of giving predominance to the complete over the deficient (2). And since the presentation was of the named things, not the names – because presenting the names would linguistically require saying 'presented them' (ʿaraḍahā) – the use of 'these' (hāʾulāʾi) and 'presented them' (ʿaraḍahum) indicates that what is presented are the named things. Therefore, the human being about whom the entire discussion of succession on earth came, learned the things he uses on earth through the teaching of Adam as a representative type, not as an individual or as a specific sample of the things that humans use in their lives until the Day of Resurrection, whether these things are animals, plants, or inventions, from Adam until God inherits the earth and those on it. However, knowledge of their properties, attributes, elements, and modalities; as for the realities, they are known through analysis of these attributes, elements, and components. And this refutes those who held that the names referred to in the verse are specific to the beautiful names of God or the names of angels, even though the discussion is not specific to the domain of religious knowledge such that this could be said, but rather it encompasses all human knowledge. Therefore, when the angels realized that the matter did not concern them because it was not solely within the domain of religious knowledge, their astonishment ceased (3). And if we preponderate that the knowledge in these names does not go beyond knowledge of properties and attributes, not knowledge of the essence and inner reality, then this does not contradict the research methodology.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the Quranic verse about teaching Adam the names. The author begins by stating that human knowledge potential starts with Adam's creation and his role as successor on earth. The verse 'He taught Adam the names, all of them' is pivotal. The author presents multiple scholarly interpretations: that Adam learned the names of all creatures, that God instilled necessary knowledge of words and meanings, or that it was merely teaching attributes. After presenting these, the author gives preponderance to the first interpretation, citing a hadith from Bukhari where Adam is described as having been taught 'the names of everything'. The author then addresses an objection: why did the verse use the masculine plural pronoun 'them' (hum) when presenting the named things, if some of them are non-rational? The response is that among the named things were rational beings (angels, humans, jinn), so the Arabic convention of giving precedence to the rational over the non-rational applies. The author also clarifies that the presentation was of the named things, not the names, as indicated by the word 'these' (hāʾulāʾi). This leads to the conclusion that Adam's knowledge was of the properties and attributes of things, not necessarily their essences, but this does not detract from the epistemological value. The passage ends by refuting the view that the names refer only to divine or angelic names, asserting that the knowledge encompasses all human domains.

الإمكان المكتسب
الأسماء
المسميات
العرض

The Quran's Condemnation of Pathological Doubt
Author presents Quranic verses condemning doubt in creedal matters such as Tawhid and the Hereafter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between constructive and pathological doubt using Quranic criteria and identify the Quran's condemnation of doubt in three distinct scenarios.

Core Idea
The Quran condemns pathological doubt, not all questions.

In the previous lessons, we explored the human capacity for knowledge. Now we turn to the Quran's direct stance on doubt. The Quran does not condemn all questioning, but it severely condemns pathological doubt—doubt that stems from arrogance or rejection of truth. This lesson examines the Quranic verses that delineate this condemnation across different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three forms of condemnation
The author presents three forms: creedal separation, direct condemnation, and implicit condemnation. Find the verses corresponding to each form in the passage.
Creedal separation: Surah Yunus 10:104. Direct: Surah Ash-Shura 42:18, An-Naml 27:66, Ad-Dukhan 44:9, Sad 38:8, Al-Hajj 22:55. Implicit: Surah An-Nisa 4:157.
2
Analyze the first form: creedal separation
In Surah Yunus 10:104, the Prophet is commanded to declare a clear separation from the doubters. Why? Because their doubt touches Tawhid, the core of Islamic doctrine.
3
Analyze the second form: direct condemnation
Verses about the Hour (Day of Judgment) directly condemn those who dispute (yumaruna) or are in doubt about it. The condemnation is severe, calling it 'far error' and threatening punishment.
4
Analyze the third form: implicit condemnation via conjecture
Surah An-Nisa 4:157 condemns those who built knowledge on weak conjecture (zann) regarding Jesus' crucifixion. The Quran says they have no knowledge except assumption, and they did not kill him for certain.
5
Synthesize: What distinguishes pathological doubt?
The common thread: doubt that rejects clear truth, especially regarding Tawhid and the Hereafter, and doubt built on conjecture rather than certain knowledge. Constructive doubt seeking truth is not condemned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موقف القرآن من الشك
إذا عرفنا جميع ما تقدّم من إمكان ذاتي وآخر مكتسب لدى الإنسان يؤهلانه للحصول على المعرفة فماذا تراه سيكون الموقف المباشر للقرآن من مسألة الشك والمتشككين تشككاً مرضياً مطلقاً.
الواقع أن القرآن الكريم يقرّر ذمّه لذلك في صيغ متعددة تختلف باختلاف المواقف الشكيّة وأصحابها، فنجده تارة يفاصل الشاكين مفاصلة عقدية لاتصال شكهم بقضايا جوهرية من جنس التوحيد والمذهبية الإسلامية الكلية وقل يا أَيُّها النّاسُ إِن كُنتُمْ فِي شَكٍّ مِنْ دِينِي فَلا أَعْبُدُ الَّذِينَ تَعْبُدُونَ مِنْ دُونِ اللهِ وَلَكِنْ أَعْبُدُ اللهَ الَّذِي يَتَوَفَّاكُمْ وَأُمِرْتُ أَنْ أَكُونَ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ [يونس: ١٠٤/١٠]. وتارة يتوجه القرآن بذمه إلى المتشككين مباشرة، وذلك حينما يبلغ شكهم إلى اليوم الآخر -مثلاً-:
أَلَا إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُمَارُونَ فِي السَّاعَةِ لَفِي ضَلالٍ بَعِيدٍ [الشورى: ١٨/٤٢]. بَلْ هُمْ فِي شَكٍّ مِنْها بَلْ هُمْ مِنْهَا عَمُونَ [النمل: ٦٦/٢٧].
وَبَلْ هُمْ فِي شَكٍّ يَلْعَبُونَ [الدخان: ٩/٤٤]. أو يمس القرآن فيتوعدهم: أُنْزِلَ عَلَيْهِ الذِّكْرُ مِنْ بَيْنِنَا بَلْ هُمْ فِي شَكٍّ مِنْ ذِكْرِي بَلْ لَمّا يَذُوقُوا عَذَاب [ص: ٨/٣٨]
وَلا يَزالُ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا فِي مِرْيَةٍ مِنْهُ حَتَّى تَأْتِيَهُمُ السَّاعَةُ بَغْتَةً أَوْ يَأْتِيَهُمْ عَذَابُ يَوْمٍ عَقِيمٍ [الحج: ٥٥/٢٢]
ويعبر القرآن الكريم عن ذمّه الضمني للشك حين يبني المرء معارفه على أساس من الظن المرجوح فيذم المتشككين في نجاة عيسى من القتل بناء على الظن الوهمي: وَإِنَّ الَّذِينَ اخْتَلَفُوا فِيهِ لَفِي شَكٍّ مِنْهُ مَا لَهُمْ بِهِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِلَّا اتِّبَاعَ الظَّنِّ وَمَا قَتَلُوهُ يَقِينا [النساء: ١٥٧/٤].
The Quran's Position on Doubt. If we know all that has preceded of innate and acquired potential in man that qualifies him to obtain knowledge, then what do you think will be the direct position of the Quran regarding the issue of doubt and the doubters who doubt pathologically and absolutely? The reality is that the Noble Quran decrees condemnation of that in multiple forms that differ according to the skeptical positions and their holders. So we find it sometimes separates from the doubters a creedal separation due to the connection of their doubt to essential issues of the kind of Tawhid and the total Islamic doctrinal system, and say: 'Say: O people, if you are in doubt about my religion, then I do not worship those whom you worship besides Allah, but I worship Allah who takes your souls, and I am commanded to be of the believers' [Yunus 10:104]. And sometimes the Quran directs its condemnation directly to the skeptics, that is when their doubt reaches the Last Day, for example: 'Verily, those who dispute concerning the Hour are in far error' [Ash-Shura 42:18]. 'Nay, they are in doubt about it; nay, they are blind to it' [An-Naml 27:66]. 'And they are in doubt, playing' [Ad-Dukhan 44:9]. Or the Quran touches them and threatens them: 'Is the Reminder sent down to him from among us? Nay, they are in doubt about My Reminder; nay, they have not yet tasted My punishment' [Sad 38:8]. 'And those who disbelieve will not cease to be in doubt about it until the Hour comes upon them suddenly or there comes to them the punishment of a barren day' [Al-Hajj 22:55]. And the Noble Quran expresses its implicit condemnation of doubt when one builds his knowledge on the basis of weak conjecture, thus condemning those who doubted the salvation of Jesus from killing based on illusory conjecture: 'And indeed, those who differed over it are in doubt about it. They have no knowledge of it except the following of assumption, and they did not kill him, for certain' [An-Nisa 4:157].
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage examines the Quran's direct stance on pathological doubt. The author first recalls the previous discussion of innate and acquired potential for knowledge, then asks what the Quran's direct position is on doubt and those who doubt pathologically. He states that the Quran decrees condemnation of such doubt in various forms depending on the skeptical attitudes and people. The first form is a creedal separation: when doubt touches essential issues like Tawhid and Islamic doctrine, the Quran commands the Prophet to declare a clear separation, as in Surah Yunus 10:104, where he says he does not worship what they worship but worships Allah. The second form is direct condemnation, especially when doubt concerns the Day of Judgment. The passage cites multiple verses: Surah Ash-Shura 42:18 says those who dispute the Hour are in far error; Surah An-Naml 27:66 says they are in doubt and blind; Surah Ad-Dukhan 44:9 says they play in doubt; and Surah Sad 38:8 threatens those who doubt the revelation with punishment. Surah Al-Hajj 22:55 states that disbelievers remain in doubt until the Hour comes or punishment arrives. The third form is implicit condemnation when one builds knowledge on weak conjecture (zan). The example is the Jews' doubt about the killing of Jesus (peace be upon him) — they followed conjecture and did not kill him for certain, as stated in Surah An-Nisa 4:157. Thus, the Quran condemns all forms of doubt that lead to misguidance, whether about fundamental beliefs or about historical facts, especially when based on mere conjecture.

الشك
المراء
الظن

Key Takeaway
The Quran condemns pathological doubt in three forms: creedal separation, direct condemnation, and implicit condemnation of conjecture, all targeting doubt that rejects truth.

The Quran's Condemnation of Doubt and Conjecture
Author presents Quranic condemnations of doubt and conjecture as epistemological failings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of Quranic censure for doubt and explain why conjecture (zann) is invalid in matters of essential truth.

Core Idea
The Quran treats pathological doubt and unfounded conjecture as epistemological failures that lead to ruin.

In this passage, the author examines the Quran's stance on doubt and conjecture. After establishing that humans have the capacity for knowledge, the Quran condemns those who doubt or follow mere assumptions. The focus is on the epistemological contrast between certainty (yaqin) and the unreliable nature of conjecture (zann). We will explore the three forms of Quranic censure and the ultimate consequence of building knowledge on weak conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of Quranic censure
Read the passage and note how the Quran responds to doubt in different contexts: (1) direct doctrinal separation when doubt touches creed (Tawheed), (2) direct censure when doubt concerns the Hereafter or revelation, and (3) implicit censure when knowledge is built on weak conjecture.
Look for verses like Yunus 10:104 for the first category, and An-Nisa 4:157 for the third.
2
Distinguish between shakk (doubt) and zann (conjecture)
The passage uses both terms. Shakk is pathological doubt that leads to disbelief, while zann is assumption based on weak evidence, often illusory. Note that in usul al-fiqh zann can be acceptable, but in creedal matters it is condemned.
Contrast with yakin (certainty), which is the epistemological goal.
3
Understand the consequence of relying on conjecture
The Quran states that conjecture 'does not avail against the truth at all' (An-Najm 53:28). Any judgment based on illusion is disregarded and leads to loss, especially when it misrepresents Allah.
See also Al-Jathiya 45:24 and Fussilat 41:22-23.
4
Relate the Quran's stance to Islamic epistemology
The passage emphasizes that doubt and conjecture are not acceptable foundations for knowledge, especially in essential matters like Tawheed and the Hereafter. Certainty (yakin) is required, and the Quran warns against following assumptions without evidence.
This principle is vital in 'Aqida and Usul al-Fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الرابع الفصل الأول - المعرفة بين الشك واليقين
موقف القرآن من الشك

إذا عرفنا جميع ما تقدّم من إمكان ذاتي وآخر مكتسب لدى الإنسان يؤهلانه للحصول على المعرفة فماذا تراه سيكون الموقف المباشر للقرآن من مسألة الشك والمتشككين تشككاً مرضياً مطلقاً.
الواقع أن القرآن الكريم يقرّر ذمّه لذلك في صيغ متعددة تختلف باختلاف المواقف الشكيّة وأصحابها، فنجده تارة يفاصل الشاكين مفاصلة عقدية لاتصال شكهم بقضايا جوهرية من جنس التوحيد والمذهبية الإسلامية الكلية وقل يا أَيُّها النّاسُ إِن كُنتُمْ فِي شَكٍّ مِنْ دِينِي فَلا أَعْبُدُ الَّذِينَ تَعْبُدُونَ مِنْ دُونِ اللهِ وَلَكِنْ أَعْبُدُ اللهَ الَّذِي يَتَوَفَّاكُمْ وَأُمِرْتُ أَنْ أَكُونَ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ﴾ [يونس: ١٠٤/١٠]. وتارة يتوجه القرآن بذمه إلى المتشككين مباشرة، وذلك حينما يبلغ شكهم إلى اليوم الآخر -مثلاً-:
﴿أَلَا إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُمَارُونَ فِي السَّاعَةِ لَفِي ضَلالٍ بَعِيدٍ [الشورى: ١٨/٤٢]. بَلْ هُمْ فِي شَكٍّ مِنْها بَلْ هُمْ مِنْهَا عَمُونَ ﴾ [النمل: ٦٦/٢٧).
﴿وَبَلْ هُمْ فِي شَكٍّ يَلْعَبُونَ ﴾ [الدخان: ٩/٤٤]. أو يمس القرآن فيتوعدهم: أُنْزِلَ عَلَيْهِ الذِّكْرُ مِنْ بَيْنا بَلْ هُمْ فِي شَكٍّ مِنْ ذِكْرِي بَلْ لَمّا يَذُوقُوا عَذَاب (ص: ۸/۳۸]
﴿وَلا يَزالُ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا فِي مِرْيَةٍ مِنْهُ حَتَّى تَأْتِيَهُمُ السَّاعَةُ بَغْتَةً أَوْ يَأْتِيَهُمْ
عَذَابُ يَوْمٍ عَقِيمٍ﴾ [الحج: ٥٥/٢٢]
ويعبر القرآن الكريم عن ذمّه الضمني للشك حين يبني المرء معارفه على أساس من الظن المرجوح فيذم المتشككين في نجاة عيسـى مـن القتـل بنـاء عـلـى الظن الوهمي: ﴿وَإِنَّ الَّذِينَ اخْتَلَفُوا فِيهِ لَفِي شَكٍّ مِنْهُ مَا لَهُمْ بِهِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِلَّا الْباعَ الظَّنِّ وَما قَتَلُوهُ يَقِينا ) [النساء: ١٥٧/٤].
ويعد الظن على هذا النحو شأناً خاصاً بالكافرين الذين يستحقون الوعيد لأنهم ينكرون الهدف من خلق السماء والأرض:
شيئاً:
ذَلِكَ ظَنُّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا فَوَيْلٌ لِلَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا مِنَ النَّارِ﴾ [ص: ۲۷/۳۸]. ولذلك فإن تلك الأحكام لا قيمة لها بوصفها ظنوناً وهمية لا تغني من الحق
وإنَّ الَّذِينَ لا يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالآخِرَةِ لَيَسَمُونَ الْمَلائِكَةَ تَسْمِيَةَ الْأُنْثَى وَمَا لَهُمْ بِهِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِن يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا الظَّنَّ وَإِنَّ الظَّنَّ لا يُغْنِي مِنَ الْحَقِّ شَيْئًا [النجم: ٢٧/٥٣-٢٨].
وقيام تلك الأحكام الظنية مبني على الظن والخرص (۱):
﴿وَما يَتَّبِعُ الَّذِينَ يَدْعُونَ مِنْ دُونِ اللَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ إِنْ يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا الظَّنَّ وَإِنْ هُمْ إِلا يَحْرُصُونَ ﴾ [يونس: ٦٦/١٠]
وأيما حكم قام على الظن الوهمي فهو مهدر غير معتبر :
وَقالُوا ما هِيَ إِلا حَياتُنا الدُّنْيا نَمُوتُ وَنَحْيا وَمَا يُهْلِكُنا إِلا الدَّهْرُ وَما لَهُمْ بِذَلِكَ مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا يَظُنُّونَ ﴾ [الجانية: ٢٤/٤٥].
ولا غرو في ذلك فإن عاقبة هذا الظن أن يردي صاحبه فيحيق بـه الخسران خاصة حين لا يسلم من ظنه السيئ حتى اعتقاده بالله وقدرته - سبحانه - : وَما كُنتُمْ تَسْتَتِرُونَ أَن يَشْهَدَ عَلَيْكُمْ سَمْعُكُمْ وَلَا أَبْصَارُكُمْ وَلَا جُلُودُكُمْ وَلَكِنْ ظَنَنتُمْ أَنَّ اللهَ لا يَعْلَمُ كَثِيراً مِمَّا تَعْمَلُونَ، وَذَلِكُمْ ظَنُّكُمُ
الَّذِي ظَنَنتُمْ بِرَبِّكُمْ أَرْداكُمْ فَأَصْبَحْتُمْ مِنَ الْخاسِرِينَ﴾ [فصلت: ٢٢/٤١-٢٣].
(1) الخرص من معانيه الكذب قال تعالى: ﴿ قُتِلَ الْخَرَاصُونَ ﴾ [الذاريات: ١٠/٥١] أي الكاذبين، انظر: المعجم الوسيط لإبراهيم أنيس ورفاقه، ۲۲۷/۱
Chapter Four, Section One - Knowledge between Doubt and Certainty
The Quran's Stance on Doubt

When we have understood all that has preceded regarding the intrinsic and acquired potential in man that qualifies him to obtain knowledge, then what do you think will be the direct stance of the Quran on the issue of doubt and those who doubt in an absolutely pathological manner?
The fact is that the Noble Quran establishes its censure of that in multiple forms that differ according to the doubting stances and their proponents. Sometimes we find it separating from the doubters a doctrinal separation due to the connection of their doubt to essential issues of the kind of monotheism and the total Islamic creed, saying: 'Say, O people, if you are in doubt about my religion, then I do not worship those you worship besides Allah, but I worship Allah who will take your souls, and I have been commanded to be among the believers' [Yunus 10:104]. And sometimes the Quran directs its censure directly to the doubters, for example when their doubt reaches the Last Day: ['No indeed! Those who dispute concerning the Hour are in distant error' [Ash-Shura 42:18]. 'Rather, they are in doubt about it; rather, they are blind to it' [An-Naml 27:66]. And 'Rather, they are in doubt, playing' [Ad-Dukhan 44:9]. Or the Quran threatens them: 'The Remembrance was sent down to him. Rather, they are in doubt about My Remembrance; rather, they have not yet tasted My punishment' [Sad 38:8]. 'And those who disbelieve will not cease to be in doubt about it until the Hour comes upon them suddenly or there comes to them the punishment of a barren day' [Al-Hajj 22:55].
And the Noble Quran expresses its implicit censure of doubt when a person builds his knowledge on the basis of weak conjecture, censuring those who doubted Jesus's salvation from killing based on illusory conjecture: 'And indeed, those who differed over it are in doubt about it. They have no knowledge of it except the following of assumption. And they did not kill him, for certain' [An-Nisa 4:157].
And conjecture in this manner is considered a special characteristic of the disbelievers who deserve the threat because they deny the purpose of creating the heavens and the earth: 'That is the assumption of those who disbelieved, so woe to those who disbelieved from the Fire' [Sad 38:27]. Therefore, those judgments have no value as illusory conjectures that do not avail against the truth: 'And indeed, those who do not believe in the Hereafter name the angels with female names. And they have no knowledge of it. They follow nothing but assumption. And indeed, assumption does not avail against the truth at all' [An-Najm 53:27-28].
And the establishment of those conjectural judgments is based on assumption and falsehood (1): 'And those who invoke partners besides Allah do not follow [anything]. They follow nothing but assumption. And they are only lying' [Yunus 10:66].
And any judgment based on illusory conjecture is disregarded and not considered: 'And they say, 'There is nothing but our worldly life; we die and we live, and nothing destroys us except time.' And they have no knowledge of that; they are only assuming' [Al-Jathiya 45:24].
No wonder, for the consequence of this conjecture is that it ruins its possessor, bringing loss upon him, especially when his evil conjecture does not spare even his belief in Allah and His power - Glorified be He: 'And you did not conceal yourselves so that your hearing and your sight and your skins would testify against you, but you assumed that Allah does not know much of what you do. And that was your assumption which you assumed about your Lord; it has ruined you, and you have become among the losers' [Fussilat 41:22-23].
(1) Among the meanings of 'kharas' is lying. Allah says: 'Cursed be the liars' [Adh-Dhariyat 51:10], i.e., the liars. See: Al-Mu'jam al-Wasit by Ibrahim Anis and his colleagues, 1/227.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This is an epistemological passage presenting the Quran's stance on doubt and conjecture. The author begins by recalling the human capacity for knowledge (both intrinsic and acquired), then asks what the Quran's direct position is regarding pathological doubt. The answer: the Quran condemns doubt in various forms. The author categorizes three types of Quranic censure. First, direct doctrinal separation when doubt touches essential creedal matters like Tawheed, citing verse Yunus 10:104. Second, direct censure when doubt concerns the Hereafter, citing verses from Ash-Shura, An-Naml, and Ad-Dukhan, and threatening those who doubt the Quran itself (Sad 38:8) and those who persist in doubt until the Hour (Al-Hajj 22:55). Third, implicit censure when knowledge is built on weak conjecture, exemplified by those who doubted Jesus's crucifixion based on mere assumption (An-Nisa 4:157). The author then argues that such conjecture is characteristic of disbelievers who deny creation's purpose (Sad 38:27) and that it has no value against the truth (An-Najm 53:27-28). Furthermore, judgments based on illusory conjecture are invalid (Al-Jathiya 45:24) and ultimately lead to ruin, especially when misapplied to Allah (Fussilat 41:22-23). The footnote clarifies that 'kharas' (خرص) means lying, citing Al-Mu'jam al-Wasit.

Key Terms
شك
ظن
يقين
خرص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one consequence of building knowledge on illusory conjecture (zann wahmi)?
A) It leads to temporary uncertainty that can later be corrected.
B) It is completely invalid and does not avail against the truth.
C) It is acceptable if it concerns worldly matters only.
D) It is a valid method when certain knowledge is unavailable.

Key Takeaway
The Quran condemns doubt and conjecture as unreliable foundations for knowledge, emphasizing that only certainty (yakin) is acceptable, especially in matters of creed and the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Read the verse An-Najm 53:28 and classify which of the three types of Quranic censure (doctrinal separation, direct censure, or implicit censure) it represents. Explain your reasoning based on the passage.
?
Task 2: Identify a modern example of conjecture that the Quran's critique would apply to (e.g., speculation about the unseen or about Allah's nature). Explain why it falls under the Quran's condemnation.

Al-Ghazali's Skeptical Argument Against Sense Perception
Al-Ghazali's skeptical argument against sense perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Ghazali's argument against the reliability of sense perception using his shadow and planet examples.

Core Idea
If reason can overrule sense, a higher judge might overrule reason.

Al-Ghazali begins his epistemological journey by challenging the most basic source of knowledge: sense perception. He uses vivid examples from ordinary experience to show how the senses can be mistaken, and how reason corrects them. This sets the stage for a deeper inquiry into the foundations of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Examine the shadow example
Identify the sense perception: the shadow appears stationary. Then note how reason, through observation over time, reveals its gradual motion. What conflict does this create?
2
Examine the planet example
The sense perceives the planet as small (size of a dinar). Geometry (a product of reason) proves it is larger than Earth. Again, reason overrules sense.
3
Generalize the conflict
Al-Ghazali states that the 'judge of sense' is consistently contradicted by the 'judge of reason'. This leads him to doubt the trustworthiness of sense perception entirely.
4
Extend the hierarchy
Now he speculates: just as reason overruled sense, perhaps there is an even higher judge (divine light) that could overrule reason. The non-manifestation of that judge does not prove its impossibility.
This is the core of his skeptical method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضاً، وأخذت تتسع للشك فيها، وتقول من أين الثقة بالمحسوسات وأقواهـا حاسة البصر، وهي تنظر إلى الظل فتراه واقفا غير متحرك، وتحكم بنفي الحركة، ثم بالتجربة والمشاهدة بعد ساعة تعرف أنه يتحرّك، وأنه لم يتحرك بغتة ودفعة، بل على التدرج ذرّة ذرّة حتى لم تكن له حالة وقوف، وتنظر إلى الكوكب فتراه صغيراً في مقدار دينار، ثم الأدلة الهندسية تدلّ على أنها أكبر من الأرض في المقدار هذا وأمثاله في المحسوسات يحكم منها حاكم الحس، ويكذبه حاكم العقل تجربة تكذيباً لا سبيل إلى مدافعته. فقلت قـد بطلت الثقة بالمحسوسات، وقد كنت واثقاً بي، فجاء حاكم العقل فكذبني، ولولا حاكم العقل لكنت تستمر على تصديقي، فلعل وراء إدراك العقل حاكماً آخر، إذا تجلى كذب العقل في حكمه كما تجلى حاكم العقل، فكذب الحس في حكمه، وعدم تحلّي ذلك الإدراك لا يدل على استحالته، فتوقفت النفس في جواب ذلك قليلا، وأيدت إشكالها بالمنام... فأعضل هذا الداء، ودام قريباً من شهرين، أنا فيهما على مذهب السفسطة بحكم النطق والمقال حتى شفا الله تعالى عن ذلك المرض، وعادت النفس إلى الصحة والاعتدال ورجعت الضروريات مقبولة، موثوقاً بها على أمن ويقين ولم يكن ذلك بنظم دليل وترتيب كلام بل بنور قذفه الله تعالى في الصدر، وذلك النور هو مفتاح أكثر المعارف. إن كلام الغزالي إذن يجيب عن التساؤلين السابقين حول تجاوز العقل إلى الحدس أو الإلهام وهو النور الذي يقذفه الله في القلب؟ ثم كيف يكون العقل دليلاً على وجود الله ونحن نشكك فيه أداة لمعرفة؟ فجواب التساؤل الأول: بعيداً عن المنهج التبريري - يفيد أن المقصود ليس الاستغناء عن العقل كلية وإنما المراد أن الاقتصار عليه وحده غير كاف، فثمة مصادر أخرى كالحدس والإلهام - بقطع النظر عن منزلتها الموضوعية - إلا أن هذا يتسق ومنهج الغزالي الفيلسوف المتصوف، والذي يقوم منهجه على الكشف والذوق وقد عدَّ علم المكاشفة (علم الباطن) أحد قسمي علم طريق الآخرة، وعده كذلك علم الصديقين والمقربين، وبه تتضح حقائق لم تكن معلومة من قبل. أما جواب التساؤل الثاني: فإن الشك في العقل حالة مؤقتة ما تلبث أن تتحول إلى حالة يقين بعد اختبارها وفق منهج الشك المعروف عند الغزالي. ولكن الملفت بحق في موضوع الغزالي ومنهجه هو عدم الاقتناع بأحكام العقل والحس، وإضافة الإلهام مصدراً جديداً مهيمناً لكأنما له كلمة الفصل في نهاية المطاف !!! أو هكذا يوحي كلام الغزالي.
Also, doubt expanded in them, and it said: from where is trust in sense-perceptions, and the strongest of them is the sense of sight? It looks at the shadow and sees it stationary, not moving, and judges the negation of motion, then through experience and observation after an hour it knows that it moves, and that it did not move suddenly and all at once, but gradually atom by atom so that there was no state of rest for it. And it looks at the planet and sees it small, about the size of a dinar, then geometrical proofs indicate that it is larger than the earth in size. This and similar things among sense-perceptions: the judge of sense judges concerning them, and the judge of reason denies them through experience, a denial that cannot be repelled. So I said: trust in sense-perceptions has been nullified, and I had been confident in them. Then the judge of reason came and denied me, and if not for the judge of reason, you would continue to believe me. Perhaps beyond the perception of reason there is another judge; if it were to appear, it would deny reason in its judgment just as the judge of reason appeared and denied sense in its judgment. And the non-manifestation of that perception does not imply its impossibility. So the soul paused a little in response to that, and I strengthened its problem with dreams... So this malady became severe, and it lasted for nearly two months, during which I was upon the school of sophistry in terms of speech and discourse, until Allah Almighty cured me of that illness, and the soul returned to health and balance, and the necessary truths became accepted, trustworthy, with security and certainty. And that was not through the arrangement of a proof and ordering of speech, but through a light that Allah Almighty cast into the chest, and that light is the key to most cognitions. So the speech of al-Ghazali thus answers the two previous questions about surpassing reason to intuition (hads) or inspiration (ilham), which is the light that Allah casts into the heart? Then how can reason be a proof for the existence of Allah while we are doubting it as an instrument of knowledge? The answer to the first question: far from the justificatory method - it indicates that the intention is not to dispense with reason entirely, rather the intention is that relying on it alone is insufficient. There are other sources such as intuition and inspiration - regardless of their objective status - except that this is consistent with the method of al-Ghazali the philosopher-sufi, whose method is based on unveiling (kashf) and taste (dhawq). He counted the science of unveiling (the inner science) as one of the two divisions of the science of the path to the hereafter, and also counted it as the science of the veracious and the near ones, and through it realities become clear that were not known before. As for the answer to the second question: skepticism about reason is a temporary state that soon turns into a state of certainty after testing it according to the method of skepticism known to al-Ghazali. But what is truly striking in al-Ghazali's subject and method is his lack of conviction in the judgments of reason and sense, and his addition of inspiration as a new overriding source, as if it has the final word in the end!!! Or so suggests the speech of al-Ghazali.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's account of his epistemological crisis and his eventual resolution through divine illumination. It begins by describing his doubt regarding sense perception: the senses judge the shadow as stationary, but reason later reveals it moves; the eye sees a planet as small, but geometry proves it large. Reason thus contradicts sense, causing al-Ghazali to lose trust in sense-perceptions. He then considers that just as a higher judge (reason) overturned the judgment of sense, perhaps there is an even higher judge that could overturn reason. This leads to a state of extreme skepticism (sophistry) lasting nearly two months. He was cured not by logical argument but by a divine light cast into his chest, which restored certainty in necessary truths. The author of the text, a commentator on al-Ghazali, then poses two questions: (1) How can one go beyond reason to intuition/inspiration? (2) How can reason be proof for God if we doubt it? The commentator answers: for the first, al-Ghazali does not reject reason entirely but holds it insufficient alone; he admits other sources like intuition/inspiration, consistent with his Sufi epistemology based on unveiling and taste. For the second, skepticism about reason is temporary and transforms into certainty through a specific method of doubt. The commentator notes that al-Ghazali ultimately gives inspiration a decisive, overriding role, which is striking.

Key Terms
السفسطة
النور
الحدس
الإلهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between sense and reason in al-Ghazali's examples?
A) Sense and reason always agree
B) Reason corrects the false judgments of sense
C) Sense is more reliable than reason
D) Reason and sense are both equally unreliable

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali uses sense-reason conflicts to initiate a skeptical regress, setting the stage for a higher source of certainty: divine light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Formulate a similar skeptical argument against a different sense perception (e.g., hearing) using the pattern: sense judges X, reason shows not-X. Then identify the 'judge of reason' that intervenes.

Divine Light as the Foundation of Certainty
Al-Ghazali's recovery through divine illumination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Ghazali ultimately relies on divine light rather than rational proof to overcome his skepticism.

Core Idea
Certainty is restored by God's light, not by logical demonstration.

After enduring extreme skepticism for two months, al-Ghazali describes his recovery as a divine intervention. He explicitly states that it was not through the arrangement of proofs or logical speech, but through a light that God cast into his chest. This lesson unpacks the nature and role of that 'nur' in his epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the crisis duration and severity
Note that al-Ghazali says the malady lasted nearly two months, during which he was in a state of sophistry (safsata). This emphasizes the depth of his doubt.
He was not just playing with ideas; it was a genuine personal crisis.
2
Find the cure described
He says: 'until Allah Almighty cured me of that illness, and the soul returned to health and balance, and the necessary truths became accepted, trustworthy, with security and certainty.'
3
Note the method of cure
Crucially, the cure was 'not through the arrangement of a proof and ordering of speech, but through a light that Allah Almighty cast into the chest.'
This is the divine light (al-nur).
4
Interpret the light's function
Al-Ghazali calls this light 'the key to most cognitions.' It restores trust in necessary truths (al-daruriyyat) without rational argument.
This is a direct epistemological foundation that bypasses discursive reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's account of his epistemological crisis and his eventual resolution through divine illumination. It begins by describing his doubt regarding sense perception: the senses judge the shadow as stationary, but reason later reveals it moves; the eye sees a planet as small, but geometry proves it large. Reason thus contradicts sense, causing al-Ghazali to lose trust in sense-perceptions. He then considers that just as a higher judge (reason) overturned the judgment of sense, perhaps there is an even higher judge that could overturn reason. This leads to a state of extreme skepticism (sophistry) lasting nearly two months. He was cured not by logical argument but by a divine light cast into his chest, which restored certainty in necessary truths. The author of the text, a commentator on al-Ghazali, then poses two questions: (1) How can one go beyond reason to intuition/inspiration? (2) How can reason be proof for God if we doubt it? The commentator answers: for the first, al-Ghazali does not reject reason entirely but holds it insufficient alone; he admits other sources like intuition/inspiration, consistent with his Sufi epistemology based on unveiling and taste. For the second, skepticism about reason is temporary and transforms into certainty through a specific method of doubt. The commentator notes that al-Ghazali ultimately gives inspiration a decisive, overriding role, which is striking.

The Relationship Between Reason and Inspiration in Ghazali's Epistemology
Reconciling reason and inspiration in Ghazali's thought

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze al-Ghazali's view on the roles of reason and inspiration (ilham) in the acquisition of knowledge, as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Reason is not rejected but is insufficient without inspiration.

Al-Ghazali's account of his recovery through divine light raises important questions: does he reject reason altogether? Or does he assign reason a limited but necessary role? The commentator directly addresses these questions, and the passage provides clues to Ghazali's nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two questions the commentator raises
The commentator asks: (1) How can one go beyond reason to intuition/inspiration? (2) How can reason be a proof for God if we doubt it?
2
Extract the commentator's answer to the first question
The intention is not to dispense with reason entirely, but to say that relying on reason alone is insufficient. Other sources like intuition (hads) and inspiration (ilham) are available.
This is consistent with Ghazali's Sufi method of unveiling (kashf) and taste (dhawq).
3
Extract the commentator's answer to the second question
The doubt about reason is temporary; it transforms into certainty after testing it through a specific method of doubt.
Ghazali's skepticism is methodological, not permanent.
4
Synthesize Ghazali's overall position
The commentator notes that Ghazali ultimately gives inspiration a decisive, overriding role. This suggests that while reason is useful, it is subordinate to divine illumination.
This dual commitment to reason and inspiration is a hallmark of Ghazali's integrative approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضاً، وأخذت تتسع للشك فيها، وتقول من أين الثقة بالمحسوسات وأقواهـا حاسة البصر، وهي تنظر إلى الظل فتراه واقفا غير متحرك، وتحكم بنفي الحركة، ثم بالتجربة والمشاهدة بعد ساعة تعرف أنه يتحرّك، وأنه لم يتحرك بغتة ودفعة، بل على التدرج ذرّة ذرّة حتى لم تكن له حالة وقوف، وتنظر إلى الكوكب فتراه صغيراً في مقدار دينار، ثم الأدلة الهندسية تدلّ على أنها أكبر من الأرض في المقدار هذا وأمثاله في المحسوسات يحكم منها حاكم الحس، ويكذبه حاكم العقل تجربة تكذيباً لا سبيل إلى مدافعته. فقلت قـد بطلت الثقة بالمحسوسات، وقد كنت واثقاً بي، فجاء حاكم العقل فكذبني، ولولا حاكم العقل لكنت تستمر على تصديقي، فلعل وراء إدراك العقل حاكماً آخر، إذا تجلى كذب العقل في حكمه كما تجلى حاكم العقل، فكذب الحس في حكمه، وعدم تحلّي ذلك الإدراك لا يدل على استحالته، فتوقفت النفس في جواب ذلك قليلا، وأيدت إشكالها بالمنام... فأعضل هذا الداء، ودام قريباً من شهرين، أنا فيهما على مذهب السفسطة بحكم النطق والمقال حتى شفا الله تعالى عن ذلك المرض، وعادت النفس إلى الصحة والاعتدال ورجعت الضروريات مقبولة، موثوقاً بها على أمن ويقين ولم يكن ذلك بنظم دليل وترتيب كلام بل بنور قذفه الله تعالى في الصدر، وذلك النور هو مفتاح أكثر المعارف. إن كلام الغزالي إذن يجيب عن التساؤلين السابقين حول تجاوز العقل إلى الحدس أو الإلهام وهو النور الذي يقذفه الله في القلب؟ ثم كيف يكون العقل دليلاً على وجود الله ونحن نشكك فيه أداة لمعرفة؟ فجواب التساؤل الأول: بعيداً عن المنهج التبريري - يفيد أن المقصود ليس الاستغناء عن العقل كلية وإنما المراد أن الاقتصار عليه وحده غير كاف، فثمة مصادر أخرى كالحدس والإلهام - بقطع النظر عن منزلتها الموضوعية - إلا أن هذا يتسق ومنهج الغزالي الفيلسوف المتصوف، والذي يقوم منهجه على الكشف والذوق وقد عدَّ علم المكاشفة (علم الباطن) أحد قسمي علم طريق الآخرة، وعده كذلك علم الصديقين والمقربين، وبه تتضح حقائق لم تكن معلومة من قبل. أما جواب التساؤل الثاني: فإن الشك في العقل حالة مؤقتة ما تلبث أن تتحول إلى حالة يقين بعد اختبارها وفق منهج الشك المعروف عند الغزالي. ولكن الملفت بحق في موضوع الغزالي ومنهجه هو عدم الاقتناع بأحكام العقل والحس، وإضافة الإلهام مصدراً جديداً مهيمناً لكأنما له كلمة الفصل في نهاية المطاف !!! أو هكذا يوحي كلام الغزالي.
Also, doubt expanded in them, and it said: from where is trust in sense-perceptions, and the strongest of them is the sense of sight? It looks at the shadow and sees it stationary, not moving, and judges the negation of motion, then through experience and observation after an hour it knows that it moves, and that it did not move suddenly and all at once, but gradually atom by atom so that there was no state of rest for it. And it looks at the planet and sees it small, about the size of a dinar, then geometrical proofs indicate that it is larger than the earth in size. This and similar things among sense-perceptions: the judge of sense judges concerning them, and the judge of reason denies them through experience, a denial that cannot be repelled. So I said: trust in sense-perceptions has been nullified, and I had been confident in them. Then the judge of reason came and denied me, and if not for the judge of reason, you would continue to believe me. Perhaps beyond the perception of reason there is another judge; if it were to appear, it would deny reason in its judgment just as the judge of reason appeared and denied sense in its judgment. And the non-manifestation of that perception does not imply its impossibility. So the soul paused a little in response to that, and I strengthened its problem with dreams... So this malady became severe, and it lasted for nearly two months, during which I was upon the school of sophistry in terms of speech and discourse, until Allah Almighty cured me of that illness, and the soul returned to health and balance, and the necessary truths became accepted, trustworthy, with security and certainty. And that was not through the arrangement of a proof and ordering of speech, but through a light that Allah Almighty cast into the chest, and that light is the key to most cognitions. So the speech of al-Ghazali thus answers the two previous questions about surpassing reason to intuition (hads) or inspiration (ilham), which is the light that Allah casts into the heart? Then how can reason be a proof for the existence of Allah while we are doubting it as an instrument of knowledge? The answer to the first question: far from the justificatory method - it indicates that the intention is not to dispense with reason entirely, rather the intention is that relying on it alone is insufficient. There are other sources such as intuition and inspiration - regardless of their objective status - except that this is consistent with the method of al-Ghazali the philosopher-sufi, whose method is based on unveiling (kashf) and taste (dhawq). He counted the science of unveiling (the inner science) as one of the two divisions of the science of the path to the hereafter, and also counted it as the science of the veracious and the near ones, and through it realities become clear that were not known before. As for the answer to the second question: skepticism about reason is a temporary state that soon turns into a state of certainty after testing it according to the method of skepticism known to al-Ghazali. But what is truly striking in al-Ghazali's subject and method is his lack of conviction in the judgments of reason and sense, and his addition of inspiration as a new overriding source, as if it has the final word in the end!!! Or so suggests the speech of al-Ghazali.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's account of his epistemological crisis and his eventual resolution through divine illumination. It begins by describing his doubt regarding sense perception: the senses judge the shadow as stationary, but reason later reveals it moves; the eye sees a planet as small, but geometry proves it large. Reason thus contradicts sense, causing al-Ghazali to lose trust in sense-perceptions. He then considers that just as a higher judge (reason) overturned the judgment of sense, perhaps there is an even higher judge that could overturn reason. This leads to a state of extreme skepticism (sophistry) lasting nearly two months. He was cured not by logical argument but by a divine light cast into his chest, which restored certainty in necessary truths. The author of the text, a commentator on al-Ghazali, then poses two questions: (1) How can one go beyond reason to intuition/inspiration? (2) How can reason be proof for God if we doubt it? The commentator answers: for the first, al-Ghazali does not reject reason entirely but holds it insufficient alone; he admits other sources like intuition/inspiration, consistent with his Sufi epistemology based on unveiling and taste. For the second, skepticism about reason is temporary and transforms into certainty through a specific method of doubt. The commentator notes that al-Ghazali ultimately gives inspiration a decisive, overriding role, which is striking.

Key Terms
الحدس
الإلهام
النور
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is al-Ghazali's view on the relationship between reason and inspiration?
A) Reason is completely rejected in favor of inspiration
B) Reason is sufficient on its own
C) Reason is necessary but insufficient; inspiration is a complementary source
D) Reason and inspiration are contradictory

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's epistemology integrates reason and inspiration, with the latter playing a decisive role in achieving certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a brief jadal (debate) between a rationalist theologian who argues that reason is sufficient for certainty about God, and a Sufi who argues that inspiration is needed. Use the passage to support the Sufi position.
